                            Books by the Author

1. The Race of Grace—introductory book in which performance-related
    themes (such as perseverance) are considered essential for rewards in
    heaven. Entrance into heaven, in contrast, is regarded as free. A strong
    defense of unconditional security derived from believers being given to
    Jesus is a particular strength of this book.
2. Is Heaven a Gift or a Reward?—while some assert that heaven is a
    reward in some sense, this book maintains that regarding heaven itself
    as a gift is best.
3. Breaking the Rocking Horse—introductory book regarding the
    distinction between having eternal life as a gift versus obtaining it as a
    reward. An overview of the outer darkness and an exegetical treatment
    of the parable of the soils are this book’s primary strengths.
4. Mere Christianity and Moral Christianity—a more comprehensive
    discussion regarding perseverance (and other performance-related
    themes) as being needed for rewards in heaven rather than for entrance
    into heaven. This book provides both introductory material and
    advanced treatments.
5. Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep—a rejection of the notion that
    profession of salvation is proven genuine by the way a person lives. This
    book focuses on the parable of the sheep and the goats and shows the
    inadequacy of conditional security in interpreting this parable.
6. Sheep and Goats—an advanced discussion of the parable of the sheep
    and goats that evaluates four interpretations that are compatible with
    unconditional security.
7. Sealed and Secure—a thorough defense of a popular argument for
    unconditional security that also defends the premise of unconditional
    promises.
8. Fallen from Grace but Not from Perfection—an advanced defense of
    unconditional security which affirms that believers can fall from grace
    but not fall from perfection.
9. Salvation—a study of how the verb save and the noun salvation are used
    in the NT. A comprehensive chart of NT usage is provided.
10. Believe—an aspectual defense of unconditional security that examines
    how the verb believe is used in the Gospel of John. A comprehensive
    chart of metaphors for believe in the Gospel of John is provided also.
11. Carnal Corinth—an affirmation of the categorical existence of carnal
    Christians within the Corinthian epistles.
12. The Outer Darkness—a magnum opus that ties together the author’s
    arguments for unconditional security and conditional rewards.

    See UnconditionalSecurity.org for the release status of these books.

                               Purpose
        Breaking the Rocking Horse summarized some of the most pertinent
key points from Mere Christianity and Moral Christianity and from The
Outer Darkness pertaining to the proveitist theory that fruit determines root.
Purportedly, the way a professing believer lives determines whether that
professing believer is a genuine believer who really has experienced
regeneration. The introductory summation provided in Breaking the Rocking
Horse applied that discussion in some detail to the parable of the soils. The
present book is as an intermediate discussion that focuses on the parable of
the sheep and the goats. The purpose herein is to refute the proveitist theory
as it relates to this parable. A more advanced discussion of the parable of the
sheep and goats will be provided in my forthcoming book: Sheep and Goats:
Four Misthological Interpretations.

                        Technical Terms
        Conditional security: the belief that perseverance is necessary to
enter heaven. (Both proveitism and forfeitism advocate conditional security.)
        Forfeitist: an advocate of conditional security who believes that those
who do not persevere forfeit (i.e., lose) the gift of eternal life.
        Performatist: someone who believes that God-enabled, good
performance on the believer’s part is necessary for entrance into heaven.
        Proveitist: an advocate of conditional security who believes that fruit
is necessary proof of salvation: If you do not have this fruit, then you were
never saved to start with. Perseverance is necessary to prove that you are
saved and to enter heaven. Thus, entrance into heaven is conditioned on the
fruit of perseverance.
        NOSAS: Not Once Saved Always Saved (i.e., conditional security).
Popularly, OSAS is not the same thing as unconditional security in that
many proveitists believe in OSAS yet insist that one must persevere in order
to reach heaven. This hybrid by the proveitist camp is not considered a valid
form of OSAS in the present book.
        NOSAS-N: the conviction held by those securitists who think that
believing OSAS is necessary in order for saving faith to occur.
        NOSAS-U: the opinion held by those securitists who think that
believing OSAS is unnecessary in order for saving faith to occur.
        OSAS: Once Saved Always Saved (i.e., unconditional security).
Technically, OSAS is not the same thing as unconditional security in that
many proveitists believe in OSAS and yet insist that one must persevere in
order to reach heaven. Thus, reaching heaven is conditioned by the proveitist
camp on perseverance. This proveitist hybrid is not a valid form of OSAS.
The only form of OSAS considered legitimate herein is the unconditional
affirmation of OSAS even if one does not persevere.
        Misthology: is derived from the Greek word for reward (misthos) and
is used for matters pertaining to rewards in heaven, in contradistinction to
matters relating to entrance into heaven.
        Misthologist: refers to an advocate of unconditional soteriological
security who has a sufficient degree of misthological competency so as to
recognize that the biblical teaching regarding the outer darkness pertains to
misthological themes so that salvation from the outer darkness depicts
conditional misthological security.
        Soteriology: is derived from the Greek word for salvation (soteria)
and is used in theological discussions in reference to salvation from hell.
        Securitist: an advocate of unconditional security. Consequently, those
in this camp believe that perseverance is not necessary to keep the free gift
of eternal life or to prove that one has the gift of eternal life.
        Unconditional security: the belief that entrance into heaven does not
require perseverance.
Page viii                                   Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep

   What does your theology say about you?
                            Introduction
        “Christians are bigots who think that they are better than everyone
else,” is a widespread criticism coming from the non-Christian quarter.
                 Illustration 1. Just Forgiven Bumper Sticker




       Bumper-sticker Christianity responds accordingly, “Christians aren’t
perfect, just forgiven.” By making such a counterclaim, Christians seek to
disavow any notions of superiority. They seek to be the light of the world, as
they are commanded by Christ, without being excessively judgmental of
outward appearances.
       Other professing Christians seek to discredit such bumper-sticker
theology as failing to do justice to what they perceive to be a clear and
necessary biblical link between faith and works. According to professing
Christians who promote this alternate perspective, “If you have saving faith,
you necessarily will express that faith in a life of good works.”
       Indeed, in the minds of those adopting this opposing outlook, the
good life of a professing Christian is thought to prove whether that person is
a genuine Christian. Those adopting the mentality expressed by this
counterclaim will be called proveitists in this book. From their perspective,
your good works prove whether or not you are genuinely a Christian.

The Narrow Gate
        A natural corollary of proveitism is that Christians, by and large, are
better than everyone else. After all, Christians are those who, by proveitistic
definition, stick to the straight-and-narrow way of life, more or less.
Everybody else is on the broad path that morally leads to destruction.
Proveitists believe themselves to be the morally superior sheep on the path
of moral performance laid out by Jesus as necessary to reach heaven:
Page 2                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
     13
        Enter by the narrow gate; for the gate is wide, and the way is
     broad that leads to destruction, and many are those who enter
     by it. 14 For the gate is small, and the way is narrow that leads
     to life, and few are those who find it. (Mt 7:13-14)

The Parable of the Sheep and Goats
          31
            But when the Son of Man comes in His glory, and all the
    angels with Him, then He will sit on His glorious throne. 32 And
    all the nations will be gathered before Him; and He will separate
    them from one another, as the shepherd separates the sheep from
    the goats; 33 and He will put the sheep on His right, and the goats
    on the left.
         34
            Then the King will say to those on His right, “Come, you
    who are blessed of My Father, inherit the kingdom prepared for
    you from the foundation of the world. 35 “For I was hungry, and
    you gave Me something to eat; I was thirsty, and you gave Me
    drink; I was a stranger, and you invited Me in; 36 naked, and you
    clothed Me; I was sick, and you visited Me; I was in prison, and
    you came to Me.”
         37
            Then the righteous will answer Him, saying, “Lord, when
    did we see You hungry, and feed You, or thirsty, and give You
    drink? 38 “And when did we see You a stranger, and invite You
    in, or naked, and clothe You? 39 “And when did we see You
    sick, or in prison, and come to You?”
         40
            And the King will answer and say to them, “Truly I say to
    you, to the extent that you did it to one of these brothers of Mine,
    even the least of them, you did it to Me.”
         41
            Then He will also say to those on His left, “Depart from
    Me, accursed ones, into the eternal fire which has been prepared
    for the devil and his angels; 42 for I was hungry, and you gave
    Me nothing to eat; I was thirsty, and you gave Me nothing to
    drink; 43 I was a stranger, and you did not invite Me in; naked,
    and you did not clothe Me; sick, and in prison, and you did not
    visit Me.”
         44
            Then they themselves also will answer, saying, “Lord,
    when did we see You hungry, or thirsty, or a stranger, or naked,
    or sick, or in prison, and did not take care of You?”
         45
            Then He will answer them, saying, “Truly I say to you, to
    the extent that you did not do it to one of the least of these, you
    did not do it to Me.”
         46
            And these will go away into eternal punishment, but the
    righteous into eternal life. (Mt 25:31-46)
Introduction                                                             Page 3
       In their self-assessment, proveitists are the heaven-bound sheep who
help the needy. Everyone else is an unloving, hell-bound goat. Such a
proveitist attitude can be traced back to their interpretation of Jesus’ parable
concerning the sheep and the goats. The proveitist interpretation of this
parable has a tendency toward a reductionist mentality that concludes:

           •   Good people go to heaven.
           •   Bad people go to hell.
           •   Christians are the good people who go to heaven.

       Logically, such proveitist theology has an inclination to an unspoken
disposition that generalizes that Christians are better than everyone else in
terms of observable behavior.
                  Illustration 2. Just Better Bumper Sticker




       If this proveitist perspective were reduced to its own bumper-sticker
theology, it would be: “Christians aren’t perfect, just better than non-
Christians!” Such an elitist claim would be the natural outworking of
proveitist theology: The way you live proves whether you are a genuine
believer. So if you are a genuine believer, you will live better than the
nonbelievers who merely profess to believe. In short, in terms of the way
they live, proveitists would conclude that they are true Christians (and thus
better than non-Christians) since their proveitistic theology regards
Christians as morally superior to non-Christians.
       Like the Pharisees of old, whom Jesus criticized, these proveitists
thank God that they are better than nonbelievers. Also like the Pharisees,
proveitists think that they are on their way to heaven, as is proven by the
pious outworking of their faith. In short, they thank God for their good
Page 4                                            Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
works because God had a hand in producing the works necessary to
authenticate their faith, making it possible for them to reach heaven.

Self Deception
       Is it any wonder, then, that many non-Christians charge Christians
with thinking that Christians are morally superior to everyone else? Truth be
told, the theology advocated by most professing Christians leads to the
conclusion that Christians think that they are better than everyone else.
Given the proveitist paradigm, proveitists have to be better than everyone
else, or they will not make it to heaven.* That is a tall order! To help
themselves measure up to that mandate, proveitists sometimes manifest a
proclivity to minimize the good done by non-Christians and to maximize the
good done by themselves. At the same time, they minimize the bad they
themselves do while maximizing the bad done by others. In other words,
proveitists have a tendency toward hypocrisy. They think they are better
than everyone else in terms of the way they live when, in fact, they often are
no better than many others in terms of how they treat others. They deceive
themselves into thinking that they are better when, in reality, they are just
forgiven—if that.

Beware of Proveitists
       Jesus warned His followers to beware of the religious hypocrites of
His day (Mk 12:38-40; Lk 12:1). This warning still needs to be sounded
today. To His antagonists Jesus responded, “Do not judge according to
appearance, but judge with righteous judgment” (Jn 7:24). These
judgmental, condescending proveitists thought that the way they lived on the
outside proved that they were right with God. Jesus taught otherwise.
       Even so, Christ acknowledged that these religious hypocrites who
opposed Him appeared righteous on the outside:
         27
            Woe to you, scribes and Pharisees, hypocrites! For you are
         like whitewashed tombs which on the outside appear beautiful,
         but inside they are full of dead men's bones and all uncleanness.
         28
            Even so you too outwardly appear righteous to men, but
         inwardly you are full of hypocrisy and lawlessness. (Mt 27:27-
         28).

Following Christ’s warning, we should be wary of those who think that their
outward performance proves their moral superiority. Such religiosity is a
sign of hypocrisy. Such hypocrisy is alive and well and can lead to hell.

*
    Forfeitists also believe that they have to be better than others.
                      Chapter 1.
              Proveitist Presuppositions
        Proveitists assume that the way a professing believer lives proves
whether that believer genuinely has experienced saving faith. According to
the proveitist theology, if you (as a professing believer) do not live a good
life, then you are not a genuine believer. Ultimately, you will go to hell as a
result of not living well enough. Purportedly, you are sent to hell simply
because you really are an unbeliever, not because you did not live a good
life. Expressed in terms of the parable about the sheep versus goats, your
lack of wool (i.e., your lack of good works) presumably proves that you are
goat rather than a sheep. This parable is thought to provide the collaboration
needed for this proveitistic perspective. After all, these parabolic goats do
nothing good for those in need, whereas the sheep help those in need.
        Something is amiss, nonetheless, when one assumes that this parable
teaches that good people go to heaven while bad people go to hell. The
contention of this book is that the purpose of this parable is not to provide
support for the mentality that the way a person lives proves whether or not
that individual is a sheep or a goat. More is afoot than such a misguided
assumption. In short, the purpose of this book is to disprove proveitism by
an examination that focuses on one of their favorite proof texts: the parable
of the sheep and the goats.
                     Illustration 3. Conditional Evidence
Page 6                                      Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
       The existence of woolly wolves is acknowledged, by Jesus, in
Mt 7:15ff and forgotten, by most, in Mt 25:33ff. Immediately, after
describing the narrow gate, Jesus warns: “Beware of the false prophets, who
come to you in sheep’s clothing, but inwardly are ravenous wolves”
(Mt 7:15). These false prophets are inwardly wolfish but outwardly
sheepish. They look good on the outside. More than a little evidence
suggests that they are proveitists.
       Regardless, many simply assume that in the subsequent passage about
the sheep and the goats that the sheep bear good works (i.e., produce wool)
because they are sheep. Conversely, the goats are assumed not to produce
good wool (i.e., good works) because they are not sheep. Jesus’ previous
warning about woolly wolves, however, throws a monkey wrench into the
proveitist machinery, which assumes that you are a sheep if and only if you
produce wool.* Before examining the monkey wrench though, an
examination of the machinery is in order.
       Proveitists insist that sheep produce the wool of good works because
proveitists assert that such wool is necessary evidence of being a sheep (i.e.,
of being saved). In making wool the necessary evidence of initially being a
sheep, proveitists hope to avoid being accused ultimately of teaching
salvation by works. According to some proveitists, works are merely
necessary proof of being saved, not a condition for being saved.† An
examination of the arguments and texts used to substantiate the proveitist
contention though will make it apparent that proveitism necessarily is
conditionalism. In proveitism, final salvation from eternal damnation is
conditioned (at least secondarily and certainly immediately) upon works.

         Sheep Produce Wool
       The first cog in the proveitist machine is the presupposition that all
sheep produce wool (i.e., all believers produce good works). If the Lord
anticipates in His parable that one will draw upon what is known about
sheep at large, then this proveitist premise is proven false from the outset.
Hairy sheep produce fur, no wool at all. Perhaps the Lord does not intend to
include such sheep in His illustrations, however. To give the proveitist
interpretation a sporting chance, such sheep will be excluded from
consideration. The Lord’s teaching about sheep will be restricted to those
types of sheep that naturally produce wool. Thus, from the outset,

*

Edwardseanism: initial justification ⇔ perseverance ⇔ final justification.
†
  According to such proveitists, works might be said to be a condition of being
saved, not a condition for being saved. See Mere Christianity and Moral
Christianity for rebuttal of this proveitist proposition as well.
Chapter 1. Proveitist Presuppositions                                    Page 7

genetically woolless sheep will be excluded from consideration, even though
such sheep make up about ten percent of the word’s sheep population.*
       Moreover, in view of Mt 7:15, let the advantage be further granted to
the proveitist position that the wool (naturally produced by the intended
sheep in Jesus’ parable) represents good works (i.e., wool = good works).
Therefore, to maximize the proveitist advantage, the sheep pictured by Jesus
in Mt 25 will be assumed herein to be sheep that naturally produce wool so
that even newborn lambs are covered with dense, finely curled wool.
Consequently, this wool will be assumed to represent good works so that all
believers naturally produce good works even from the point of birth.
Granting these multiple assumptions to proveitists allows them to be granted
the first part of their proof which is: If you are a believer, then it is
reasonable to presume that you produce good works. The if-then symbol
(⇒)† may be used to summarize their position:

    •   Sheep ⇒ wool (If he/she is a sheep, then he/she produces wool.)
    •   Believer ⇒ good works (If he/she is a believer, then he/she
        produces good works.)

Wool is Produced by Sheep
        The proveitist scheme also needs for their statements to be readable in
reverse so that if the animal produces wool, then it must be a sheep (i.e.,
wool ⇒ sheep). After all, proveitists want the wool to be a determining
factor as to the identity of the sheep. For this reason, it will not do to have
wool (i.e., good works) being produced by goats, much less by wolves. (For
now, the obvious problems inherent in such presuppositions are being
ignored so that the proveitists can have their cake and eat it too.) Therefore,
let it be conceded (for the moment) that these sheep naturally produce wool;
the wolves do not. Accordingly, at the very least, it may be conceded that the
wolves in Mt 7:15 are wearing sheep’s clothing. They are not wolves of the
Tibetan variety (called woolly wolves because of the clothing that they
naturally produce).‡ The wool they are wearing is not wolves’ clothing. By
the same principle, woolly goats are excluded from consideration.§ The
premise thus adopted herein is that the goats and wolves in Jesus’ parables

are not able naturally to produce wool (i.e., they cannot produce the same
type of good works as produced by sheep). Therefore, for the sake of
argument, the inverse of the initial statements made above are adopted.
Sheep produce wool. Inversely, wool is produced by sheep. More
specifically, anytime you find wool, you know it was produced by sheep.

    •    Wool ⇒ sheep (If he/she produces wool, then he/she is a sheep.)
    •    Believer ⇒ good works (If he/she produces good works, then
         he/she is a believer.)

If these statements are combined with the previous statements, the result is:
sheep ⇒ wool and sheep ⇐ wool.* These statements can be condensed with
one conditional symbol pointing both ways: sheep ⇔ wool. Accordingly, it
is a sheep if and only if it produces wool.†

         Wool is Necessary
      The second half of the proveitist argument stipulates that wool is
necessary for a positive outcome at judgment and that the outcome
determines who will go to heaven or hell.
                 Illustration 4. Bema Judgment of Believers

Chapter 1. Proveitist Presuppositions                                    Page 9

         Such an assessment is better suited for those theologies that teach
general judgment. Nevertheless, proveitism can be found readily enough in
dispensationalism, which teaches a separate judgment for believers versus
unbelievers. If you are being judged at the dispensational bema, it is already
a foregone conclusion that you are a raptured believer, who is in heaven, and
that the outcome of the judgment cannot determine if you go to heaven.
         When dispensationalists join hands with those who believe in a joint
judgment for both believers and unbelievers (as though good works are
necessary to do well at judgment and go to heaven), one certainly may
question how doing so is compatible with dispensationalism. Yet, so that
dispensational proveitists also can have their cake and eat it too, this
proveitist discrepancy on the part of dispensationalists will be ignored in the
present book. For sake of the present discussion, let the proveitist machine
be granted as many advantages as necessary to interact with their overall
perspective. As will be seen forthwith, even the granting of such advantages
is still insufficient for proveitism to gain the upper hand.
         The next step in the proveitist chain of events is where the proveitist
argument shatters upon the shores of works righteousness, with no logically
consistent place left to conceal their disadvantage. According to this
proveitistic cog, if you produce (enough) good works, then you are allowed
into the proveitist heaven.

    •   Wool ⇒ sheepfold
    •   Good works ⇒ heaven (i.e., final justification)

        Proveitists can become very creative at this point in devising
ingenious explanations as to why their theology does not teach salvation by
works. Classic proveitists clarify that initial justification is apart from works
but final justification is by God-enabled works. Simple proveitists think that
God-enabled works can provide a reasonable basis for final justification.
Sophisticated proveitists teach that final justification is according to works,
not by works. Virtual proveitists not only condition final justification on
God-enabled works but also import postconversional God-enabled works
into initial justification. Discrepant proveitists insist that good works are
only necessary evidence of initial justification, not a condition for final
justification.
        Rather than reproduce my arguments provided elsewhere as to the
conditional nature of the proveitist argument or garner arguments from
classic, simple, sophisticated, and virtual proveitists themselves to this
effect, it will suffice to allow Jesus, with one simple word from the parable
before us, to demonstrate the discrepancy inherent within discrepant
proveitism: “Then the King will say to those on His right, ‘Come, you who
are blessed of My Father, inherit the kingdom prepared for you from the
Page 10                                       Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
foundation of the world. For [gar] I was hungry, and you gave Me
something to eat” (Mt 25:34-35).
        The explanatory for (gar) introducing verse 35 tells why the sheep are
allowed to inherit the kingdom in verse 34. Giving food is not the result of
inheriting the kingdom but the requirement for inheriting it. And if it is
assumed that inheriting the kingdom is synonymous with entering the
kingdom, as proveitists claim, then giving food is a requirement for entering
heaven. Even the inevitable view of works still makes works a necessary
requirement for final salvation in this parable.* The final verdict as to
whether one is righteous (Mt 25:37,46) is conditioned on proveitist
performance: Inevitable works are necessary works.
        Jesus is not saying, contrary to discrepant proveitism, that works only
prove that the righteous are sheep (and that these sheep consequently inherit
the kingdom just because they are sheep). Instead, the Lord plainly points to
the works these sheep produce as providing the reason these sheep are
allowed to inherit the kingdom. The sheep inherit the kingdom because of
their works.
        Although sophisticated proveitists might object to saying that the
sheep were justified by works, even sophisticated proveitists would
acknowledge that the sheep are justified at this judgment because of their
works. In any event, those proveitists who insist that the good works are
merely the evidence of their initial salvation are stymied by the fact that this
evidence is the basis (i.e., condition) for their final salvation.
        Biblically, final justification is contrastable with initial justification:
“He [initially] saved us [past tense], not because of works done by us in
righteousness, but according to his own mercy” (Tit 3:5; ESV). Initial
justification/salvation is not because of works. Final justification, on the
other hand, is because of works (as is demonstrated in the parable of the
sheep and the goats, among other passages) and thus pertains to rewards.
        For proveitists, final justification is equatable with heaven. Therefore,
as already noted, their theology may be expressed accurately as:

    •     Good works ⇒ heaven (i.e., final justification).

Being allowed into proveitist heaven is conditioned on postconversional
performance (i.e., on works). Allegedly, this is not salvation by works.
Explanations as to why this is supposedly the case vary according to which
proveitist theory is invoked.

*
  As to the proveitist claim regarding the supposed inevitable nature of these
good works (so that sheep inevitably produce these good works just because
they are sheep), this assertion fails to circumvent the becausal and therefore
conditional nature of Jesus’ statement.
Chapter 1. Proveitist Presuppositions                                 Page 11

        Heaven is Conditional
       Regardless of theoretical differences among the various proveitistic
branches, if one enters proveitist heaven, it is only because one has produced
the necessary performance. According to their theology, the only way for a
sheep to enter heaven is to produce the necessary evidence of being a sheep,
namely good works. Accordingly, the second half of the proveitist argument
may be concluded with the reverse statements.

    •    Sheepfold ⇒ wool
    •    Heaven (i.e., final justification) ⇒ good works

       Just as the first half of the proveitist argument could be expressed by
the bidirectional if-and-only-if symbol (⇔), so the second half of the
proveitist position can be summarized by the bidirectional arrow:

    •    Wool ⇔ sheepfold
    •    Good works ⇔ heaven (i.e., final justification)

        You will enter the proveitist sheepfold of heaven if and only if you
produce the necessary good works. Yet it would be unfair to the proveitist
perception to act as if proveitism believes that anyone can produce these
good works or to misrepresent proveitism as simply conditioning final
justification on the production of good works. To the contrary, entrance into
heaven is proveitistically conditioned on sheepish performance, not simply
on performance itself. Goat hair will not qualify for entrance.

        Full Equation
      The first and second halves of the proveitist statements thus need to
be combined to adequately represent the full proveitist equation.

    •    Sheep ⇔ wool ⇔ sheepfold
    •    Believer ⇔ good works ⇔ heaven

According to such premises, if you are a sheep, then you will produce wool.
Conversely, you will produce wool if and only if you are a sheep. Likewise,
if you produce wool, then you will enter the sheepfold. In turn, you will
enter the sheepfold if and only if you produce wool. Correspondingly, if you
are a regenerate believer, then you will produce good works (of a sufficient
quantity and quality to qualify for entrance into heaven). Furthermore, you
will produce such good works if and only if you are a regenerate believer.
Likewise, if you produce good works, then you will enter heaven. In turn,
you will enter heaven if and only if you produce good works.
Page 12                                      Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep

        Intermediate Link
        The intermediate link in the conditionalist chain needs a word of
explanation. Even securitists (i.e., advocates of unconditional security such
as the present author) can agree with proveitists that there is a sense in which
only regenerate believers can produce good works: Everyone who produces
righteousness is born of God (1Jn 2:29; TM). Where securitists and
conditionalists part company is when conditionalists make that production a
necessary practice. To clarify, I will restate this verse with a typical
expanded proveitist understanding: Everyone who practices righteousness is
born of God and only those who practice righteousness are born of God
(1Jn 2:29 proveitist paraphrase). Demanding that poieo be translated as
practice is highly questionable. Expanding the verse to mean that only those
who practice a holy lifestyle are born of God is thoroughly objectionable.
        For the proveitist theory to have a fighting chance, it needs a
manifestative righteousness that necessarily distinguishes those who are
born of God from those who are not born of God.1 A missing link at this
critical juncture would cause the theory of proveitism to collapse
theologically just as badly as the theory of evolution breaks
paleontologically because of its missing links. Perseverance is the missing
link needed by proveitists. In fact, the proveitist chain of events could be
summarized by this equation:

    •     Initial justification ⇔ perseverance ⇔ final justification

       Because of the critical problems presented for this summation by a
text such as Rev 2:2-4 (which indicates that perseverance is not enough
since perseverance must be accompanied by love) and because a text such as
Rev 2:19 (which indicates that loving perseverance is not enough since it
must be accompanied by moral and theological purity), let the perseverance
in the above equation represent the morally and theological pure and loving
continuation necessary to qualify as an overcomer in those contexts,
particularly since those contexts clearly limit the promised positive outcome
(associated by proveitists with final justification) on such performance so as
to make the conditional nature of the outcome established beyond any doubt.
In short, let perseverance be regarded as the perseverance necessary for the
desired outcome. Even so, the outcome is conditional. One must overcome
to have this outcome.

        Explanation of Illustration
       With these considerations before us, Illustration 3. Conditional
Evidence may be considered in further detail. The word saved in the
equation sheep ⇔ wool ⇔ saved refers to final salvation. Obviously, the
word saved is placed as the end term of this equation to make this clear. This
Chapter 1. Proveitist Presuppositions                                 Page 13

final proveitist salvation/justification is not conditioned simply on the
production of wool (i.e., perseverance) but on the production of a wool (i.e.,
perseverance) that can be produced only by sheep. Thus, final salvation is
conditioned jointly on the necessity that one be a sheep and that one produce
wool as a result of being a sheep. To proveitists, the parable of the sheep and
the goats seems to serve as irrefutable proof supporting this conclusion,
since both aspects (sheep and wool) are stressed as being necessary for the
final verdict.
                    Illustration 5. Heart of the Argument




       The heart of the proveitist illustration above is simply a pictorial
representation of the mathematical expression: sheep ⇔ wool ⇔ saved.
Being saved (in the end), as denoted by its place at the end of the equation,
is conditioned on one being a sheep that produces wool. Final justification
requires the wool as proof that one is a sheep. Some proveitists try to limit
the function of wool in this parabolic judgment to a role of confirmative
evidence so that the verdict is based solely on the fact that one is a sheep.
              Illustration 6. Misrepresentation of the Argument




       Discrepant proveitists are particularly prone to misrepresent the
nature of the proveitist argument by giving excessive (and sometimes
exclusive) stress to the confirmative nature of the evidence so as to deny or
dismiss its conditional import. They distort the nature of the argument,
acting as if (1) the Lord were teaching that the wool proves that you are a
sheep and (2) that the (exclusive) reason you are saved is because you are a
sheep. They would pretend that the equation is: wool ⇔ sheep ⇔ saved.
Page 14                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
This distortion encroaches on being dishonest. Jesus clearly says that the
reason these sheep inherit the kingdom is because of their performance.
These sheep gave Him something to eat, so He gives them the kingdom. He
gives because they gave. The attempt, on the part of some proveitists, to
twist what the Lord is saying by turning it around (so as to make it mean that
the sheep inherit the kingdom simply because they are sheep, as proven by
their performance) is to discredit the Lord’s own explanation. Additionally,
there are multiple texts that clearly state the conditional nature of such
performance. This final justification is certainly conditioned on the wool
produced by the sheep, both in this text and in a variety of similar texts.
       Those proveitists who are forthright in acknowledging that their
theology conditions the outcome of the sheep on the wool they produce are
being much more honest with the text. For that matter, proveitists who
acknowledge the conditional nature of the relationship are being much more
logically consistent than their discrepant counterparts. If the outcome were
not conditioned on the production of the wool, then the production of the
wool would not be necessary for the outcome. By acknowledging that the
wool is necessary, discrepant legalists unwittingly have admitted that the
outcome is conditioned on the wool.
                    Illustration 7. Focus of the Argument




       Consistent proveitists—along with consistent forfeitists—will
acknowledge that proveitism conditions the outcome on perseverance. To be
saved (in the end), one must persevere (in the interim). Sheep are saved
because they persevere in producing good works. Logically, this can be
nothing other than salvation by works. Even goats can pick up on the fact
that proveitist sheep are teaching salvation by works and claiming to be
saved (in the end) because they produce these works, whereas those whom
they label as goats do not. No thinking goat will miss seeing the rat in the
proveitist argument.
Chapter 1. Proveitist Presuppositions                               Page 15

          Illustration 8. Conversation Between a Sheep and a Goat
Page 16                                      Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep




        Surprisingly to some perhaps, better informed securitists will agree
that in this context the inheritance/salvation is conditioned on works! With a
slight (but very important modification to the proveitistic Illustration 7
above), securitists may agree with conditionalists even regarding the basic
accuracy of that diagram. So why are securitists not susceptible to the same
criticism from the goats? Because securitists do not necessarily claim to be
better than goats and because securitists do not speak out of both sides of
their mouths when teaching about salvation by works.
        According to securitists, Christians are not necessarily perfect in their
production of wool, nor are Christians necessarily observably better than
goats in the way they live. The fundamental difference between the lost and
the saved is that the saved are saved. As tautological as this statement may
be, it should not go without saying that Christians are forgiven, but not
necessarily better than everyone else in terms of noticeable performance. So
how can securitists be true to Jesus’ parable without stipulating that sheep
always act better than goats? That is the subject matter of the next chapter.
                         Chapter 2.
                     Securitist Proposal
       Securitists believe that heaven is assured unconditionally for the
believer. Rewards in heaven, however, are an entirely different matter. The
way believers live really matters when it comes to rewards. The consistent
securitist has no problem affirming that the inheritance of the kingdom is a
reward. Paul, for that matter, affirms that the inheritance is a reward based
on the believer’s works (Col 3:23-24). Therefore, it comes as no surprise to
the securitist when Jesus, in complete agreement with Paul, pictures the
inheritance of the kingdom as something given in response to the sheep’s
performance (Mt 25:34-35). The theology of Paul’s resurrected Christ
matches the theology of Matthew’s earthly Jesus.
                        Illustration 9. Securitist Model




Future Salvation
       A comparison of the securitist model (Illustration 9) with the focus of
the proveitist argument (Illustration 7) reveals two distinguishing features
between the two illustrations. First, in the securitist model, it is not assumed
that if you are a sheep, you necessarily will produce wool. According to
securitists, if you are a sheep and produce wool, then you will be saved
(A & B ⇒ C). A very important ampersand has been added to the securitist
model.
       Second, the converse is not assumed. The proveitist model believes:
sheep ⇐ wool (with sheep ⇔ wool as the combined result). The securitist
model does not assume that if you produce wool, then you are a sheep.
Consequently, the large arrow in the securitist model only points one
direction (for the combined result: A & B ⇒ C). 2
Page 18                                  Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep

Chapter 2. Securitist Proposal                                           Page 19

        In both the proveitist model and the securitist model, salvation is
conditioned on perseverance. Of course, securitists understand this type of
salvation as referring to rewards in heaven (i.e., co-glorification with Jesus
on His throne).* Unlike proveitists, securitists do not make a reward of
heaven. When Jesus says, for example, “The one who endures to the end will
be saved” (Mt 10:22; 24:13) and “by your perseverance you will save your
soul-lives” (Lk 21:19; TM), securitists acknowledge that such salvation is
conditioned on perseverance. From the securitist point of view, it is natural
that this future salvation be conditioned on performance because this future
salvation is a reward. Paul likewise affirms that if believers persevere, then
they will rule with Jesus (2Tim 2:12). Salvation from the loss of one’s soul-
life, in terms of heavenly rewards (so that one is qualified to rule in the
millennial kingdom as a result of the way one lives), is conditioned on
perseverance.†
        As to the proveitist misrepresentation of the situation in Illustration 6,
securitists do not jump to the conclusion that only those who endure to the
end are saved or have been saved. Jesus is not giving a litmus test by which
to determine if one is already saved. Rather, He is giving the condition for
final (i.e., future) salvation. Jesus is talking about a future salvation when He
says that those who persevere will be saved and that those who persevere
will save their soul-lives (i.e., their earthly lives). Likewise, Jesus is not
inspecting wool in the parable of the sheep and goats to determine if those
under review are sheep. He rewards them on the basis of their works; He
does not separate the sheep from the goats on the basis of their works.
Separation is based on what they are: a sheep or a goat. Compensation is
based on what they do: help in spreading the gospel.

Commands to Produce Wool
       The whole proveitist notion that Jesus intends to teach that what a
person does proves what a person is reduces to absurdity when one realizes
that the NT, Jesus included, commands believers to produce good works.
Yet you do not have to command sheep to produce wool! Neither Jesus, in
particular, nor the NT, in general, takes for granted that theological sheep
will persevere in their production of moral wool. The production of the wool
is not considered inevitable! For example, Jesus refers to believers as “the
light of the world.” Rather than turn around and say, “If you do not shine,

Page 20                                       Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
then you are not a light,” He exhorts these believers, “Let your light shine”
(Mt 5:14,16). If the observable production of light is not automatic to
regenerate lights, then why assume that the production of discernible wool is
automatic to regenerate sheep? If those who are light can hide their light,
why can sheep not hide their wool?

Securitist Mathematics
       The mathematics behind the securitist model, therefore, do not
presuppose that all sheep will produce a sufficient degree of observable wool
so as to distinguish them from the goats around them. To be sure, according
to securitists, if you are a sheep and produce sufficient wool, then you will
be saved. Nonetheless, this statement does not mean: sheep ⇔ sufficient
wool ⇔ saved. Instead, it means: (sheep + sufficient wool) ⇔ saved.* The
ampersand is represented mathematically by the plus sign. The only way you
are going to be saved, in the manner described in this context, is to be a
sheep and produce a distinctive amount of wool.
                     Illustration 11. Securitist Clarification




       The less technical representation of the securitist position will suffice,
however: A & B ⇒ C. If (A) you are a sheep and (B) if you produce wool,
then (C) you will be saved (in terms of inheriting the kingdom). Since what

*
  Some might object that the B-part of the condition is not a co-condition for the
fulfillment but merely descriptive. Logically, this is possible in certain cases.
See my book, Salvation. A compound protasis does not prove that both parts of
the protasis are required for the apodosis. That is, A & B may not be related in
the same way to the apodosis C. However, in the present case B cannot be
relegated to being merely descriptive since Jesus explicitly states that C is given
because of B. Because B is necessitative, C is conditioned on B.
Chapter 2. Securitist Proposal                                          Page 21

is at stake for the sheep is the inheritance of the kingdom rather than
salvation from eternal fire, this clarification is added to the securitist model
by the substitution of inherit for the word saved. Conditionalists will object
that what is at stake for the sheep is both inheritance of the kingdom and
salvation from damnation. A more reasonable deduction though is that
salvation from the eternal fire is conditioned on separation (i.e., on what they
are) rather than on what they do (i.e., on wool). Nevertheless, goats go to
hell (1) because of their poor performance (gar again in Mt 25:42) and
(2) because they are goats (lacking regeneration—i.e., lacking a sheep
nature). Sheep, likewise, inherit the kingdom (1) because of their good
performance and (2) because they are sheep (i.e., not lacking regeneration).

The Big Three
        Just because the acquisition of the inheritance is conditioned on
being a sheep and on doing what sheep naturally do (in terms of producing
wool), this duality does not mean that salvation from damnation must be
conditioned both on what goats are and on what they do. Salvation from
damnation is based exclusively on the separation of the sheep from the
goats. Jesus does not say, “Because you gave Me something to eat, I have
separated you from the goats.” Proveitists unjustly read the confirmation of
initial justification into the judgment. Securitists, in contrast, take the
passage at face value.
                Illustration 12. Singular and Dual Conditions

                      Separation Acquisition                   Damnation
Sheep                              
Wool                                
Goat                                                                
Hair                                                                 
        Looking down the separation column (i.e., the second column), initial
justification (i.e., being a sheep rather than a goat) is seen to be the singular
basis for the separation. Correspondingly, goats are separated from sheep for
the singular reason that they are goats. As to the next column, final mistholic
justification (i.e., acquisition), in demarcation, is based dually on being both
a sheep and on producing wool. In the last column, damnation is based
jointly on goatish identity and performance.3
        As usual, the purpose of this Mt. Olivet parable is to contrast the
outcome of two different groups of people. This does not mean that the same
groups of people are being contrasted in each parable in the Mt. Olivet
Discourse. Quite the opposite is demonstrable. As always, judgment is based
Page 22                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
on works. Never does this mean that salvation from eternal damnation is
based on works. The works of the sheep qualify them for acquisition of the
kingdom, not for entrance into the kingdom. Just as the works of the goats
provide a basis for the damnation of the goats, so the works of the sheep, in
turn, provide a basis for the acquisition of the sheep.

Because They Are Sheep
         Do sheep produce wool because they are sheep? Of course they do.
Still, this does not prove proveitism. Let a follow-up question be asked, “Do
all sheep produce a sufficient amount of wool so as to distinguish them from
goats and to qualify them for kingdom inheritance just because they are
sheep?” Hardly! If believers produced enough good works to qualify for the
inheritance just because they are regenerate, then there would be no need to
exhort them to produce such good works after they become regenerate. Their
shepherds (i.e., pastors) could just sit back and let the good works roll!

Natural Wool Production
       Naturally, one would expect sheep that genetically are disposed to
produce wool to do so. The point made by securitists is that such production
is natural, not necessary. Securitists find it natural to assume that all
believers will produce some degree of good works. Where securitists object
is when proveitists start making the production of good works necessary to
reach heaven. Entrance into securitist heaven is obtained for the sheep (not
by the sheep) by the Shepherd (not for the Shepherd). Jesus finished
everything that was necessary (performance wise) for the sheep to enter
heaven. Redemption has been accomplished fully for the redeemed by the
Redeemer. The sheep do not redeem themselves. Not only with their lips,
but also with their theology, sheep sing:

                 Redeemed—how I love to proclaim it!
                 Redeemed by the blood of the Lamb;
                 Redeemed through His infinite mercy;
                 His child, and forever, I am.

Contrary to their lips, but in harmony with their theology, goats sing:

                 Redeemed—how I hope to achieve it!
                 Redeemed by the sweat of my brow;
                 Redeemed by His enabling grace;
                 His child and forever I’ll be.”

      Are you a sheep or a goat? Your song will tell. The tell-tale sign of a
sheep versus a goat is the song produced by their theology. Goats look away
Chapter 2. Securitist Proposal                                        Page 23

from the Redeemer to their fur for proof of redemption. Sheep look away
from their fleece to their Redeemer for proof of redemption.

Proof of Faith
        Nevertheless, proof of faith is extremely important to sensible sheep.
Peter encourages the sheep: “In this you greatly rejoice, even though now for
a little while, if necessary, you have been distressed by various trials, that
the proof of your faith, being more precious than gold which is perishable,
even though tested by fire, may be found to result in praise and glory and
honor at the revelation of Jesus Christ” (1Pet 1:6-7). Peter’s statement may
be summarized as: proof ⇒ praise.
        Contrary to proveitists, Peter is not saying, proof ⇒ sheep. Peter’s
concern is not that the sheep prove themselves to be sheep so that God will
allow them into heaven. Rather, Peter wants the sheep he is feeding to
qualify for the reward of God’s praise as the result of their proven faith.
        Paul also confirms that judgment may result in God’s praise for
woolly sheep (1Cor 4:5). Judgment is based on works. Such works provide
the basis for being rewarded with praise, glory, and honor.
        Faith is proven by works. Just as exercising proves that you believe
that exercise is good for you, so living a good life (in view of promised
rewards) proves that you believe that God rewards good behavior. However,
lack of good behavior no more proves that you do not have faith than lack of
exercise proves that you do not believe that exercise is good for you. We do
not always act in a manner consistent with our faith.
        Some people hardly ever act in a manner consistent with some of
their beliefs. Witness the couch potatoes. Nevertheless, their lack of
consistency, lack of proof, lack of wool, or lack of sweat does not prove that
they do not have faith. Notwithstanding the existence of their faith though,
the positive benefits of exercise are limited to those who prove their faith in
exercise by exercising. In similar fashion, praise, glory, and honor are
limited to those believers who prove their faith by their faithfulness.
        Persevering faith that proves itself in works is rewarded: “Being
rewarded the salvation of your soul-lives as the outcome of your faith”
(1Pet 1:9; TM). This type of salvation is called the salvation of your souls by
Peter. The earthly lives (i.e., soul-lives) of these believers will be found to
result in heavenly rewards if they persevere. Because rewards are based on
works, this rewarded faith is a work. Thus, this type of faith is a work.
        Initial justification, in contrast, was received by faith apart from
works. This type of faith is not considered a work, for good reason. A
singular, passive, persuasion is not meritorious. Soteriological faith is not a
work. In contradistinction, final justification, in terms of rewards, is
obtained by a preserving faith that is itself considered a work. Because the
Page 24                                      Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
outcome of mistholic faith is a reward, misthological faith must be
considered a work.*
       Lexically, faith is passive persuasion. Logically, faith must be
distinguished from the proof of faith. Salvation from eternal damnation is
conditioned on faith alone, not on the proof of faith. Salvation in terms of
rewards, on the other hand, is conditioned on both the proof of faith and
perseverance in faith.
       Saving faith, in terms of initial justification (i.e., regarding entrance
into heaven), is the passive persuasion that one has eternal life (i.e., that one
possesses permanent life permanently) the moment one believes in Christ for
the free gift of eternal life.
       Saving faith, in terms of final justification (i.e., regarding rewards in
heaven), is (1) the persevering persuasion that one has eternal life as a gift
the moment one believes in Christ for the free gift of eternal life and (2) the
persevering persuasion that one enters subsequently into a much fuller
experience of eternal life by living a God-enabled good life. Such faith must
be enjoined with good works to be saving misthologically. Live well and
prosper is more than a Vulcan sentimentality. It is a biblical possibility.
                     Illustration 13. Securitist Summation




*
  Soteriological (derived from soteria) is used in reference to salvation from
hell. Misthological (derived from misthos) is used in reference to rewards.
Chapter 2. Securitist Proposal                                       Page 25


       Proof of faith is natural, not inevitable. Such proof is certainly not
necessary or infallible evidence as to whether one has been born again and
become a child of God (1Pet 1:3). Peter exhorts the sheep: “As obedient
children, do not be conformed to the former lusts which were yours in your
ignorance, but like the Holy One who called you, be holy yourselves also in
all your behavior” (1Pet 1:14-15). In other words, Peter is saying in effect,

      Sheep, do not behave like goats! Be woolly sheep, not hairy
      goats—in the way you behave. The proof of your faith is
      necessary, not to be children, but to be obedient children, and
      thus ultimately to be rewarded children who save their earthly
      lives from being wasted by going up in smoke at the Judgment
      Seat of Christ (cf. 1Cor 3:11-15).

        In the natural world, shepherds do not have to exhort their sheep to
produce wool. But shepherds do have to feed them, so wool production is
not guaranteed. In the theological world, shepherds not only have to feed
their sheep, they also have to exhort their sheep to produce wool.
                Illustration 14. Shepherd Pleading with Sheep




       Paul actually begs Christians to get their act together (Gal 4:12). In
the physical arena, shepherds do not get down on their knees and beg their
sheep to produce wool because physical sheep inevitably produce wool. But
theological sheep do not necessarily produce wool. Therefore, it is necessary
for their shepherds to plead with them to produce the wool that they
naturally should produce.
Page 26                                      Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep

Natural is not Necessary
        Risking one’s life to help those who proclaim the gospel during the
tribulation and not taking the mark of the beast certainly will be strong
indicators that one has believed the gospel. Such good works naturally will
demonstrate that one has believed the gospel. Conversely, not helping
evangelists during that time period naturally will indicate that one does not
believe their message. Moreover, taking the mark of the beast will be
infallible proof that one does not believe the gospel.4 Unfortunately, because
of such considerations, some dispensationalists have jumped to the proveitist
conclusion that one’s status as a sheep or goat is proven by one’s works.
        As will be seen in the ensuing discussion, Jesus had just demonstrated
contextually (by means of the preceding three parables) that some sheep
behave very badly. Startling as it may be, some believers even beat their
fellow believers instead of helping them (Mt 24:49)! Therefore, the
dispensational presupposition that all believers naturally will demonstrate
their faith during the tribulation is open to strenuous objection. Even if the
sheep are rewarded for what comes naturally (i.e., for helping those who
helped them by sharing the gospel with them), Jesus is by no means saying
that all sheep necessarily act lovingly, even if responding lovingly is a
natural expression of their sheep nature.
        A more reasonable supposition is that Jesus is illustrating that the way
one treats Jesus’ representatives (i.e., His brethren) is representative of the
way one treats Him. The reason rewards and punishments are meted out in
such disproportionate portions to the service or disservice shown is because
of the Infinite Person to whom the action is ultimately rendered or withheld.
Jesus’ point is not that all sheep will be helpful, even if by nature they are so
inclined, but that all helpful sheep will be rewarded to a surprising degree
because of the Person to whom the service ultimately is rendered.*
        The claim that the production of (white) wool proves whether one is a
child of God ignores the theological terrain in which the children of God
feed. The biblical texts, upon which the sheep feed, pose an interesting array
of woolly creatures within that terrain: goatish sheep, sheepish goats, woolly
wolves, and carnivorous sheep, as well as black sheep and gray sheep. These
theological animals will be examined in the remaining discussion. Even the
weaker claim made by some dispensationalists (and shockingly by some
securitists)—that Jesus is indicating that all sheep naturally will demonstrate
their status by such positive behavior—misses the mark entirely.

*
  Jesus is not illustrating the natural goodness of the sheep so much as the
surprising degree of the reward or punishment that comes from helping or not
helping the tribulational evangelists because of the greatness of the Person to
whom the service is administered or withheld. See ensuing discussion of
congruent merit and demerit especially in Surprise, 85.
                           Chapter 3.
                          Goatish Sheep
       Has Peter misconstrued the Lord’s parable about sheep by suggesting,
as was demonstrated in the previous chapter, that sheep could act like goats?
Not at all! Proveitists are the distortionists, not Peter. Jesus already has
provided the foundation for Peter’s assessment in His previous parables
within the Mt. Olivet Discourse. We will quickly survey those parables.

Unfaithful Believers
       One-in-the-same servant, whom Jesus has put in charge of His work
during His absence, has the potential of behaving or misbehaving (Mt 24:45-
51). If the servant behaves well, then he will be put in charge of the
kingdom (Mt 24:47). If that servant misbehaves, he will weep as a result
when the Lord returns and deals with him, because goatish sheep (i.e.,
believers who act like goats) will not be put in charge of the kingdom. This
parable of the servant certainly lays the foundation for concluding that
believers may not produce the wool (i.e., the good works) expected of them.

Pure Believers
        The ten virgins are morally pure (Mt 25:1-13). Thus, they do not take
the mark of the beast. Yet participating in the wedding feast (i.e., in
millennial rulership) requires more than being a virgin—more than being a
believer who does not take the mark of the beast. Being a morally pure sheep
is not enough to qualify for rulership. To be an overcomer in the fullest
sense of the word (i.e., to be a woolly sheep) not only requires that one be a
believer but also necessitates that one not take the mark of the beast and that
one love the Lord more than one’s own soul-life (Rev 12:11). Perseverance
in faith and love, accompanied by moral and theological purity, is required
to enjoy the festivities. With this additional parable, the Lord fortifies the
impression that not all believers produce a performance sufficient to qualify
for participation in the celebrations associated with kingdom rulership.

Entrusted Believers
         In the next parable, the slaves entrusted with the talents likewise
represent believers (Mt 25:14-30). Yet one of these slaves hides his talent
(i.e., the light of his regeneration). This sheep hides his light. He refuses to
let it shine. In the end, his opportunity to demonstrate his regenerate nature
by means of godly service via responsible stewardship is taken away. He
does not lose his regeneration that he already possesses, but this goatish
sheep does lose the opportunity to demonstrate that regeneration via ruling
the cities of the kingdom.5 In each of these three Olivet parables, Jesus
Page 28                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
contrasts the outcome of those who inherit the kingdom with those who do
not inherit it.

Regenerate Believers
       Believers already have participated in spiritual regeneration via new
birth: “He saved us, not on the basis of deeds which we have done in
righteousness, but according to His mercy, by the washing of regeneration
and renewing by the Holy Spirit” (Tit 3:5). This facet of regeneration is
nonforfeitable and individual. Quantitatively, present regeneration for each
individual believer is a gift that stretches out unconditionally into eternity
future from the initial point of salvation.
       These regenerate sheep also have the potential to participate in the
future millennial regeneration via rulership: “Jesus said to them, “Truly I say
to you, that you who have followed Me, in the regeneration when the Son of
Man will sit on His glorious throne, you also shall sit upon twelve thrones,
judging the twelve tribes of Israel” (Mt 19:28).
                   Illustration 15. Duality of Regeneration




        Qualitatively, this future regeneration is a reward conditioned on
present performance. To be regenerate in this sense is to be a judge/ruler
within the kingdom society. Jesus proceeds in the next verse to equate this
combined misthological and societal dimension of regeneration with
inheriting eternal life: “And everyone who has left houses or brothers or
sisters or father or mother or children or farms for My name's sake, shall
receive many times as much, and shall inherit eternal life” (Mt 19:29).
        Eternal life, like regeneration, has a duality. In the parable of the
sheep and goats, this duality is seen in the fact that those who already are
sheep (and thus who already possess eternal life) are rewarded with
Chapter 3. Goatish Sheep                                                  Page 29

something they do not yet posses—eternal life. A contradiction is not
implied because two different dimensions of eternal life are in view.*
                     Illustration 16. Duality of Eternal Life




       The goats “will go away into eternal punishment, but the righteous
into eternal life” (Mt 25:46). The goats have not yet been cast into the
eternal punishment of the eternal fire (Mt 25:41). Similarly, the sheep have
not yet entered into eternal life in terms of ruling the kingdom (Mt 25:34).
Just as inheriting eternal life pertains to a future realization of eternal life as
a reward for being a woolly sheep, so going into eternal life refers to a future
acquisition of eternal life as a reward for one’s behavior as a sheep. The
sheep do not yet possess eternal life (nor the inheritance) in this manner.
This qualitative experience of life is yet future.

Smoking Gun
       In the three aforementioned parables cited at the beginning of this
chapter, the Lord contrasts furry sheep, so to speak, with woolly sheep.
Furry sheep do not inherit the kingdom. They will miss out on the future
acquisition of eternal life. Their experience of regeneration will be
diminished, not demolished.
       In the present parable (that of the sheep and goats), furry goats
completely miss out on eternal life because they currently do not have
eternal life. The goats are damned because they do not have eternal life—
because they are goats rather than sheep. Some securitists might say, on the
basis of Rev 20:11-15, that the goats were damned exclusively because of

*
 In technical terms, the two dimensions of eternal life would be distinguished as
soteriological versus misthological life.
Page 30                                        Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
their goatish nature, not because of their goatish works. The position taken
herein is that they were damned because of both: because of their goatish
meat and because of their goatish fur, as it were. Nevertheless, the principle
reason they are damned is because of their goat meat, not because of their
goat fur. The fur is just circumstantial evidence. The smoking gun, to speak
idiomatically, is found in the meat, not in the hide.
         One reason for making this assessment is because some sheep act like
(i.e., look like) goats. Even so, such sheep are saved despite their production
of goatish traits. Forfeitists insist that such sheep lose eternal life and are
damned as a result. To the contrary, while it is true that such sheep lose
eternal life, this does not prove that they lose the gift of eternal life. How can
the possession of a gift be conditioned on performance? It cannot be! Just as
emphatically, a gift that is constituted as eternal life can never be lost.
Conditionalists are on slippery footing when they condition the possession
of a gift on performance, even more so when they condition retention of a
gift on ongoing performance when that gift is constituted as a life that does
not end and that already is possessed.
         Securitists are on much firmer footing in asserting that goatish sheep
lose the reward of eternal life, not the gift of eternal life. Adopting, then, the
securitist premise, since the production of goat hair by goatish sheep does
not result in such sheep being sent to hell, the fundamental reason goats are
sent to hell must be because of their goat meat, not because of their goat
hair. Or, as stated in the chart, separation is based solely on who they are—
goats (i.e., unbelievers).*
         In one sense, then, those securitists are correct who maintain that the
sole reason a person is sent to hell is because that individual lacks the gift of
eternal life. Judgment is not for the purpose of deciding who is a sheep or a
goat, nor for separating the sheep from the goats, but for deciding the fate of
those who are sheep versus those who are goats. Positive and punitive
rewards are meted out. For those who are goats, the reward will be eternal
fire. For woolly sheep, the result will be eternal life—as a reward. For furry
sheep, the result is the loss of eternal life—as a reward.
         Furry sheep keep the gift of life. Since furry sheep are not thrown into
hell, it stands to reason to suggest, from a limited perspective at least, that
fur is not the reason people are sent to hell. The reason goats die in their sin
(i.e., in a furry state; cp. Jn 8:21,24) and are sent to hell is because they are
not sheep—because they have not been given eternal life. One might object
that their sins have not been forgiven soteriologically, so goat fur produced
by goats is not dealt with in the same manner as goat hair produced by
sheep. Regardless, it is not the fur, as such, that provides the basis for the


*
    See Illustration 12. Singular and Dual Conditions, 21.
Chapter 3. Goatish Sheep                                                 Page 31

separation but the nature of the creature. Christians are new creatures in
Christ (2Cor 5:17) even if they live like woolless goats in the world.

Woolless Sheep
       That a sheep may be woolless is acknowledged by Peter: “For he who
lacks these qualities is blind or short-sighted, having forgotten his
purification from his former sins” (2Pet 1:9). The qualities to which Peter
refers are the qualities of Christian character he just listed in 2Pet 1:7-9, such
as self-control. Such traits are described by Paul as the fruit of the Spirit
(Gal 5:22-23). In other words, these qualities refer to what is being described
in the present book as wool. Peter is describing a regenerate sheep that lacks
wool. This particular sheep’s former sins are forgiven, but the quality of
entrance this sheep will have into heaven will not be abundant (2Pet 1:11).
                    Illustration 17. What’s on Your Camel?
Page 32                                      Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
       In Peter’s way of thinking, Christians aren’t necessarily perfect; they
might just be forgiven. This is certainly true of the specific believer he has in
mind in 2Pet 1:9. Accordingly, I have no doubt as to which bumper sticker
Peter would have put on the back of his camel.
       Nevertheless, Peter calls upon Christians not to be satisfied with just
being forgiven, and understandably so. For example, Christians are supposed
to be forgiving because they are forgiven and to be loving because they are
loved. The Bible warns that if Christians fail to be woolly (i.e., fail to be
forgiving and loving), they will not inherit the kingdom.
       Obviously, the fact that such sheep are forgiven poses a vexing
problem for proveitism. Such sheep are regenerate but fail to produce the
expected wool. They are an embarrassment to the founding presupposition
of proveitism: sheep ⇒ wool. This proveitist hypothesis is swept away by
Peter. Sheep do not necessarily produce wool, certainly not enough wool to
qualify for inheriting the kingdom, much less a sufficient quality and
quantity of observable wool to distinguish them, by appearance, from wool-
producing goats (i.e., from goats that act like sheep).
       Woolly sheep will have an abundant entrance into the kingdom.
Woolless sheep will have an impoverished entrance into the kingdom. Paul
describes this impoverished entrance as being “saved, yet so as through fire”
(1Cor 3:15). The English idiom would be saved by the skin of their teeth.

Furry Sheep
       In this same context, Paul describes carnal believers who look and act
like natural people (i.e., like the lost). The Greek word sarkikos is translated
as carnal by the KJV. This popular designation will be used herein.
       14
         But a NATURAL man does not accept the things of the Spirit
       of God; for they are foolishness to him, and he cannot
       understand them, because they are spiritually appraised. 15But
       he who is SPIRITUAL appraises all things, yet he himself is
       appraised by no man. 16For who has known the mind of the
       Lord, that he should instruct Him? But we [apostles] have the
       mind of Christ. 1And I, brethren, could not speak to you as to
       spiritual men, but as to CARNAL men, as to babes in Christ. 2I
       gave you milk to drink, not solid food, for you were not yet
       able to receive it. Indeed, even now you are not yet able, 3for
       you are still carnal. For since there is jealousy and strife among
       you, are you not carnal, and are you not walking like natural
       men? (1Cor 2:14-3:3; TM)

       Three categories of people are seen in this passage: natural, carnal,
and spiritual. These carnal believers are described by the apostle Paul as
living/walking like the natural/lost (i.e., according to the dictates of their
Chapter 3. Goatish Sheep                                                 Page 33

fleshly desires). These carnal Christians are living lives characterized by
jealousy (zelos) and strife (eris). Yet these very vices are part of the vice list
in Gal 5:20 where Paul warns Christians that those who live like this will not
inherit the kingdom of God (Gal 5:21).
        In terms of the present discussion, Paul is saying that such carnal
sheep live like lost goats. The problem is not just that such sheep lack the
positive traits (i.e., the fruit of the Spirit) he has listed but that they also
produce the negative traits he also lists. Carnal sheep not only lack wool;
they produce hair—goat hair (i.e., fur).

Wolfish Sheep
       As if acting like goats were not bad enough, other passages describe
the reality of carnivorous sheep who “have fallen from grace” into “a
different [non-saving] gospel” and who “bite and devour one another”
(Gal 1:6-8; 5:4,15). According to Paul, wolves are not the only creatures
capable of biting the sheep. In functional terms, believers are quite capable
of becoming wolves either morally, theologically, or both.
                      Illustration 18. Carnivorous Sheep




       The sheep in the above illustration are pictured rather ambiguously.
Do they just have fangs like wolves, or could they be pictured as fanged
sheep in wolf’s clothing? Not all fanged sheep fall headlong into legalism.
However, those believers who do fall from grace into legalism would be
better pictured as fanged-sheep in wolf’s skin.
       Some carnivorous sheep are more wolfish than others. Just as Jesus
pictures those unbelievers who taught a false gospel as being wolves in
sheep’s clothing (Mt 7:15), so those sheep who teach a false gospel could be
pictured as sheep in wolf’s clothing. Forfeitists would suppose that those
sheep that fall from grace into legalism become wolves in wolf’s clothing.
That is, forfeitists believe that such believers forfeit the gift of eternal life
and become wolves in terms of their nature. To the contrary, securitists point
out that Paul affirms that these fallen sheep were still sons of God (Gal 3:26;
4:6-9,28). They remained sheep underneath their wolf’s skin.
Page 34                                      Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
               Illustration 19. Fanged Sheep in Wolf’s Clothing




       These particular fanged sheep in Galatians are best pictured in wolf’s
clothing because they have fallen into a legalistic perversion of the gospel.
To enjoin the warnings given by Jesus and Paul, one would do well to
advise: “Beware not only of wolves in sheep’s clothing but also beware of
sheep in wolves’ clothing.” Both bite. A flock of sheep can become a pack
of wolves—functionally speaking.
       In terms of identity (i.e., pertaining to the type of meat under the fur),
a sheep is still a sheep. Yet in terms of the fur or fangs, sheep may cease to
be sheep. Forfeitists insist that such sheep completely cease to be sheep and
return to being goats or become wolves instead. Not so. Underneath the fur
and despite the fangs, they are still children of God in regard to their
regenerate nature.
       Paul acknowledges that believers can abandon the faith, but he does
not believe that they cease to be believers (in the regenerate sense of the
word) when they do so. For example, just because someone stops
committing murder, this does not mean that such a person is no longer
considered a murderer. Likewise, just because a believer stops believing, this
does not mean that such a person is no longer considered a believer (in terms
of the effect produced by the original belief). The transformation of a wolf
or a goat into a sheep—in terms of the meat underneath (i.e., in terms of the
new nature)—is an irreversible metamorphosis since the new creature is
given eternal life (an eternal change of his or her nature; cf. 2Cor 5:17).
Chapter 3. Goatish Sheep                                               Page 35

                             Illustration 20. Black Sheep




        According to Paul, for example, flat-tire believers do not become
unbelievers, they become worse than (cheiron) unbelievers (1Tim 5:8). *
Such sheep live worse than many goats, but this does not mean that such
sheep become goats. Even black sheep are still considered sheep. A similar
situation is described by Peter: “For if after they have escaped the
defilements of the world by the knowledge of the Lord and Savior Jesus
Christ, they are again entangled in them and are overcome, the last state has
become worse than [cheiron] the first for them” (2Pet 2:20; TM).
        Peter contrasts their first state as believers with their final state as
believers. When some people become saved, it is like a gray goat becoming
a gray sheep (e.g., some of the carnal believers described by Paul would fall
into this category). Some of these gray-sheep believers may progress to
moral victory and even to Christian maturity and become white sheep.
Unfortunately, sometimes these mature white-sheep believers go back to
