their old way of life. In fact, they may even become more riotous after
slipping back into their old ways than they ever were before.
        Their resulting behavior could be blacker than it ever was before.
Morally, their last state (as black sheep) could be worse than their first state
(as gray sheep). Consequently, their final behavior could be worse than their
initial carnal behavior—and by logical extension, worse than their initial
goatish behavior (although Peter is contrasting their initial carnal behavior).6
        As seen in this survey, in the real world some sheep look like goats;
some sheep are worse than goats; some sheep (to various degrees) act like
wolves. Jesus and His disciples acknowledge these realities. Proveitism is
thus devoid of realism. Contrary to proveitistic notions, the way professing
believers live does not prove if they are sheep or goats underneath (or even

*
    See Illustration 21. Flat-Tire Believers, 37.
Page 36                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
wolves for that matter). Some sheep wear gray goat fur; some sheep wear
black goat fur; some sheep wear wolf’s fur. The only way that we, as fallible
human beings, have of judging what is underneath the profession of other
people is to open their mouths, look down their throats, and examine the
theology that comes out. Be warned. You could lose a finger or two doing
so. These sheep bite! Trying to defang such sheep or wolves is not a
bloodless process.
                          Chapter 4.
                        Sheepish Goats
       As noted in the previous chapter, according to Paul, some believers
live worse than unbelievers (1Tim 5:8). The whole proveitist notion that the
way you live proves whether you are a genuine believer blows a flat tire
when the way some NT believers live is taken into consideration.
Comparatively speaking, the argument for woolless sheep is proof of woolly
goats (i.e., sheepish goats). That is, some unbelievers morally live better
lives than some believers.

Woolless Believers are Brethren
      Paul is explicit: “But if anyone does not provide for his own, and
especially for those of his household, he has denied the faith, and is worse
than an unbeliever” (1Tim 5:8). Such a person is not an unbeliever. Rather,
such a person is worse than an unbeliever. Contrary to forfeitist and
proveitist theology, the NT still regards such a person as a believer. The
whole conditionalistic schema, in which such a person is said to become an
unbeliever or said never to have been a believer, blows a flat tire on the
highway of NT exegesis.
                     Illustration 21. Flat-Tire Believers




       This NT believer did not have a working faith. Most unbelievers are
better workers than this believer. This Christian’s dead, non-productive faith
would save him from hell, but it would not save him from a merciless bema
judgment (Jam 2:13-26) or from the outer darkness. Like the lazy slave in
the Mt. Olivet Discourse who did nothing (Mt 25:26), this lazy Christian that
Paul mentions certainly fails to be the worker that he is supposed to be.
Contrary to conditionalists, the NT repeatedly regards such believers as
Christian brothers:
Page 38                                      Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep

        11
          For we hear that some among you are leading an
        undisciplined life, doing no work at all, but acting like
        busybodies. 12 Now such persons we command and exhort in
        the Lord Jesus Christ to work in quiet fashion and eat their own
        bread. 13 But as for you, brethren, do not grow weary of doing
        good. 14 And if anyone does not obey our instruction in this
        letter, take special note of that man and do not associate with
        him, so that he may be put to shame. 15 And yet do not regard
        him as an enemy, but admonish him as a brother.
        (2Thess 3:11-15)

       These disobedient Christian brethren are as useless as a flat tire
when it comes to putting their faith into practice. Their faith is
completely dead and useless when it comes to providing for the needs
of others. James does not hesitate to draw the necessary implications:
        14
           What use is it, my brethren, if a man says he has faith, but he
        has no works? Can that faith save him? 15 If a brother or sister
        is without clothing and in need of daily food, 16 and one of you
        says to them, “Go in peace, be warmed and be filled,” and yet
        you do not give them what is necessary for their body, what use
        is that? 17 Even so faith, if it has no works, is dead, being by
        itself. (Jam 2:14-17)

       Woolless sheep are worse than many unbelievers. The faith of such
sheep is dead, useless, and unproductive. In the preceding verse, James
warns such believers that the judgment will be merciless for them: “For
judgment will be merciless to one who has shown no mercy; mercy triumphs
over judgment” (Jam 2:13). Works of mercy are required from Christians in
order for Christians to be shown mercy at the Judgment Seat of Christ for
the simple reason that judgment is always based on works.*
       John specifies that the person closing his heart against a fellow needy
Christian brother is indeed his Christian brother:
        17
           But whoever has the world's goods, and beholds his brother
        in need and closes his heart against him, how does the love of
        God abide in him? 18 Little children, let us not love with word
        or with tongue, but in deed and truth” (1Jn 3:17-18)




*
    See “Judgment is Always Based on Works” in Breaking the Rocking Horse.
Chapter 4. Sheepish Goats                                              Page 39

       The Jacobean (and Pauline) a brother is the Johannine his brother. In
other words, John slams the door shut on the proveitist presumption that the
Jacobean person ignoring the needs of a needy brother is not a Johannine
brother. What upsets James and John is not that the world does not provide
for Christians in need. What disturbs them is that Christians do not provide
for needy Christians. Whereas proveitists would like to pretend that such
close-hearted Christians are merely professing Christians, John confesses
that such close-hearted Christians are actual Christians. According to John,
the one closing his heart is indeed his brother. The distasteful reality is that
some Christians are unloving. These loveless Christians are woolless.
Christians are not necessarily better than the lost in terms of their behavior.
       When such Christians are compared to goats, as Paul does in
1Tim 5:8, such believers are seen to produce less wool than do some
unbelievers. Stated conversely, some unbelievers produce more wool than
do some believers. Goats do not naturally produce wool, but theological
goats may produce wool in the sense of living loving, morally better lives
than do some believers. Conversely, sheep naturally produce wool, but,
comparatively speaking, theological sheep might not produce wool.
       John exhorts his little lambs to produce wool (i.e., to love with
works). Again, the production of wool (e.g., helping Christian brethren) is
natural but does not happen automatically or inevitably. A shepherd does not
have to exhort physical sheep to produce wool. Theological sheep, however,
need such exhortations because theological sheep do not necessarily produce
wool.
       Like James and Paul, John alludes to the bema to motivate his little
lambs: “Little children, abide in him, so that when he appears we may have
confidence and not shrink from him in shame at his coming” (1Jn 2:28).
Woolless sheep will be put to shame at the bema because they will be shown
not to have shown works of mercy to their brethren. Believers are naturally
woolly, not necessarily woolly. Misthologically, wool is necessary to do
well at the bema.
       Christ put the relationship between showing mercy to others and
being shown mercy oneself (as a result of one’s performance) this way:
“Blessed are the merciful, for they shall receive mercy” (Mt 5:7). According
to Christ, believers in Christ might fail to show mercy to their fellow
believing brethren (Mt 18:3-35). If you want to experience mercy at the
Judgment Seat of Christ, then as a sheep you better earn this mercy by
earning a living for yourself and by providing for the needs of your physical
family and for your family of believers. Be woolly!

Apples and Oranges
       So what is to be made of the fact that the goats in Jesus’ parable do
not help believers? The commendation that Jesus gives the sheep is that they
Page 40                                      Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
help these brothers of Mine (Mt 25:40). The condemnation Jesus implicitly
brings against these goats in not helping the least of these is that they did not
help Jesus’ brothers. His brethren, not their own brothers, are the ones whom
the goats failed to help. Jesus does not criticize them for not helping these
brothers of yours. These goats are not Christians. The issue for the goats is
not that of believers helping their brethren. Nevertheless, some goats are
more helpful in helping others than some sheep are in helping believers.7
        Then why are unloving goats sent to hell while unloving sheep are
not? Because goats are goats! When it boils down to whether one goes to
heaven or hell, producing wool is really immaterial. The singular material
issue is whether one is a goat or a sheep, not whether one is furry or woolly.
        Then why does Jesus point to their fur and wool respectively?
Because the issue is not simply heaven versus hell! The principle subject
under discussion in the Mt. Olivet parables is inheriting the kingdom. Wool
is necessary to inherit the kingdom. Wool counts. Jesus uses woolless goats
as opposed to woolly sheep in order to underscore the necessity of wool for
kingdom inheritance.* Kingdom entrance is not the dominant theme.
        Naturally, Jesus includes references to theological fur and wool in His
parable of the sheep and goats because judgment is always based on works.
Judgment is not based on whether one is a sheep or a goat. Separation, on
the other hand, is based exclusively on one’s nature: whether one is a sheep
or a goat.
        Judgment though is based on one’s performance. The performance of
a sheep has to be judged separately from the performance of a goat because
a furry sheep is not treated like a furry goat, and a woolly goat is not treated
like a woolly sheep. Therefore, judgment presupposes separation according
to nature before acquisition or damnation can be rendered according to
performance.
        One has to compare apples to apples and oranges to oranges if one is
going to be a good judge. And God is a good judge. The Lord is assuring us
that He knows how to compare apples to apples and oranges to oranges. He
is not comparing bad sheep to good goats (i.e., furry sheep to woolly goats),
not because furry sheep do not exist in the theological arena, but because He
already has dealt with bad sheep in the previous three parables and has dealt
with good goats (i.e., woolly counterfeits) elsewhere.†
*
   More precisely, the Lord is making a contrast between congruent merit and
demerit. Therefore, He naturally contrasts the little done by those who are
positively rewarded with the little done by those who are negatively rewarded.
†
  To shift metaphors, when one is contrasting grapefruit and oranges, one does
not include tangelos. This does not mean that tangelos do not exist. Similarly,
when contrasting sheep and goats, one does not necessarily include goatish
sheep and sheepish goats, but this does not mean that such theological creatures
do not exist.
Chapter 4. Sheepish Goats                                                  Page 41

Woolly Wolves
        Before giving the parable about the woolly sheep and furry goats,
Jesus had pictured false prophets as woolly counterfeits: “Beware of the
false prophets, who come to you in sheep’s clothing, but inwardly are
ravenous wolves” (Mt 7:15). He immediately proceeded to describe their
stooges as dupes who point to their good works as proof that they are saved.
Proveitists are such dupes. As if in response to an inquiry from the Lord as
to why these proveitists think that the Lord should let them into heaven, they
respond: “Lord, Lord, did we not prophesy in Your name, and in Your name
cast out demons, and in Your name perform many miracles?” (Mt 7:22).
They point to their works as proof that they are saved, and Jesus rejects them
on that very basis (Mt 7:23).
        This description by Jesus in Mt 7:22 provides explicit confirmation of
the theological existence of such woolly creatures. Sheepish unbelievers
exist. Some proveitists frantically try to switch gears when they come to this
passage by insisting that the wool referred to in this passage refers to words
rather than works because proveitism insists that works prove whether or not
one is saved. Admitting that, for all appearances, the wolves and their
followers can produce the same wool of good works as is produced by sheep
makes it laughable for proveitists to insist that the wool of good works is the
means by which counterfeit believers are discerned.
        Contrary to such proveitists, a counterfeit is not discerned by
comparing where the counterfeit is indiscernible from the authentic item but
by analyzing where it is different.* To be sure, only sheep can produce
authentic sheep wool—authentic good works that flow from a regenerate
nature (1Jn 2:29). We know this deductively. Nevertheless, we are not
capable of discerning the difference experientially. For all appearances, the
wool worn by woolly wolves and by woolly goats appears to be just as
genuine as the wool produced by sheep. These theological creatures each
produce wool by God’s enabling grace. God knows the difference between
wool produced from a regenerate nature versus wool produced from an
unregenerate nature because He knows the source of the wool. We humans

*
  Even if the image on a fake bill appears to be completely authentic, it is
different from the image on a truly authentic bill in that it comes from a different
source—a different printing press. We may not be able to discern, by examining
the works, whether the good works produced by a professing believer come
from a different source than the authentic good works produced by a genuine
believer. Nevertheless, if we are able to deduce from their theology that the
person making a profession is a wolf in sheep’s clothing, then we can deduce
that their good works are not authentic. Synthetic good works (i.e., good works
produced by a combination of God’s enabling grace and legalistic performance)
are denounced by the Lord (Mt 7:23; Lk 18:11).
Page 42                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
do not necessarily know the source of the wool, however. Consequently, it is
impossible for us to determine if professing believers are lost or saved based
on the wool they produce. Thus, proveitism proves false.

Good Without God
        Atheists are promoting the slogan Good Without God (even writing a
book by this title) and espousing the virtues of compassionate humanism.
Obeying the golden rule of helping others is commended by atheists apart
from any belief in God. An atheist could, in fact, do something nice for a
theist. For that matter, the lost can do something nice for Jesus’ ministers.
Christians are not the only nice people on the planet. Genuine Christians are
not the only people who help others. Such an admission does not mean that
Jesus’ words are devoid of realism. Proveitism might push Jesus’ words to
unrealistic extremes, but a biblical view would acknowledge that lost people
can do good things for others—even for Christians.
        Of course, if an unbeliever can do good things for a believer, then it
stands to reason that a professing believer (who in fact might be lost) can do
good things for believers. In other words, providing food or drink to a
servant of Christ does not prove that one is a genuine believer. Even though
Jesus’ depiction is realistically accurate for the tribulational period being
portrayed in the parable of the sheep and goats, the Lord is illustrating a
principle, not providing an ironclad depiction of the lost versus the saved,
when He describes the goats versus the sheep in such black and white terms.
He is not saying that the lost never raise a finger to help or that the saved
always lend a helping hand. Proveitism proves unrealistic. Contrary to
proveitism, Jesus is not stating that one’s works prove whether one is a
genuine sheep.
                       Illustration 22. Cry of the Wolf
Chapter 4. Sheepish Goats                                                Page 43

         Jesus Himself destroys the proveitist machine when He throws a
monkey wrench the size of Kansas into it. His point is that you will be duped
by a wolf in sheep’s clothing if you look to the wolf’s clothing to determine
if it is a wolf. Jesus clarifies that the way to discern the theological nature of
this theological creature is to listen to its theology (Mt 16:11-12). Jesus
refers to this discernment as knowing them by their fruits (Mt 7:16). Woolly
wolves must be discerned as such by their teaching because woolly wolves
look like sheep:
      27
        Woe to you, scribes and Pharisees, hypocrites! For you are
      like whitewashed tombs which on the outside appear
      beautiful, but inside they are full of dead men’s bones and all
      uncleanness. 28 Even so you too outwardly appear righteous
      to men, but inwardly you are full of hypocrisy and lawlessness.
      (Mt 23:27-28)

       Just as the Pharisaical wolves of Jesus’ day looked good on the
outside and produced beautiful coats of wool in terms of their performance,
theological wolves today may look good on the outside. They even say many
pious things.8 Still, woolly wolves look like sheep but sound like wolves—if
you listen carefully and holistically. When the rubber meets the road, woolly
wolves teach salvation by works and look to their works as proof that they
are saved.9

Good With God
        Certainly, the Biblicist is free to challenge the atheist who insists that
people can do good to one another without God. In the absolute sense (i.e.,
in the theological sense), it may be affirmed: “There is none who does good”
(Rom 3:12). However, this same biblical epistle makes a distinction between
being a righteous person and a good person. Moreover, it acknowledges that
good people (in the relative sense) exist (Rom 5:17). Indeed, the epistle
entertains the startling possibility that nonreligious people can perform as
well, in one manner of speaking, as the typical religious person.
      14
         For when Gentiles [atheists included] who do not have the
      Law do instinctively the things of the Law [such as loving God
      and others], these, not having the Law, are a law to themselves,
      15
         in that they show the work of the Law written in their hearts,
      their conscience bearing witness, and their thoughts alternately
      accusing or else defending them. (Rom 2:14-15)
      26
        If therefore the uncircumcised man keeps the requirements of
      the Law [which can be summed up as loving God and others],
Page 44                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
      will not his uncircumcision be regarded as circumcision? 27 And
      will not he who is physically uncircumcised, if he keeps the
      Law, will he [the nonreligious person] not judge you [the
      religious person] who though having the letter of the Law and
      circumcision are a transgressor of the Law? (Rom 2:26-27)

       People, even lost people, can be relatively kind and loving to one
another. The theist accurately would insist that God’s common grace makes
this common good possible. People are not good without God’s enabling
grace. The good that even lost people do is through God’s grace.
       Atheists, naturally refuse to give glory to God for the good they do,
but this does not prove that they are able to do good without God. It only
proves that they are robbing God of the glory that He is due when they do
good. The Bible provides examples of lost people refusing to give glory to
God for their accomplishments. They are held accountable by God for
failing to give Him the glory for what they do (Acts 12:23). Likewise, Jesus
acknowledges that evil people can do (relatively) good things (Mt 7:11). For
that matter, unsaved people, like Cornelius (Acts 11:14), can do good things
that even God likes—relatively speaking (Acts 10:1-35).

Phenomenally Good
       Not only can lost people do things that are morally good, they can do
things that are phenomenally good. Casting out demons is not only morally
good, it is phenomenally good, yet even lost people can ostensibly perform
this phenomenal good of casting out demons (Mt 7:22). This phenomenon is
amazing in that one would normally expect that only the saved could
perform this action (Mt 12:26-28; Mk 16:17; Lk 11:18; Acts 19:11-16). The
authority given by Jesus to the 70 plausibly extended to Judas who also cast
out demons (Lk 10:17). Certainly, the authority conveyed by the Lord on the
twelve disciples to cast out demons and to perform healings applied to Judas
(Mt 10:1-4). As is commonly acknowledged, if all the other disciples but
Judas had been able to perform such miracles, then the genuineness of
Judas’ discipleship would have been suspect from the beginning. Because
his discipleship was not suspect by the other disciples, Judas must have been
able to perform these miracles as well—by the authority of Jesus.10
       What should be concluded, therefore, from such phenomenal displays
of God’s power by nominal believers is that goats can produce wool—and
lots of it—by God’s enabling grace. The goats in Mt 7:22 performed many
(authentic) miracles in the name of Jesus. The genuineness of their wool is
admitted by proveitists in that these wolves in sheep clothing actually did
perform the good works they claimed to perform. Consequently, proveitism
is proven false. Good works do not prove if a person is saved.
                          Chapter 5.
                     Inherit the Kingdom
       The initial blessing pronounced upon the righteous sheep is that they
inherit the kingdom: “Then the King will say to those on His right, ‘Come,
you who are blessed of My Father, inherit the kingdom prepared for you
from the foundation of the world’” (Mt 25:34). These sheep explicitly are
identified as the righteous (Mt 25:37,46). The composite picture, then, is that
the righteous inherit the kingdom. The righteous inherit the kingdom
because of their practical righteousness, as Jesus immediately demonstrates:
“For (gar) I was hungry, and you gave Me something to eat” (Mt 25:35).
       One must not only be a sheep; one must also act like a sheep in order
to qualify for the inheritance. Being a sheep is the first requirement;
behaving like a sheep is the second requirement. Or expressed in theological
terms, imputational righteousness must be met with ethical righteousness in
order for sheep to qualify for the acquisition of the kingdom. The reason
practical righteousness is necessary is in order to inherit the kingdom, not in
order to prove that one is a sheep. Whether or not one is a sheep is
determined by the separation, not by the judgment that ensues.

Unrighteous
       Could a person be imputationally righteous yet fail to be practically
righteous? Yes. In this parable, Jesus leaves this possibility open by placing
the judgment (based on practical righteousness) after the separation (based
on imputational righteousness). However, in the context (by contrasting the
outcome in the previous three parables for those believers who were
imputationally righteous yet practically disobedient), the Lord confirms that
one may be imputationally righteous yet fail to be practically righteous.
Sheep who misbehave do not inherit the kingdom.
       Further collaboration comes from Paul. One can almost hear him
asking the Corinthian Christians, “Are you not familiar with the parable of
the sheep and the goats?” when he marvels, “Do you not know that the
unrighteous (adikos) will not inherit the kingdom of God?” Within the same
verse, he immediately proceeds to warn these sheep:
      9
        Do not be deceived. Neither fornicators, nor idolaters, nor
      adulterers, nor homosexuals, nor sodomites, 10 nor thieves, nor
      covetous, nor drunkards, nor revilers, nor extortioners will
      inherit the kingdom of God. 11 And such were some of you.
      But you were washed, but you were sanctified, but you were
      justified (i.e., declared righteous, dikaioo) in the name of the
      Lord Jesus and by the Spirit of our God. (1Cor 6:9-11)
Page 46                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
       Paul had just adjudged that some of these righteous believers were
behaving unrighteously (adikeo, 1Cor 6:8) before reminding them that the
unrighteous will not inherit the kingdom. These imputationally righteous
sheep were behaving unrighteously. These righteous believers, who were
behaving unrighteously, are warned that those who behave unrighteously
(adikos) will not inherit the kingdom.
       Paul then warns, “You yourselves behave unrighteously (adikeo) and
defraud, and you do these things to your brethren! Do you not know that
those behaving unrighteously (adikos) will not inherit the kingdom of God?”
(1Cor 6:8-9a; TM).11 Paul is making application to the sheep in Corinth of
the principle laid down in the parable of the sheep and the goats by Jesus.
       As indicated by the context of Jesus’ parable and as picked up upon
by Paul, this parable has application for believers: Sheep who act like goats
will not inherit the kingdom. Paul reiterates this same point when addressing
the believers in the church at Galatia: “I forewarn you just as I have
forewarned you that those who practice such things shall not inherit the
kingdom of God” (Gal 5:21). Those believers who are unrighteous in their
behavior will not inherit the kingdom. Unrighteous sheep are included in this
exclusion from kingdom inheritance. Kingdom inheritance, not entrance, is
at stake.
       Jesus parabolically differentiates the sheep from the goats with a
verb, specifying that the shepherd will separate (aphorizo) the sheep from
the goats. At the point of the separation within the parable, we know nothing
about the character of these theological animals. Separation is based on their
nature, not on their performance. The separation is made because of what
they are, not because of the color of their fur or wool.
                         Illustration 23. Separation
Chapter 5. Inherit the Kingdom                                          Page 47

        The Lord next—then (tote)—passes judgment on their performance:
what they do and what they do not do. Separation precedes evaluation. As a
result, as the parable unfolds, in comparing apples with apples (in terms of
whether or not they help His needy brethren), one discovers that the Lord
has contrasted gray goats with white sheep.
                       Illustration 24. Then Evaluation




       These goats are not sent to hell because they persecute Christians or
because they take the mark of the beast. Such black deeds are not under
review in this parable. The charge brought against these goats is simply that
they did not help the Lord’s needy brethren. These particular goats are not
charged with persecuting the Lord’s brethren. Sins of omission are
exclusively in view. These sheep, in contrast, help the Lord’s brethren. They
are morally white by contrast.

Black Sheep
       Just as the Lord makes a categorical separation with the verb separate
(aphorizo), Paul does so grammatically with the Greek article the (hoi). At
the beginning of the Corinthian context in 1Cor 6:1, Paul categorically refers
to the lost as the unrighteous (hoi adikoi). In this context, Paul uses this
articular expression to refer to the identity of the lost as a class of people in
differentiation to the saints (hoi hagioi) as a separate class of people
Page 48                                        Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
            *
(1Cor 6:2). He then uses the verbal form of behaving unrighteously
(adikeo) to assert that some of the Corinthian sheep are behaving
unrighteously. Whereas the list of sins Jesus pictures is composed of sins of
omission, Paul’s vice list consists of sins of comission. By characteristically
lumping these vice-prone sheep in with black goats, Paul makes a
comparison using the black sheep within the Christian family.
                    Illustration 25. Hoi Adikoi versus Adikoi




    •     Hoi adikoi—categorically unrighteous
    •     Adikoi—characteristically unrighteous

      Paul uses an anarthrous† term (adikoi without the article) for
unrighteous in 1Cor 6:9 to refer to the black character of those described by
the vice list and warns that those so characterized will not inherit the
kingdom—even if they are sheep. Paul is pointing out, most strongly, that
those who categorically are righteous will not inherit the kingdom if they

*
  In 1Cor 6:2, Paul does not say that all the saints will judge the world, only that
the judgment of the world is limited to those who are classified as saints.
Context clarifies that only saintly saints will judge the world.
†
  Anarthrous is a term of Greek grammar which means that the word in question
does not have a Greek article. When the Greek article (for the) is missing, the
word is said to be anarthrous. If the word had an article, it would be called an
articular expression. For example, the dog is an articular expression in English
because, in this instance, the word dog has the English article the in front of it.
Articular then is the term in Greek grammar which means that a word has the
Greek article. Articular constructions can be used to classify in terms of identity
in contrast to anarthrous terms that indicate behavior.
Chapter 5. Inherit the Kingdom                                        Page 49

characteristically are unrighteous. In other words, the unrighteous righteous
will not inherit the kingdom. Those who are imputationally righteous will
not inherit the kingdom if they are practically unrighteous. Legal
righteousness needs experiential righteousness to qualify for kingdom
rulership.
        These black sheep used to be black goats before they were justified—
i.e. before they were declared legally righteous (1Cor 6:11). Now they are
no longer hoi adikoi (i.e., they are no longer classified as lost goats). But
these justified believers are still acting like adikoi (unrighteous) people and
thereby living in such a way that will disqualify them from inheriting the
kingdom. Paul provides a similar vice list in Galatians (5:19-21) and
likewise makes the same application and provides the same warning:
      19
         Now the deeds of the flesh are evident, which are: immorality,
      impurity, sensuality, 20idolatry, sorcery, enmities, strife,
      jealousy, outbursts of anger, disputes, dissensions, factions,
      21
         envying, drunkenness, carousing, and things like these, of
      which I forewarn you just as I have forewarned you that those
      who practice such things shall not inherit the kingdom of God.

        Immediately, Paul provides positive traits, known as the fruit of the
Spirit, as being characteristic of those who walk by the Spirit (Gal 5:22-25).
In like manner, Jesus makes positive performance necessary for His sheep to
inherit the kingdom and enter into life.
        Paul also makes positive performance necessary for reaping eternal
life (Gal 6:8). Naturally, one would deduce that Paul regards this positive
performance as good works that Christians must perform for the future
acquisition of eternal life as a reward. The meritorious nature of this
inheritance is spelled out by Paul for the sheep at Colossae:
      23
         Whatever you do, do your work heartily, as for the Lord
      rather than for men; 24 knowing that from the Lord you will
      receive the reward of the inheritance. It is the Lord Christ
      whom you serve. 25 For he who does wrong will receive the
      consequences of the wrong which he has done, and that without
      partiality. (Col 3:23-25)

Reward of the Inheritance
       The inheritance of the kingdom is a reward based on one’s work.
Jesus conditions the inheritance of the kingdom on the sheep doing such
things as giving His brethren something to drink (potizo, Mt 25:35). The
Lord already had made it clear that this inheritance is a reward for faithful
believers. In Mt 5:5, He promised inheriting the earth as a reward for
Page 50                                       Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
meekness. In the account of the rich ruler, Jesus made it clear that inheriting
eternal life (cf. Mt 25:34,46) requires sacrifice (Mt 19:16,21,28-29).* The
benefit that is granted is a reward—even when all that is done to earn that
reward is the simple task of giving a cup of cold water to Jesus’ prophets:
      40
        He who receives you receives Me, and he who receives Me
      receives Him who sent Me. 41 He who receives a prophet in the
      name of a prophet shall receive a prophet’s reward (misthos);
      and he who receives a righteous man in the name of a righteous
      man shall receive a righteous man’s reward (misthos). 42 And
      whoever in the name of a disciple gives to one of these little
      ones even a cup of cold water to drink (potizo), truly I say to
      you he shall not lose his reward (misthos). (Mt 10:40-42)

       The inheritance is a reward, and rewards are based on works.
Therefore, the actions performed in order to inherit the kingdom are works.
Paul does not hesitate to condition the reward of the inheritance on works.
Moreover, he makes no bones about the fact that he regards things based on
works to be owed: “Now to the one who works, his reward (misthos) is not
reckoned as a gift, but as what is owed” (Rom 4:4; TM).
       Paul knows the difference between a gift and a reward. The latter is
based on works; the former is not. Despite (1) the obvious nature of the
deductions derived from Jesus’ parables and promises, (2) the lucidity of
Paul’s explicit statements about the inheritance being given as a reward
based on works, and (3) the clear differentiation between a gift and a reward,
proveitists recoil at the mention of merit and shriek in horror at the thought
of God owing the kingdom to those believers who work for it. Proveitist are
desperate to deny any thought that the inheritance is a merited reward.


*
  Mt 19:16 is difficult to chart. See Illustration 26. Into Life (Eternal), 56. For
one thing, Matthew records the rich young ruler as asking what he must do to
obtain eternal life, but Mark and Luke record him as inquiring as to what he
must do to inherit eternal life (Mk 10:17; Lk 18:18). The working theory
adopted herein is that Mark and Luke have stated what the ruler said while
Matthew has stated what the ruler meant. Although the ruler actually asked
what he must do to inherit eternal life (Mk 10:17; Lk 18:18), he assumed that
the same requirement would be necessary to have eternal life (Mt 19:16). In his
mind there was no difference between having and inheriting eternal life since the
only perception he had of eternal life was that of merit. Jesus answered both
what the ruler meant (Mt 19:17) and what he said (Mt 19:21, cp. 16:27).
Alternatively, the rich young ruler conceivably could be inquiring what he must
do to inherit the kingdom as a king, and Jesus may be answering him
accordingly. This latter alternative is marked in gray in Illustration 26, (p. 56).
Chapter 5. Inherit the Kingdom                                       Page 51

Unmerited or Merited
       So proveitists will jump out of the Gospel of Matthew, run for shelter
into the Gospel of Luke, and exclaim that believers are unworthy of the
kingdom. Proveitists thereby attempt to refute any declaration that the
inheritance is merited. Sure enough, Jesus does relate this parable in Luke:
      7
        Which of you, having a slave plowing or tending sheep, will
      say to him when he has come in from the field, “Come
      immediately and sit down to eat”? 8 "But will he not say to him,
      “Prepare something for me to eat, and properly clothe yourself
      and serve me while I eat and drink; and afterward you may eat
      and drink”? 9 He does not thank (charis) the slave because he
      did the things which were commanded, does he? 10 So you too,
      when you do all the things which are commanded you, say,
      “We are unworthy (achreios) slaves; we have done only that
      which we ought to have done.” (Lk 17:7-10; TM)

      Thanks
       The proveitist exclamation will not withstand biblical examination,
however. For one thing, even within the Gospel of Luke, Jesus teaches that
believers can earn thanks (charis) as a reward/payment:
      32
         And if you love those who love you, what thanks (charis) is
      that to you? For even sinners love those who love them. 33 And
      if you do good to those who do good to you, what thanks
      (charis) is that to you? For even sinners do the same. 34 And if
      you lend to those from whom you expect to receive, what
      thanks (charis) is that to you? Even sinners lend to sinners, in
      order to receive back the same amount. 35 But love your
      enemies, and do good, and lend, expecting nothing in return;
      and your reward (misthos) will be great, and you will be sons of
      the Most High; for He Himself is kind to ungrateful and evil
      men. (Lk 6:32-35; TM)

Jesus implicitly affirms that thanks can be earned as a reward. A comparison
with Matthew makes the equation between the two explicit in this context.

      Mt 5:46                Lk 6:32
If you love those who If you love those who
love you, what reward love you, what thanks
(misthos) have you?   (charis) is that to you?
Page 52                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep


       Matthew, Luke, and Jesus are all on the same page. Proveitists are the
ones out of harmony with the biblical record. For the record, the Bible is
clear that no one has first given (prodidomi) to God “that it might be paid
back (antapodidomi) to him again” (Rom 11:35).12 However, the Bible is
equally clear that the Lord does repay His giving servants. In fact, they “will
be repaid (antapodidomi) at the resurrection of the righteous.” (Lk 14:14).
       Simply, you cannot put God into a position where He must reward
you, thank you, or repay you as though you obligated Him apart from His
taking the initiative to obligate Himself originally and graciously apart from
your performance. In other words, although the Bible rejects the notion that
rewards can be earned by our initiative merit, it affirms that rewards may be
earned by our derivative merit. We can earn rewards because God has taken
the initiative in promising us rewards and in providing us with the means to
earn these rewards. Stated in technical terms, the Bible affirms condign
merit and congruent merit (as defined herein).*
       In nontechnical terms, James states that the Lord promised the
heirship of the kingdom to those believers who love Him (Jam 2:5). By
means of His promise, the Lord has obligated Himself to reward those
believers who lovingly serve Him. The Lord will bestow the inheritance of
the kingdom upon them. But there is no way that believers could have taken
the initiative and obligated the Lord to make this promise. Peter summarizes:
      19
         For this finds thanks (charis), if for the sake of conscience
      toward God a man bears up under sorrows when suffering
      unjustly. 20 For what credit is there if, when you sin and are
      harshly treated, you persevere (hupomeno) with patience? But
      if when you do what is right and suffer for it you patiently

*
  Condign merit—is used in reference to performance that incurs reward
because Christ won these rewards for us and contingently obligated Himself to
us by promising these rewards to us on the basis of our derivative moral merit.
Although the opportunity to earn the reward is not merited, the reward is
merited. In short, condign merit points to the obligatory nature of the
reward. Quantitatively, every reward is merited. Solely on the basis of God’s
gracious initiative, He makes it possible for us to earn rewards through His
enabling grace: grace-grace
                       merit -reward.
       Congruent merit pertains to rewards that graciously, yet proportionally,
outweigh any effort that we may have put into our character development or any
improvement actually made in our character as a result of that effort. In short,
congruent merit points to the excessiveness of the reward. Qualitatively, the
scope of the reward far exceeds what we deserve. As an expression of God’s
grace, although all rewards are based on merit, God rewards us with greater
benefits than we deserve: merit-gracious reward.
                                      works
Chapter 5. Inherit the Kingdom                                         Page 53

      persevere (hupomeno), this finds thanks (charis) with God.
      (1Pet 2:19-20; TM)*

        Those who persevere earn thanks as a result. Thanks is even equated
with credit (kleos: fame, glory) in this context. If you persevere on earth,
God will make you famous in heaven. God gives you a thank you by
exalting you: “God is opposed to the proud, but gives thanks [charis] to the
humble. Humble yourselves, therefore, under the mighty hand of God, that
He may exalt you at the proper time” (1Pet 5:5-6).
        Paul, likewise, states that God will praise such believers (1Cor 4:5).
Jesus Himself, in the Mt. Olivet Discourse no less, affirms that God will
give good servants a “Well done” (Mt 25:21,23) and make them rulers in
heaven. According to Jesus, God will honor such servants (Jn 12:26). In
short, those believers who persevere will be rich and famous in heaven.
        Those proveitists who jump to Lk 17:7-10 in a vain attempt to prove
that believers do not merit thanks are doing a grave injustice to the biblical
record. Without doubt, believers do not merit thanks apart from God’s
initiative. God is not obligated to make it possible for believers to merit
thanks. Nevertheless, God has graciously done so.
        One reason some people have such a difficult time with this
elementary observation is because the word charis, which has been
translated consistently above as thanks, is the Greek word for grace.
Popularly, grace is defined as God’s unmerited favor. Yet clearly it refers to
God’s merited favor in the above texts (and in other such related texts).
        Naturally, the question will be asked, “How can grace be earned as a
result of works yet result in salvation apart from works?” The answer is
simple, “Whether God’s favor is earned is dependent upon the context.”
Context is king. The key is to note whether grace is the result that is given or
the basis for what is given. Stated in these terms, grace means favor.
Whether that favor is merited or unmerited is determined by the context. In
the above contexts, it is merited.

       Unworthy
      Similar mistakes abound regarding the word unworthy. Granted, in the
Lukan parable, when correcting those who otherwise might take the concept
of merit and worthiness to unbiblical extremes, Jesus prescribes the proper
*
  Perseverance results in a reward: “Therefore, do not throw away your
confidence, which has a great reward. For you have need of perseverance
[hupomone], so that when you have done the will of God, you may receive what
was promised” (Heb 10:35-36; TM). The promised reward is conditioned on
perseverance. Conditionalists make heaven a reward, rather than a gift, by
conditioning heaven on perseverance.
Page 54                                       Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
attitude of the servants as saying, “We are unworthy (achreios) slaves”
(Lk 17:10). Notwithstanding, in trying to avoid an unhealthy extreme,
proveitists have gone off the deep end in the opposite direction by claiming
that those who persevere are unworthy of reward. According to Jesus,
however, unworthy (achreios) slaves are thrown into the outer darkness. Is
Jesus saying that all believers are unworthy and therefore thrown into the
outer darkness? Of course not! Nor is He implying that unworthy slaves who
are thrown into the outer darkness are unworthy of their punishment!
        Jesus, in particular, and the Bible, in general, on numerous occasions
affirms that believers may be worthy of their reward. For example, Jesus
acknowledges, “The laborer is worthy of his reward [misthos]” (Lk 10:7;
TM). The Lord makes being worthy of Him conditioned on the believer’s
performance (Mt 10:37-38). Those believers who are counted worthy of the
resurrection are considered sons of the resurrection (Lk 20:3-36).* The Lord
promises that those believers who overcome will walk with Him in white
garments because “they are worthy” (Rev 3:4). Paul prays that believers will
“walk in a manner worthy of the God” who calls them into His “kingdom
and glory” (1Thess 2:12). Paul further exhorts believers to persevere so that
they “may be considered worthy of the kingdom” (2Thess 1:5).
        Kingdom would be translated best as kingship in such passages. No
one enters the kingdom based on their personal worthiness. Contrastively,
inheriting the kingdom, entering the kingdom as a king, or entering into the
kingship of the kingdom are synonymous expressions based on personal
worthiness. As noted in the parable of the sheep and the goats, the sheep
inherit the kingdom because of what they have given to Jesus, not simply on
the basis of what Jesus has given them. Therefore, they rule the kingdom.
        Jesus previously had given them eternal life as a gift, which thereby
made them sheep and resulted in their being separated from the goats. These
sheep subsequently inherit the kingdom because of what they did after
becoming sheep. After Jesus gave them living water, they gave Him a cup of
water. Jesus did not consider them meritoriously worthy of the living water.
He gave it to them independent of any performance on their part. The
inheritance of the kingdom though is granted because of their performance.
These sheep merit (i.e., are worthy of) the kingship. They could not take the
initiative in obligating Jesus to express thanks, much less to grant thanks that
results in co-kingship. Nevertheless, they could respond to Jesus’ promise
that any believer who receives a righteous prophet by giving the prophet a
cup of water would share in that prophet’s reward. By doing so, they would
obligate Jesus to fulfill the promise He made to them and to grant them such
a reward. They would derive merit by responding in the prescribed manner
to Jesus’ promise. Jesus Himself encourages them to do so.

*
    Such sonship would denote inheritance rights.
                             Chapter 6.
                          Into Eternal Life
        The opening promise Jesus makes in the Mt. Olivet Discourse is: “The
one who perseveres [hupomeno] to the end [telos] will be saved” (Mt 24:13;
TM). His benediction pronounced on those who do just that is just that: The
righteous will go away into eternal life (Mt 25:46). This closing declaration
is the fulfillment of the opening expectation (∴ saved = go into eternal life).
In this context, the righteous are those sheep who persevere (until the end of
the tribulation) and who thereby will be saved in terms of going into eternal
life (eis zoe aionios),* which, in turn, is equated with inheriting the kingdom
(cp. Mt 25:34). This final (end, telic) salvation is a future reward based on
the believer’s present performance and refers to the believer’s future
inheritance of the kingdom (also denoted as entrance into life).† In short,
believers who want this type of salvation will have to work for it.
        This form of salvation is not a gift. In the NT, sometimes salvation is
a gift; most of the time it is not. Context determines whether or not the form
of salvation in view is received apart from works or, in contradistinction,
obtained as a result of works. The same may be said about eternal life.
Sometimes it is a gift; sometimes it is a reward. Which dimension of
(eternal) life is in view is determined by the context.

Present Life
       Sometimes this life is identified explicitly as eternal life; sometimes it
may be referred to simply as life. Perhaps some securitists make too
thoroughgoing a distinction between entrance into life versus entrance into
life eternal by insisting that entrance into life must be distinguished from
entrance into eternal life. For example, in Acts 11:18 Luke records that God
had “granted to the Gentiles also the repentance that leads to life [eis zoe].”


*
  Literally, into life eternal.
†
  Other than the brief comments herein for Illustration 10 (p. 18), for a study
pertaining to salvation, see the author’s book, Salvation: A Categorical Analysis.
Unlike proveitists who contend that a final salvation attained by works refers to
going to heaven, securitists believe that a final salvation attained by works refers
to rewards in heaven. The present study will not pursue discussing final
salvation by works since this has been done elsewhere. Instead, it will be
content with an examination of the phrase entrance into life since this expression
is the dimension of reward explicitly mentioned in the parable of the sheep and
goats. Furthermore, the present study will not explore those texts dealing with
entrance into the possession of eternal life as a gift or as an enriched present
experience since such texts are dealt with in Salvation and The Outer Darkness.

*
  Soteriological (derived from soteria) is used in reference to salvation from
hell. Soteriological life therefore is freely received upon the condition of faith.
Misthological (derived from misthos) is used in reference to rewards.
Misthological life therefore is acquisitioned upon the condition of faithfulness.
Chapter 6. Into Eternal Life                                             Page 57

       Some securitists would contend that repentance in Acts 11:18 leads to
harmony with God and to an enriched experience of life during one’s time
on earth. This aspect of their analysis is correct, yet it is open to discussion
as to whether they are correct when they insist that Luke intends to limit the
scope of this statement to the present enrichment of one’s earthly life.
       Certainly, the concern expressed by such well-meaning securitists
(who seek to limit the scope of life to the present experience in Acts 11:18)
is valid. The gift of eternal life cannot be conditioned on changing one’s
performance (i.e., on repentance). On the other hand, Luke could be
referring to repentance as a helpful precondition (rather than as a theological
condition) for the gift of eternal life. If so, then the record in Acts is not
contradicting Paul’s emphasis that the gift of eternal life is not conditioned
on repentance (i.e., on changing one’s performance). If this is the case, Acts
may be alluding to the fact that repentance was helpful for the Gentiles
receiving the gift of (eternal) life (by faith). Additionally, Acts may be
alluding to the fact that repentance is necessary for the present experience of
eternal life, and results in the future acquisition of eternal life as a reward.
       Nevertheless, (1) in deference to those securitists who correctly stress
the present harmony with God that even the lost may achieve through
repentance, (2) in recognition that the full expression eis zoe aionios always
refers to life as a gift or as a future acquisition (never to a present experience
of life), (3) in acknowledgment that the abbreviated anarthrous expression
eis zoe never refers elsewhere to the gift of life, and (4) in view of the
observation that nowhere else in the NT does eis zoe refer to anything other
than the present experience of life, it might be concluded that the principle
(and perhaps exclusive) reference being made in Acts 11:18 is to a present
enriched experience of fellowship with God after all.*
        In a contrapositive temporal vein, some securitists would regard
Prov 11:18-19 as referring simply to the preservation of one’s physical life:
“The wicked earns deceptive wages, but he who sows righteousness gets a

*
   These statements are consistent with the manner in which other items are
categorized on the present chart. Statements number three and four are not
incontestable, however, in that 2Cor 2:16 could be charted as pertaining to other
dimensions of life. If so, then, assuming that the historical parallel behind the
triumph in 1Cor 2:14 is what is known as the Roman Triumph (i.e., a parade of
victory for the victorious Roman general), the argument could be made, for
example, that Paul and his fellow preachers were a fragrance (i.e., heralds) to
those who had the gift of life and who additionally shared experientially in that
triumph. They were marching to the Emperor’s bema to be awarded “an eternal
weight of glory far beyond all comparison” (2Cor 4:17). In this case, the future
misthological acquisition of life might be in view. Securitists have several valid
interpretations from which to choose for Acts 11:18.
Page 58                                       Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
true reward [misthos]. He who is steadfast in righteousness will attain to life,
and he who pursues evil will bring about his own death.” If the allusion is to
the temporal sphere, then what is reaped as the result of sown righteousness
is merely an enhanced quantity of earthly life.* Alternatively, other
securitists believe that experiencing the full quality of life is under
discussion and would cite abundant life in Jn 10:10 as a conceptual parallel.
This second interpretation is preferred in the chart for Acts 11:18. The fact
that such life is described as a reward only rules out the possibility of
considering this life a gift. The possibility of qualitatively obtaining such life
as a future (i.e., heavenly) reward (rather than merely as a temporal
extension or enrichment) also is entertained on the chart.

Acquisition of (Eternal) Life
       The two passages discussed above are charted as having potential
applicability to the future. For the purpose of the present study, the future
acquisition of (eternal) life is the principle subject of inquiry since this is the
dimension of eternal life awarded by Jesus to the sheep who aid His
brethren. Thus, the remainder of this discussion will be limited to those
remaining passages on the chart that deal with this future aspect. The
background for Jesus’ promise to the sheep goes back to the OT where the
identical phrase occurs in a similar context dealing with rewarded
performance—at the end of the tribulation no less!
      1
        Now at that time [“the end of time,”11:40, just before the end
       of the tribulation] Michael, the great prince who stands guard
       over the sons of your people [the Jews], will arise. And there
       will be a time of distress such as never occurred since there
       was a nation until that time; and at that time your people [the
       Jews], everyone who is found written in the book [of life],
       will be rescued. 2 And many of those who sleep in the dust of
       the ground will awake, some to eternal life, but others to
       disgrace and eternal shame. 3 And those who have insight
       will shine brightly like the brightness of the expanse of heaven,
       and those who instruct the many in righteousness, will shine
       like the stars forever and ever. (Dan 12:1-3; TM)

      At the end of the tribulation, many (not all) will be raised. Those who
have their names in the Book of Life will need to be raised so that they may

A judgment of believers is exclusively in view in this Danielic text. In this
particular OT context, the resurrection and attending judgment of Jewish
believers is exclusively in view. The black sheep among the Jewish believers
who are judged at this time will be submitted to eternal shame (not eternal
flame).

       The black sheep who are judged at the bema also will experience a
similar fate. The LXX word for shame (aischune) is the same word that
Jesus uses concerning believers who experience shame at His judgment
(Rev 3:18). The verbal form (aischuno) is used of believers being ashamed
when the Lord returns in 1Jn 2:28, and a compound of it is used of their
being put to shame in Mk 8:38. As for disgrace (oneidismos), Paul says
believers may fall into disgrace (1Tim 3:7).
       White sheep will awake and pass into eternal life as a result of the
judgment. In the parable of the sheep and the goats, Jesus holds forth the
same possibility of entering into eternal life to those Gentile believers who
help His brethren. These brethren who are helped would be Jewish believers,
specifically Jewish evangelists.* After all, Jesus already had promised as
much to those who help them: “He who receives a prophet in the name of a
prophet shall receive a prophet’s reward; and he who receives a righteous
man in the name of a righteous man shall receive a righteous man’s reward”
(Mt 10:41).

*
  Incidentally, the supposition that this is a general judgment that involves
everyone who ever lived—as represented by the sheep and goats—is reduced to
absurdity since the brethren are not a part of the company being judged. The
dispensational understanding is necessary to interpret this parable intelligibly.
Page 60                                              Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
        The Danielic implication is that those passing into eternal life already
have eternal life. The Jews raised at this time are raised because their names
are in the Book of Life. (Otherwise, they would not have been raised until
the end of the millennium with the rest of humanity.) If they are found
faithful, then they go into eternal life as a reward. For those who do not have
dispensational framework by which to work out such details, they can
observe the simple fact that the sheep in Jesus’ parable already have eternal
life before going into eternal life. They are sheep after all! Thus, they
already have a sheep (regenerate) nature. They already have received eternal
life as a gift before they enter into possession of eternal life as a reward.
        Most importantly, judgment does not confer the gift of eternal life
upon them. The delineation between those who have the gift of eternal life
and those who do not have the gift of eternal life is made in advance, either
by resurrection or separation. What is yet to be determined by judgment is if
those who already possess eternal life as a gift will also qualify to inherit it
as a reward. Those believers who qualify for this quality of eternal life will
have their names misthologically recorded as a result of the judgment.
        The OT presents this background for the victorious Jewish survivors:
“And it will come about that he who is left in Zion and remains in Jerusalem
will be called holy—everyone who is recorded for life in Jerusalem” (Is 4:3).
Some would call this the Book of Millennial Life. To be sure, it is a promise
of reward for Jewish believers who victoriously survive the tribulation and
who therefore are allowed to dwell in the earthly Jerusalem during the
millennial kingdom. Be that as it may, in view of several considerations, it
will be called the Book for Eternal Life in the present discussion.
        For one thing, Daniel supplies the appellation eternal (aionios) to the
quality of life that is bestowed upon those Jewish believers who emerge
victorious from the tribulation. So whereas both Daniel and Isaiah say that
the result is eis (to, into, for) zoe (life), Daniel extends the result into
eternity.* Thus, adding the Danielic appellation of eternal provides this
additional clarification.
        Additionally, Jesus opens up the possibility for Gentiles to have their
names recorded in such a misthological book. Furthermore, the Lord also
has made a similar promise to Christian overcomers living during the church
age in reference to the New Jerusalem (Rev 3:12). Therefore, for present
purposes, the Book for Eternal Life will be regarded as having at least three
chapters: one for Jewish tribulational white sheep, another for Gentile
tribulational white sheep, and another for Christian white sheep. This Book
for Eternal life is thus a misthological record of various classes of white
sheep from different dispensations. As a reward for their faithfulness, their
names are recorded in this misthological book.

*
    Eis life (Isaiah) = eis eternal life (Daniel).
Chapter 6. Into Eternal Life                                                  Page 61

       Most significantly, the soteriological Book of Life (explicitly
identified as such in Rev 20:12,15) is to be distinguished from this
composite misthological Book for Life.* In the NT, the Book of Life is a
record of those who (are foreknown to) possess the gift of eternal life
independently of their works. The Book for Life though will be a record of
those who come into possession of the reward of eternal life as a result of
their works. Only those who already have their names in the Book of Life
have the potential to have their names recorded in the Book for Life—
pending the results of their judgment.† In terms of logical order, one must
first come to faith and have one’s name in the Book of Life before one
potentially can qualify to have one’s name in the Book for Life as a result of
one’s faithfulness.‡

Soulical Life
        Soul winners, as they are popularly called (cp. Prov 11:30), are
currently laying up treasures (wages, rewards) for themselves in heaven.
This dynamic between increasing one’s future reward by the impact one is
having presently in this manner is expressed this way by John: “Already he
who reaps is receiving wages [misthos], and is gathering fruit for life eternal;
that he who sows and he who reaps may rejoice together” (Jn 4:36). Those
engaged in evangelism already are laying up future rewards for themselves
in heaven. These wages for eternal life have the potential to result in the
acquisition of eternal life.
        That being the case, saving souls is also a good way to save one’s
own soul: “He who loves his soul-life (psuche) loses it; and he who hates his
soul-life (psuche) in this world shall keep it for eternal life (zoe)” (Jn 12:25;

  Just as the Book of Life is really a Book of Eternal Life and yet designated by
its shorter title in Scripture, it will suffice to refer to the Book for Eternal Life by

misthological book could be referred to as the Book of Jerusalem Life
(encompassing the reward of citizenship in the capital cities of the earthly
Jerusalem and the Heavenly/New Jerusalem during the millennial kingdom and
on into the eternal state). Note that eis appropriately can be translated for (as
well as to, into, resulting in, etc).

      after Me, let him deny himself, and take up his cross daily, and

      Jesus will be ashamed of those sheep who are ashamed of Him.
