      24
        Then Jesus said to His disciples, “If anyone wishes to come
      after Me, let him deny himself, and take up his cross, and
      follow Me. 25 For whoever wishes to save his soul-life (psuche)
      shall lose it; but whoever loses his soul-life (psuche) for My
      sake shall find it. 26 For what will a man be profited, if he gains
      the whole world, and forfeits his soul-life (psuche)? Or what
      will a man give in exchange for his soul-life (psuche)? 27For
      the Son of Man is going to come in the glory of His Father with
      His angels; and will then repay every man according to his
      works.” (Mt 16:24-27; TM)

        The salvation of one’s soul (psuche) is conditioned on one’s daily
performance and is associated with being repaid according to one’s works.
The salvation of one’s soul-life (psuche) never refers to receiving the free
gift of eternal life (zoe); it never refers to salvation from eternal damnation.
In passages such as those above, salvation of the soul refers to the possibility
of a believer saving his or her earthly life in terms of heavenly rewards. A
richer experience of eternal life in heaven is at stake. The future acquisition
of eternal life as a reward is in jeopardy for believers regarding the salvation
of their earthly lives at the bema and is conditioned on daily practice.
       In the parable of the sheep and the goats, Jesus states that righteous
sheep go into eternal life. Practical righteousness is in view. Paul, likewise,
refers to God’s enabling grace as making experiential righteousness and its
attendant reward of eternal life possible: “Grace might reign through
Chapter 6. Into Eternal Life                                           Page 63

righteousness resulting in (eis) eternal life” (Rom 5:21; TM). In other words,
the wool of white sheep will result in eternal life as a future reward. This
reward is based on their wool (i.e., on their practical righteousness).
       As seen thus far, practical expressions of righteousness such as
helping Jewish evangelists during the tribulation, gathering evangelistic
results personally, and giving up one’s life in terms of daily sacrifices result
in eternal life. These are works that result in receiving eternal life as a
reward. The last passage on the chart, however, may puzzle those not well
acquainted with the doctrine of rewards. How can the mercy of the Lord
Jesus Christ resulting in eternal life in Jude 1:21 (TM) refer to something
earned by one’s performance? How can the Lord’s mercy resulting in the
acquisition of eternal life be attributable to human performance? Quite
nonchalantly, the verse is talking about mercy conditioned on the believer’s
behavior. This is a future mercy: a mercy not yet received. That is why
believers are anxiously to await it.
       Appallingly, some Christians are offended by the mention of earned
mercy. Yet Jesus promises: “Blessed are the merciful, for they shall receive
mercy” (Mt 5:7). Obviously, receiving such future mercy—at the time
rewards are dispensed in heaven (Mt 5:12)—is a reward. Clearly, this
misthological mercy is conditioned on showing mercy here and now on
earth. James expresses this truth straightforwardly in crystal-clear terms:
“For judgment will be merciless to one who has shown no mercy; mercy
triumphs over judgment” (Jam 2:13). The deduction to be derived from such
passages is that Christians who show no mercy will not attain mercy
resulting in eternal life. They will neither get this misthological mercy nor
its misthological attendant—eternal life (as a reward).
                  Illustration 28. Get Ready Bumper Sticker




      If James were to put the popular just forgiven sticker on one side of
his bumper, it stands to reason that he would counterbalance it by placing
the popular get ready slogan on the other side of his bumper, made
applicable to Christians, as this warning accurately expresses his theology:
      8
        You too be patient; strengthen your hearts, for the coming of
      the Lord is at hand. 9 Do not complain, brethren, against one
      another, that you yourselves may not be judged; behold, the
      Judge is standing right at the door. (Jam 5:8-9)
Page 64                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
                        Illustration 29. James’ Camel




       Even though James acknowledges that believers already have been
shown unearned mercy in the form of being given eternal life as a free gift
(Jam 1:17-18), they will not qualify for being shown additional future mercy
in the form of being awarded eternal life as a future acquisition if they do not
acquire it by showing mercy to others now. James is well aware that
Chapter 6. Into Eternal Life                                          Page 65

Christians might not be any more merciful than anyone else. Christians are
not always very Christian in showing mercy to others. Despite the fact that
Christians are forgiven, they are not let off the hook for being unmerciful or
unchristian in how they treat others.
      James didactically is reiterating a truth already presented parabolically
by Jesus. The Lord Jesus (James’ half brother) had addressed the possibility
of some believers being dealt with in a merciless manner at judgment
because they were unmerciful themselves:
         32
            Then summoning him, his lord said to him, “You wicked
         slave, I forgave you all that debt because you begged me.
         33
            Should you not also have had mercy on your fellow slave,
         even as I had mercy on you?” 34 And his lord, moved with
         anger, handed him over to the merciless jailor until he should
         repay all his debt. 35 So My heavenly Father also will do to
         you, if each of you does not forgive his brother from your
         heart. (Mt 18:32-35; TM)

       The issue here is how believing brethren treat one another.* Although
this will be shocking news to proveitists, at the judgment the Lord is going
to be very angry with some believers because of how unlovingly they have
treated their fellow believers. As Jesus eloquently testifies (and as Peter,
James, and Paul attest), black sheep exist—among the brethren—and these
black sheep are believing brethren.
       Just because black sheep are not the subject of discussion in the
contrast Jesus makes with the goats does not mean that all sheep are better
than all goats. Far from necessarily being better than everyone else, some
Christians are ruthless even in how they treat their believing brethren.
Consequently, not all those who possess eternal life will go away into eternal
life. Not all soteriological possessors will be misthological possessors. Jesus
limits His promise of this future acquisition of eternal life to those sheep
who are better than gray goats. The eternal life already possessed by the
sheep is not in jeopardy. Nevertheless, how their postconversion
performance will be dealt with at judgment (in regards to inheriting the
kingdom) is very much in question.

Life versus Death
       What happens to sheep who presently have eternal life but who do not
futuristically inherit eternal life? Life and death are opposites. Death is the
absence of life. The contrast between life and death is so natural that one can

*
    See Woolless Believers are Brethren, 37.
Page 66                                       Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
find explicit mention of death in at least one verse from each of the four
categories in Illustration 26. Into Life (Eternal) .

•   Physical life versus death: for life (eis zoe) is contrasted with for death
    (eis thanatos) in 2Sam 15:21; Prov 11:19.
•   Soteriological life versus death: into life (en ho zoe) is contrasted with
    spiritual death (thanatos) in Jn 5:24.
•   Experiential life versus death: into life (eis zoe) is contrasted with into
    death (eis thanatos) in Rom 7:10; 2Cor 2:16. Into life (eis ho zoe) is
    contrasted with in death (en ho thanatos) in 1Jn 3:14.
•   Misthological life versus death: into life eternal (eis zoe aionios) is
    contrasted with in the death (en ho thanatos) in Rom 5:21.*

        As is well known among securitists, black sheep are subject to
premature physical death as a result of their behavior and are prone to a
diminished experience of eternal life (i.e., to experiential death) in the
present. What is not equally well known, even among securitists, is that
black sheep are also subject to death in terms of rewards. The only area of
the chart that is assured unconditionally to believers is the possession of the
gift of eternal life.
        A long physical life is not ensured. A wonderful earthly life is not
assured. Most significantly, since the acquisition of eternal life is a reward,
this future acquisition of eternal life is not guaranteed unconditionally. For
this reason, securitists acknowledge that black sheep will not obtain the form
of eternal life denoted in the misthological column of the chart. Black sheep
will die in the sense of missing out on the life represented by the
misthological column. Those believers who fail to reap eternal life will die
in the sense of not having misthological life.
        A double indication of this type of death for black sheep already has
been provided. Black sheep lose their soul-lives (psuche) in terms of keeping
their soul-lives for eternal life (zoe). These sheep lose their psuche and miss
out on the acquisition of zoe. These two future forms of life are rolled into
one future reward concept, described by Jesus as inheriting the kingdom.
Conceptual parallels for believers experiencing death because of their poor
performance are well known (e.g., Rom 8:13; Gal 6:8; Jam 1:15).
        Some securitists limit the death in such passages to temporal death
(i.e., to premature physical death or to an experience of death during the
present). Nevertheless, even these securitists would acknowledge that such
believers can fail to acquire eternal life as a reward. The most natural
conclusion, then, would be to admit that failing to acquire eternal life as a

*
  Similarly, into righteousness (eis dikaiosune) is contrasted with into death (eis
thanatos) in Rom 6:16.
Chapter 6. Into Eternal Life                                            Page 67

reward (i.e., as an inheritance of the kingdom) could be considered a form of
misthological death that extends beyond the grave. Just as death is the
absence of life, misthological death is the absence of misthological life.
        On the other hand, some securitists—who correctly acknowledge
(1) that such death extends into the future beyond the grave and (2) that it
pertains to the acquisition of this life as a reward—attempt to limit this death
to an age-long death that only lasts for the one thousand years of the
millennium. This latter group of securitists often will follow up with the
suggestion that black sheep can be hurt by the second death for one thousand
years during the millennium.
        Whereas the securitists of the earlier group are to be faulted for not
following through on logical implications of the fact that the form of reward
that is lost is life, they at least would be consistent in acknowledging that the
life in question is eternal. They would affirm that aionios life means eternal
life in both the soteriological and misthological columns in Illustration 26.
Into Life (Eternal). Many in the latter group of securitists though would be
inclined to view aionios life as only meaning age-long life in the
misthological column. Their suggestion would be guilty of special pleading
by assuming that aionios life means a different quantity of life in the future
than it does in the present.
        The securitist proposal herein, in contradistinction, is that those sheep
who fail to reap eternal life as a reward will suffer eternal consequences in
terms of rewards. Aionios life is understood consistently to be eternal life in
both columns. Also, the logical implications of this interpretation are
embraced herein. Those believers who fail to acquire eternal life as a future
reward lose out on eternal life as a future reward for all eternity.
Misthological death is eternal. Just as rewards are eternal and just as the gift
of eternal life is eternal, so the failure to acquire the reward of eternal life
has eternal consequences.
                     Illustration 30. Go Into Eternal Life
Page 68                                      Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep


         Because going into eternal life is equated with inheriting the kingdom
(and thus with kingship), it may be pictured appropriately as a crown of life
(cp. Jam 1:12; Rev 2:10). Going into eternal life as a reward would be
roughly analogous to putting one’s head in the crown of life. The crown of
life is a misthologically superlative experience of eternal life.
         If someone held a lottery ticket and threw it away, only to find out
subsequently that it was the winning number, that person might well
exclaim, “I could just die!” Indeed they might view their remaining years,
comparatively speaking, as death—a diminished experience of life compared
to what they perceive that it could have been. In like fashion, the Bible
exhorts believers:
      35
         Therefore, do not throw away your confidence, which has a
      great reward. 36 For you have need of endurance, so that when
      you have done the will of God, you may receive what was
      promised. 37 For yet in a very little while, He who is coming
      will come, and will not delay. 38 But My righteous one shall
      live by faith; and if he shrinks back, My soul has no pleasure in
      him. 39 But we are not of those who shrink back to destruction,
      but of those who have faith to the preserving of the soul [i.e.,
      soul-life, psuche]. (Heb 10:35-39)

       The salvation of one’s soul-life in terms of rewards is at stake. The
loss of one’s soul-life would be a misthological form of soulical death. Such
death is not limited to temporal loss but also extends to eternal loss—in
terms of rewards. Do not throw this misthological opportunity away!
       By way of analogy, if you withdraw your investment from the stock
market because times are tough, you forever lose the compounding earnings
and interests (rewards) that you would have continued to earn if you had left
the money in the stock market. Your retirement might be permanently
impeded and seriously depleted. Likewise, withdrawing from serving the
Lord when times are difficult will have an eternal impact on the rewards you
could have had otherwise.
       The ramifications of throwing away one’s soul-life are eternal.
Rewards are eternal. The loss of rewards is also eternal. This reward is life.
Therefore, the loss of this reward is an eternal loss of life—eternal death—
misthologically speaking. Thankfully, the misthological loss of life can
never dip below the soteriological threshold.* Even so, an eternal loss of
eternal life (at the misthological level) is a place no believer will want to go.

*
 For discussion and illustration of this threshold, see Sheep and Goats: Four
Misthological Interpretations.
                   Chapter 7.
        From the Foundation of the World
       Jesus tells the white sheep: “Inherit the kingdom prepared for you
from the foundation of the world” (Mt 25:34). What about those woolless
and black sheep who fail to produce sufficient quantities of wool to qualify
for inheriting the kingdom? Did they not have an inheritance prepared for
them from the foundation of the world also? If not, then why do these
woolless sheep (whose names are written in the Book of Life from the
foundation of the world) not have an inheritance prepared for them from the
foundation of the world? On the other hand, if black sheep do have an
inheritance prepared for them from the foundation of the world, then why do
they not receive it?

Book of Life
        In the NT, those whose names are in the Book of Life have had their
names recorded there from the foundation of the world: “And all who dwell
on the earth will worship him, everyone whose name has not been written
from the foundation of the world in the book of life of the Lamb who has
been slain” (Rev 13:8). “Those who dwell on the earth will wonder, whose
name has not been written in the book of life from the foundation of the
world” (Rev 17:8). A corollary to this statement is that those whose names
are not in the Book of Life were never recorded there. Names in the Book of
Life are either permanently absent or permanently present.14
        Consequently, the fact that this inheritance is from the foundation of
the world denotes the permanence of this reward just as the presence of
one’s name in the Book of Life from the foundation of the world denotes the
permanence of this gift (Rev 13:8). Whereas the latter provides security in
terms of entrance into heaven, the former describes security in terms of
rewards in heaven. Because the reward is the future acquisition of eternal
life, the inheritance itself must likewise be eternal. Rewards are eternal.
        The Book of Life nullifies an exclusively corporate view of election.
God already knows in advance those individuals who will believe. Their
names, and only their names, already are permanently recorded in the Book
of Life. God foreknows who will believe in Christ and chose to place them
in Christ. Particular predestination of each person in particular is implicitly
affirmed. The reason their names are in the Book of Life from (apo) the
foundation of the world (Rev 13:8; 17:8) is because they already were
chosen before (pro) that time period (Eph 1:4), even before (apo) the
beginning of time (2Tim 1:9). This latter text rules out foreseen works as the
basis for this particular selection. It does not rule out foreknowledge of
punctiliar faith as the basis for the selection.
Page 70                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
       The Book of Life is a record of those who (are foreknown to) possess
the gift of eternal life independently of their works. These names are
recorded from the foundation of the world. The Book of Life is distinguished
from the Books of Works.
      12
        And I saw the dead, the great and the small, standing before
      the throne, and books were opened [i.e., Books of Works]; and
      another book was opened, which is the Book of Life; and the
      dead were judged from the things which were written in the
      books [i.e., Books of Works], according to their deeds. 13 And
      the sea gave up the dead which were in it, and death and Hades
      gave up the dead which were in them; and they were judged,
      every one of them according to their deeds. 14 And death and
      Hades were thrown into the lake of fire. This is the second
      death, the lake of fire. 15 And if anyone’s name was not found
      written in the Book of Life, he was thrown into the lake of fire.
      (Rev 20:12-15)
                    Illustration 31. Sequence of the Books




Books of Works
      Two classes of books are observed readily in this classic passage
from Revelation. The Book of Life is in a class all by itself and is pictured in
the above illustration as being in a bookcase all by itself. According to the
context, judgment is not based on what is written in the Book of Life.
Judgment, as always, is based on works. Specifically, those being judged are
Chapter 7. From the Foundation of the World                           Page 71

judged from the things which were written in the books (i.e., Books of
Works). They are not judged from what is written in the Book of Life. They
are judged from their works written in the books (of works).
       What is critical to note is that the Book of Life is a record of those
who already have eternal life. At the time of judgment, the sheep already
have eternal life. They are already sheep. Separation is based on their nature,
not on their performance. Their names already are recorded in the Book of
Life (as possessing eternal life). To be sure, their names already were
recorded in this book from the foundation of the world. And they did not
come into actual possession of the gift of eternal life until the time they
believed. Nevertheless, they already have eternal life, as a gift, before the
judgment commences. The purpose of the judgment is not to confirm that
their names are in the Book of Life or to determine if they already have
eternal life. That is a given.
       The Books of Works are not opened to find necessary evidence of
their names being in the Book of Life. When the biblical details of judgment
are examined fairly, it becomes apparent that the whole proveitist portrayal
of judgment is a betrayal of the actual nature of the judgment. Works do
have a very important role to play, nonetheless, even regarding those whose
names are already in the Book of Life. Future entrance into eternal life as a
reward is based on works. Those who have their names in the Book of Life
have the potential to enter into eternal life in terms of inheriting the
kingdom—if their postconversion works qualify them for such an entrance.

Book for Life
       Entrance of the believer’s name in the Book for Life is based on the
believer’s works and is a record of those for whom the kingship has been
prepared. Passages discussing the future acquisition of eternal life make it
clear that reaping eternal life as a reward is contingent upon the believer’s
performance and perseverance. One might hypothesize that just as their
names were written in the Book of Life from the foundation of the world
(because of their foreseen coming to faith), so their names were recorded in
the Book for Life from the foundation of the world (because of their
foreseen perseverance in the faith).
       After all, the Book for Life is a record of those who will inherit the
kingdom prepared (hetoimazo) for them from the foundation of the world.
Some securitists suggest that this preparation is merely corporate for
believers as a class. If so, then their individual names would not be recorded
beforehand. If such is the case, then names would only be recorded in the
Book for Life after the judgment. On the other hand, Jesus’ statement in
Mt 20:23 might be taken to suggest that particular positions of reward
already have been prepared: “To sit on My right and on My left, this is not
Mine to give, but it is for those for whom it has been prepared [hetoimazo]
Page 72                                      Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
by My Father” (Mt 20:23). While an exclusively cooperate view regarding
the Book for Life is not impossible for the securitist position, neither is it
necessary.
        Some proveitists, however, counter that these particular people have
been chosen in advance by God to inherit the kingdom independent of their
works, so these individuals sovereignly are preselected by God to inherit the
kingdom before they work. Supposedly, the good works performed by the
sheep merely show that they are sheep (i.e., people preselected to inherit the
kingdom). The good they do is only evidence that God has preselected them.
Their preselection (i.e., election) is not conditioned on their works. Their
wool simply is evidence that they are sheep, not a condition for being a
sheep. The wool is the fruit rather than the root (so to speak) of their being a
sheep. Reputedly, their election is not based on their performance.
        Such a convoluted proveitistic view is confounded by the fact that
Jesus uses gar (for) to prove that their sheepish performance provides the
reason these sheep inherit the kingdom. They inherit the kingdom because
of the good they do. Just as faith is the condition for initial salvation (even
though those who come to faith certainly have their names written in
advance in the Book of Life), so good works are the condition for final
salvation in terms of rewards (even though those who persevere in faith
probably have their names written in advance in the Book for Life*).
        Even if the names of faithful believers are recorded in advance in the
Book for Life, this does not limit the securitist option to particular mistholic
election. More importantly, particular election provides no grounds for the
proveitist opinion that inheritance is not conditioned on performance. The
logical sequence suggested in Illustration 31. Sequence of the Books still
would be correct. The names of the sheep (all the sheep) are entered in the
Book of Life from the foundation of the world because they (will) believe.
In real time, they subsequently believe. And after their conversion, their
postconversion works count toward their inheriting the kingdom. Their
names as kings of the kingdom (i.e., as heirs of the kingdom) are written in
the Book for Life because of their postconversion performance.
        The logical sequence is that (1) their names are written in the Book of
Life beforehand because, in time, they will come into possession of the gift
of life by believing in Jesus for eternal life. (2) After coming into possession
of the gift of life, they have opportunity to work for the acquisition of the
reward of life. Their works after that point in time are recorded in the Books
of Works ahead of time. (3) At judgment those relatively few believers who


*
  The working hypothesis being entertained at this point in the discussion is that
the individual names of faithful believers are recorded in the Book for Life from
the foundation of the world.
Chapter 7. From the Foundation of the World                             Page 73

qualify for this quality of eternal life will actualize* their inheritance. God
foreknows who will qualify and has recorded their names in advance. He
knows so well, in fact, that He is already preparing an appropriate place for
them.
        In terms of logical sequence, their names are recorded in the Book of
Life before their names are recorded in the Book for Life because the
inclusion of their names in the latter book is conditioned on what they do
after their names are recorded in the former book: Book of Life then Book
for Life. In-between these books, their postconversional works logically
must be taken into account between the inclusion of their names in the
former and latter book. Hence, the full sequence would be: (1) Book of Life,
then (2) Books of Works, and then (3) Book for Life.
               Illustration 32. More Names in the Book of Life




        In summary, their names are written in the Book of Life because they
believe. Next, their works are written in the Books of Works because they
must have their postconversional works judged.† Last, their names are
written in the Book for Life because they persevere—if they persevere.
        More names will be written in the Book of Life than in the Book for
Life because not all names written in the former will qualify for the latter.
Only those believers who persevere will qualify for this additional quality of
life.‡ The Book of Life is a record of those who (logically and actually)
already have the gift of eternal life before the judgment starts. The Book for
Life is a record of those who do not yet have eternal life as a reward before
their works are taken into consideration.

*
  Just as the sheep did not come into actual possession of the gift of life until
they believed, so the faithful sheep do not come into actual possession of the
reward of life until they are judged. They go away into life at that time.
†
  The works of believers previous to their becoming believers will not be in the
Books of Works.
‡
  For example, a Book of NFL Players would be much larger than a Book of
Hall of Fame NFL Players. One must have one’s name in the former to qualify
potentially (on the basis of one’s performance) for the latter.
Page 74                                      Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep

Prepared for You
       The working model above is quite content with the hypothesis that the
names recorded in both the Book of Life and in the Book for Life have been
recorded therein from the foundation of the world. An alternative model,
however, will be entertained to demonstrate that misthology is not dependent
upon this preferred working model. Although the kingdom has been
prepared for the faithful from the foundation of the world, perhaps it might
not yet have been set in stone as to whom those particular believers will be
who inherit the prepared positions of rulership. Even though Jesus speaks of
the place as having been prepared from the foundation of the world, He also
speaks of the preparation as yet to be completed:
      2
        In My Father's house are many dwelling places (mone); if it
      were not so, I would have told you; for I go to prepare
      (hetoimazo) a place for you. 3 And if I go and prepare
      (hetoimazo) a place for you, I will come again, and receive you
      to Myself; that where I am, there you may be also. (Jn 14:2-3)

       Why did Jesus tell his disciples that the reason He could not give
them positions on His left and right hand was because such positions were
for those for whom they already had been prepared and then tell them that
He was going to prepare a place for them? Is the preparation already
completed or not? A more dynamic model might suggest that Jesus cannot
give away such exalted positions freely because He already has obligated
Himself contingently. These positions are reserved for those for whom they
are prepared, and they are prepared for those who prove to be the most
qualified contestants. The identity of those particular individuals is not yet
set in stone. Nevertheless, the previous model can respond that the names
are not set in stone in terms of contingency, only in terms of certainty, just as
the Book of Life already has the names of those who will believe.

       Reserved
        All believers have an “inheritance which is imperishable and
undefiled and will not fade away, reserved in heaven for” them—corporately
as a class and potentially as individuals (1Pet 1:4). What is reserved for them
is an inheritance in heaven, not an inheritance of heaven. In other words,
rewards in heaven, not the reward of heaven (i.e., not heaven as a reward), is
the subject under discussion in 1Pet 1:4.
        God has not made a mistake in making the reservation for all
believers. Some securitists, however, have made a mistake in thinking that
just because a potential reward is eternal it must be unconditional in its final
realization so that all believers will realize this potential inheritance. To the
contrary, the reward is eternal; the reservation is not. Because the realization
Chapter 7. From the Foundation of the World                              Page 75

of the reservation is contingent, the reservation itself is not eternal for those
individuals who have not yet come into the realization of the reward. Once a
believer is granted the reward, the reward is no longer contingent. Until then,
however, the reservation is not eternal for the individual in question in that it
is not yet eternal for those who have not come into the realization of that
reservation. The reservation of the inheritance for all believers as a class
does not guarantee that every individual within that class will achieve that
inheritance.
       Just as a school may secure diplomas in advance for all students
within a graduating class and yet disqualify some of those students from
receiving a diploma, so God may disqualify some of the potential candidates
from receiving their potential reward if their faithfulness to His standard is
found substandard. Even so, once the diploma/reward has been awarded, it is
never rescinded.15

       Citizenship
        All believers are citizens of heaven in that their citizenship is in
heaven: “For our citizenship is in heaven, from which also we eagerly wait
for a Savior, the Lord Jesus Christ” (Phil 3:20). On the other hand, citizens
do not necessarily own the place. Those found qualified at the bema for the
inheritance of the kingdom, in contrast, actually inherit the kingdom
(Mt 25:34; 1Cor 6:9-10; Gal 5:21). They are heirs of the kingdom, not
merely citizens of the kingdom. These heirs rule the kingdom. Obviously,
the parallelism between citizenship and inheritance is not absolute. Not all
citizens of a kingdom are rulers within that kingdom.
        Nevertheless, a duality pertaining to citizenship does apply to the
present discussion. When about to face a flogging for preaching the gospel,
Paul pointed out that he was a Roman citizen by birth (Acts 22:25-29). In the
chapter previous to that one, Paul appealed to this citizenship as a citizen of
Tarsus for the right to speak: “Paul said, ‘I am a Jew of Tarsus in Cilicia, a
citizen of no insignificant city; and I beg you, allow me to speak to the
people’” (Acts 21:39). Paul spoke both of his Roman citizenship and his city
citizenship. The Bible likewise indicates a distinction between being a
citizen of heaven versus being a citizen of Jerusalem. Not all believers will
be found worthy to qualify for citizenship in the reward city—Jerusalem.
       The heavenly city is prepared (as a reward) for those of whom God is
not ashamed to be called their God. In fact, God “has prepared [hetoimazo]
a city for them” (Heb 11:16). Consequently, there is no reason to think that
this city is prepared for those believers of whom the Lord will be ashamed
(Mk 8:38; Lk 9:26; 1Jn 2:28). Certainly, their citizenship will not be in that
city. They will not live in that city. Just as sitting at Jesus’ right and left is
reserved for those for whom it is prepared, so occupying a place with Jesus
in that city is reserved for those for whom it has been prepared.
Page 76                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
       The biblical imagery certainly is suggestive of this exclusive
citizenship: “Then I, John, saw the holy city, New Jerusalem, coming down
out of heaven from God, prepared [hetoimazo] as a bride adorned for her
husband” (Rev 21:2; NKJ). Heavenly citizenship is a gift. City citizenship
(in the New Jerusalem) is a reward prepared for those believers who prepare
themselves for it. Wise servants prepare (hetoimazo) themselves (Lk 12:47).
Those saints who are citizens of the bridal city prepare (hetoimazo)
themselves (Rev 19:7).16
       To be sure, “eye has not seen and ear has not heard…all that God has
prepared [hetoimazo] for those who love Him” (1Cor 2:9). The problem is
that not all believers love God. Paul’s promised preparation does not apply
necessarily to those believers who do not love God. Jesus seems to limit His
preparations in Jn 14:2-3 to those believers who do love God, when He
expounds upon these preparations: “If anyone loves Me, he will keep My
word; and My Father will love him, and We will come to him, and make Our
dwelling place [mone] with him” (Jn 14:23; TM). Apparently, those
dwelling places currently being prepared by Jesus are for those believers
who love God.17
       An individualization of the collective preparation is suggested by
Jesus in Jn 14:2-3. When Jesus finishes preparing that place for His Bride,
His Father will send Him back to get His Bride. Possibly, the reason Jesus
has not yet returned is because He is still preparing the bridal city for the
bridal saints. Perhaps the Father has a set number of occupants in mind to
share Christ’s throne as His Bride.* If so, then the Father would wait and
send the Son when the number of names in the chapter from the Book for
Life dealing with the church-age saints matches this predetermined number.
       Whether or not these names are written in advance or are simply
known in advance makes no substantial difference. Even if the Lord has not
recorded them in advance, He knows the names in advance. Yet there is
evidence to suggest that those names are recorded in advance (Heb 12:23;
cp. Is 4:3). Still, for sake of completeness, the alternate proposal also is
entertained herein that the names in the Book for Life have not been
recorded yet but await registration upon completion of the evaluation.
       The names of those in the Book of Life are recorded from the
foundation of the world. Even if this explicit verification is missing
regarding the Books of Works and the Book for Life, to have one’s name
recorded in the Book for Life merely presupposes that one’s works and the
outcome of the judgment are known in advance. God foreknows these
details, so it is quite possible that He already has recorded them as well.18


*
 Some securitists make the interesting proposal that the final number of saints
who inherit the kingdom will match the number of those angels who fell.
                             Chapter 8.
                             Conclusion
Broken Links
        The proveitist interpretation of the parable of the sheep and goats can
be surmised as sheep ⇔ wool ⇔ sheepfold. Some might be inclined to look
at this as a three-link chain. Actually, it is five. As usual, a chain is only as
strong as its weakest link. The thesis of this book has been that this proveitist
chain is broken and that the wool is meritorious. A superficial assessment
would be that the argument employed herein is dependent upon proving the
meritorious nature of the wool. Not so. Further, it would be wrong to assume
that if this point of contention is proven regarding the meritorious nature of
the wool, it results in a singular break in the chain. Indeed, many proveitists
insist that the wool is not meritorious and thereby assume that their chain is
unbroken. In actuality, however, their chain is composed of multiple broken
links. Merit is only one such link. Even if the issue of merit were not brought
into consideration, the proveitist chain would still fall apart.

       First Link
       The first broken link in the proveitist interpretation is the assumption
that all sheep produce wool (sheep ⇒ wool). As demonstrated herein,
comparatively speaking, sheep may be woolless. For that matter, sheep may
be hairy instead of woolly.
                       Illustration 33. First Broken Link




Some sheep produce less wool than goats. Some sheep act like hairy goats.
Some sheep bare fangs like wolves. The proveitist supposition that all sheep
sufficiently manifest their true nature by the way they live so as to be
discerned as sheep by the way they live is demonstrably false.
Page 78                                   Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep

      Second Link
      The next broken link in the proveitist presumption is the belief that
wool is produced only by sheep (sheep ⇐ wool). To the contrary, goats and
wolves produce wool. To be sure, this synthetic wool is composed of grace
and legalism. Nonetheless, this blend of wool, for all outward appearances,
can pass for the real thing.
                    Illustration 34. Second Broken Link




Would-be wool critics would have to play God to try to pass themselves off
as reliable judges of what lays underneath the wool. Since God alone is
qualified to make such a judgment and since even God Himself does not
make His judgment regarding who is a sheep or a goat on such basis, no
reliable grounds exist for fallible humans pretending that they can do so.

      Third Link
       The third broken link in the proveitist chain of events is the
presumption that if you produce wool, you will be granted final salvation in
terms of being allowed into heaven. In other words, if you produce wool,
then you will be allowed into the heavenly sheepfold (wool ⇒ sheepfold).
                     Illustration 35. Third Broken Link




Even though this chain is broken by the breakdown in the previous two
links, it also ruptures under the strain of its own argument in that it
Chapter 8. Conclusion                                                Page 79

conditions soteric salvation on works—an anathemized gospel. Although
some proveitists try to avoid the conditional nature of the proveitist
interpretation, an exegesis of the proveitist proof texts makes it clear that
Jesus truly does condition the result on the believer’s performance. For
example, the sheep are given the inheritance and eternal life as a reward for
their performance. Their wool does not prove that the sheep are sheep.
Rather, the wool is the basis for the reward given to the sheep. Thus,
contrary to proveitism, being allowed into the sheepfold cannot be the
outcome for producing wool.

      Fourth Link
      A corollary of the proveitist presumption above is that if you are
allowed into the heavenly sheepfold, then you necessarily will have
produced wool. The only people allowed into proveitist heaven are those
who prove that they are sheep by the amount of wool they produce (wool ⇐
sheepfold).
                    Illustration 36. Fourth Broken Link




Wool results in reward. The Bible teaches that some believers will have their
works burnt up at the bema. Comparatively speaking, in terms of inheriting
the kingdom and being rewarded with eternal life, such believers will be
found woolless. Therefore, this proveitist link is groundless. Not everyone
saved from hell and allowed into the sheepfold of heaven will be found
woolly.

      Four Links
       When these links are joined together and represented mathematically,
the threefold result is: sheep ⇔ wool ⇔ sheepfold. But when broken apart,
four broken links are discerned, with each link having a reference to the
middle term of the mathematical expression. Wool is the common
denominator in each link.
Page 80                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep


                  Illustration 37. Four-Link Proveitist Chain




                          •    sheep ⇒ wool
                          •    sheep ⇐ wool
                          •    wool ⇒ sheepfold
                          •    wool ⇐ sheepfold

       Four Types of Legalism
        Various strategies are implemented by competing (and at times
offsetting) proveitists in trying to find a way to mend their broken chain.
Four legalistic attempts within the proveitist camp have been mentioned:

•   Claiming that postconversional wool is mere evidence of initial
    salvation rather than a condition for final salvation.
•   Claiming that initial salvation is by grace and that final salvation is by
    God-enabled wool.
•   Claiming that initial salvation is by grace and that final salvation is
    according to God-enabled postconversional wool.
•   Claiming that initial salvation is by God-enabled postconversional wool
    and that final salvation is by God-enabled postconversional wool.

       The first proveitist claim mentioned above is refuted by the remaining
three proveitist claims and by Jesus’ explanation as to the reason He is
giving the inheritance. The remaining three claims are nullified by the
demonstrably broken nature of the links in general and by a consideration of
merit in particular. Nevertheless, let it be noted that the broken nature of the
above four links, and thus of the proveitist argument in general, can be made
independently of a stringent examination of merit.

       Five Links
       Although a demonstration of merit actually is not necessary for a
rebuttal of proveitism, passing over it would be unwise since it is fatal to the
proveitistic scheme. Some proveitists attempt to prove that perseverance
does not involve any merit because the latter three legalistic claims condition
entrance into heaven on perseverance. Obviously, these proveitistic
Chapter 8. Conclusion                                                Page 81

arguments lead directly to the conclusion that heaven is merited—a
Protestant anathema.
        If proveitists must condition entrance into heaven on performance
(and they must, given their theological framework), then they wish to
disassociate this perseverance with any merit so that they will not be
perceived as teaching that heaven is merited. This link regarding merit is so
weak in the proveitist argument (yet so vitally necessary to their argument)
that it merits a place of its own in the proveitist chain.
                 Illustration 38. Five-Link Proveitist Chain




       Wool is the common denominator for each of the four primary links.
This wool must be nonmeritorious for the proveitist to have any hope of
avoiding teaching that heaven is a merited reward in proveitism. As noted,
proveitists invoke several schemes in trying to circumvent an admission of
merit on their part. These evasive maneuvers have not been hunted down in
the present discussion, however. Technically, not even merit has to be
proven in order to disprove proveitism. It suffices to note that the Bible
teaches that salvation from hell is by grace apart from works. Thus, final
salvation (in reference to hell) cannot be according to works. Whether or not
those works are meritorious is, in this respect, an auxiliary matter.
       This intuitive understanding finds confirmation when it is noted that
the key context dealing with the Book of Life clearly differentiates one’s
works from having one’s name recorded within the Book of Life. Having
one’s name in the Book of Life is a separate matter from being judged
according to works. What is given according to one’s works is a positive or
negative reward, not a gift. Because salvation from hell is a gift, such
salvation cannot be according to one’s works—contrary to proveitist claims
to the otherwise.
       Conversely, the parable of the sheep and goats certainly is open to a
securitist interpretation in that the separation between the sheep and goats
precedes judgment. The separation is not based on performance.
Confirmation that the securitist interpretation is correct comes quickly
enough from an examination of the context and from an inspection of the
broken arguments of those advocating the alternative—conditional security.
Page 82                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep

Besieging Links
       Positive evidence in favor of unconditional security advocated by
securitists has been presented herein by surveying various types of believers
depicted in the Scripture. Not all sheep are as white as those sheep dealt with
in the parable of the sheep and goats.

       Gray Sheep
                           Illustration 39. Same As




       Some believers act like unbelievers. Paul describes such believers as
carnal believers. A believer need not be the worst of the worst in order to fall
into this category. Jesus describes this type of potential carnality as the
hiding of one’s light instead of letting it shine (Mt 5:14-16). If one hides
one’s light, one appears lost even if one is saved. Such gray sheep look like
everyone else—lost.

       Black Sheep
                         Illustration 40. Worse Than
Chapter 8. Conclusion                                                    Page 83

       On the other hand, some Christians stand out from the rest—
negatively. These Christians act worse than the typical unbeliever.
Nonetheless, Paul does not regard these black-sheep Christians as
unbelievers. Indeed, he admonishes the rest of the flock to regard these black
sheep as fellow brethren (2Thess 3:14-15). These black sheep are part of the
Christian family. Additionally, whatever else might be said about the person
bearing the name Christian brother in 1Cor 5:11, the instructions Paul
provides are (at the very least) based on his working hypothesis that the
person in question might actually be a brother, even though this Christian
brother’s level of immorality is worse than that of the lost (1Cor 5:1).*

       White Sheep
                          Illustration 41. Better Than




*
  If the person in question in 1Cor 5:1 is a believer, then this verse proves that
proveitism is false. For in that case the way people live does not prove if they
are believers. Proveitists attempt to question whether this person was actually a
believer by asserting that he was merely a so-called brother. However, their
doing so is a case of special pleading in that the word in question (onomazo) is
not translated as so-called anywhere else in the NT. It should simply be
translated as “anyone named brother.” This Greek word is never used to cast
doubt on the reality of the designation anywhere else in the NT.
        Also, Paul’s logic would be self-defeating if this person were not
genuinely being considered a brother. Paul is giving orders as to how to deal
with immoral brothers rather than with immoral people in general. If this
person’s immoral behavior proved that this individual was not a brother, then he
would not be treated as a brother. He would be treated as an immoral person of
the world instead. Consequently, if he were an unbeliever, he would not be
subject to the shunning Paul is calling upon for immoral believers (1Cor 5:9-10).
Paul’s working premise must be that this black sheep is a believer.
Page 84                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep


        Naturally, Scripture has a number of white sheep. These sheep are
better than black goats, of course. They are also better than the gray goats, as
one would expect. But are white sheep better than the best-of-the-best goats?
In other words, are white sheep hands-down better than white goats in terms
of observable performance? Perhaps not. How good are the best-of-the-best
atheists? How good are the best-of-the-best moralists from other religions?
How good are the best-of-the-best legalists within Christendom?
        Are we simply to deny that white goats exist? Scripture does not
spend much time addressing this possibility, although the possibility is
hinted (Mt 23:27). Moreover, some texts suggest that even God’s white
sheep, at least on some occasions, are morally inferior to some goats.
        Abraham lied about his wife and was reproached by Pharaoh for
doing so (Gen 12:11-20). Neco (king of Egypt) was on a God-ordained
mission when Josiah (a white sheep) interfered. Josiah was killed as a result
(2Chron 35:20-27). Even aside from such occasional OT examples,
deduction from NT teachings would lead to the conclusion that all is not as it
appears when trying to judge if one is a sheep or a goat on the basis of one’s
performance. To confine the scope of this discussion to those who put their
hope in Christ, consider this thought provoking question: “Was Mother
Teresa necessarily saved?” Or if that is too specific, try this one: “Is every
professing Christian who lives a good life like Mother Teresa necessarily
saved?” No. Just because a person in some sense believes in Christ and lives
a good life, this does not prove that this person has eternal life.19

       Wolfish Sheep
                       Illustration 42. Legalistic Sheep
Chapter 8. Conclusion                                                   Page 85


        Legalism does not save. Those people who believe that their Christian
good works are necessary to enter heaven cannot be saved by that
perspective or by that performance. Even legalists will acknowledge that
legalists are not saved! Of course, legalists will try to excuse their particular
form of legalism and try to acquit themselves of legalism, but the principle
still remains that the saving efficacy of legalism is denied by many legalists.
Therefore, even from the legalistic perspective, it is highly inconsistent for
legalists to pretend that white wool proves regeneration when they
themselves admit that white wool does not necessarily prove regeneration.
Just as the Pharisees of Jesus’ day looked good on the outside, so Pharisaical
followers of Christ today might look great outwardly. The legalistic
contention that fruit proves root is refuted by legalism itself. The whole
notion that Jesus is teaching that performance proves profession is nullified
by the legalistic concession that legalism does not prove profession.
        On the other hand, some genuine believers have become legalists
after their conversion. Goats can became genuine sheep and then become
wolfish sheep. They remain sheep (i.e., children of God) in terms of their
identity, but they became wolves in terms of their theology. What will the
Lord do with these wolfish sheep? Will He allow them to inherit the
kingdom because they perform good deeds? Of course not. When the Lord
judges the works of believers, He is also going to judge their corresponding
motives (1Cor 4:5; 13:3). Poor motives can wipe out good performance at
the judgment. Legalistic sheep will not inherit the kingdom.

Surprise
        Some commentators make a considerable jump in logic to conclude
that since the sheep were surprised at having served the Lord, one should not
serve the Lord out of hope of reward or out of a desire for corresponding
misthological salvation. These interpreters even regard the ignorance of the
sheep regarding having performed any good works to be an admirable
(perhaps even necessary) attribute, proving that the sheep were not seeking
to merit a reward.
        More thoughtful exegetes acknowledge that Jesus’ promise of reward
to those who assist His prophets in Mt 10:40-42 provides the backdrop to the
point Jesus is making in the parable of the sheep and goats about helping His
brethren. Moreover, these sheep, gathered from the nations, will be familiar
with the gospel of the kingdom, preached to all the nations, which is that
those who persevere to the end will be saved (Mt 24:13-14). No virtue
should be attributed to any presumed ignorance of the Lord’s promise. Nor
are those commentators to be thought virtuous who impugn the Lord’s
character by suggesting that He is eliciting improper motives by promising
rewards and salvation in response to performance. The sheep are not
Page 86                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
commended for being ignorant of Jesus’ promise of rewards or for ignoring
His promise of salvation as a reward. Rather, their surprise is that so little
counts so much.
       The message of the parable of the sheep and goats is not that good
people go to heaven and bad people go to hell. Rather, it is that good sheep
inherit the kingdom (and misthologically enter into eternal life) as their
reward—a reward that is surprisingly disproportionate to their merit. Goats
get hell as their reward—a reward that is seemingly disproportionate to their
demerit. Congruent merit and demerit are pictured.*

Occam’s Razor
        Neither salvation nor the assurance of salvation (when that salvation
is referring to salvation from hell) is conditioned on the proof of one’s faith.
Salvation by faith is conditioned on faith, not on the proof of one’s faith.
The beauty of such simplicity is denied by proveitists.
                        Illustration 43.Occam’s Razor




       If Occam’s razor is invoked, then conditional security is cut asunder.
Biblically, the reward of eternal life (not the gift of eternal life) is
conditioned on the sheep’s performance. Proveitists will insist that this

*
  Had the Lord wished to picture strict merit, no surprise would have been
present. Not even condign merit would have occasioned such surprise. The
contractual labors in the vineyard, who contracted for a day’s wage for a day’s
labor, were not surprised that they were paid a day’s wage. Nor were they
surprised that the labors who only worked part of a day were paid. What
surprised the contractual workers was that the subsequent non-contractual
workers were paid as much as the contractual workers who worked the full day.
The contractual workers were shocked by the congruent payment given to the
non-contractual workers (Mt 20:1-16).
Chapter 8. Conclusion                                                     Page 87

performance is nonmeritorious—that this performance is only necessary
evidence of the fact that the professing believers in question really are sheep.
Proveitists even have the audacity to call what Jesus gives in response to that
performance a gift! Contrary to such proveitistic claims, Jesus labels what
He gives as a reward. The Lord explicitly labels that what He gives—in
response to those who give Him a cup of cold water (via assistance rendered
to His prophets)—a reward. Indeed, He makes a triple reference to this
reward in the space of two verses:
      41
         He who receives a prophet in the name of a prophet shall
      receive a prophet's reward; and he who receives a righteous
      man in the name of a righteous man shall receive a righteous
      man's reward. 42 And whoever in the name of a disciple gives
      to one of these little ones even a cup of cold water to drink,
      truly I say to you he shall not lose his reward (Mt 10:41-42).

        The subject matter is reward, not gift. What is promised in response
to this assistance is a reward. This passage, in turn, provides the backdrop to
the parable of the sheep and goats. Because rewards are based on works and
because the (so-called) evidence results in rewards, this evidence is
necessarily a work—meritorious work at that.
        Without a doubt, giving a prophet this type of assistance and helping
Jesus’ evangelistic brethren naturally indicate that those rendering such
assistance believe the evangelistic message. This natural relationship,
however, cannot be pressed unduly to an erroneous conclusion. They
believe, therefore, they help. This does not mean that all believers help;
rather, help is a natural indicator of belief. Those responding in the
prescribed manner do two different (yet related) things: believe and help.
They get two related (yet distinguishable) results: eternal life as a gift for
believing and eternal life as a reward for helping.
        Their helping the evangelists is natural evidence that they believe the
evangelists, and this natural evidence is a necessary requirement for the
additional benefit (i.e., the reward). Although the evidence is natural, it is
meritorious. The evidence upon which the reward is based and upon which
the judgment is pronounced is meritorious evidence, as is evidenced by the
fact that Jesus promises that such performance will result in a reward.
        In conclusion, the gift of eternal life is free; the reward of eternal life
is costly. This is not to say that the gift is a reward. Far to the contrary,
eternal life is being offered in two different capacities: as a gift and as a
reward (not as a gift-reward) and in that order: gift then reward. You can get
the gift without getting the reward. You cannot earn the reward without first
receiving the gift. The argument of those who try to make the gift a reward
is broken to pieces.
Page 88   Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
                               Appendix 1.
                       Broken Allegiances
        The criticisms made within this present book have not been limited to
the proveitist camp. Indeed, weaknesses in arguments advanced by fellow
securitists have been noted. Nevertheless, such weaknesses are not inherent
to the securitist position and are avoided easily by consistent securitists.
        Having demonstrated the consistency with which Jesus’ teaching on
the parable of the sheep and goats can be interpreted within the securitist
framework, and also having shown the futility of trying to interpret it from a
proveitist framework, a further critique now needs to be made regarding
those within the securitist camp who say, for all practical purposes, that
unconditional security does not really matter in terms of evangelism.

GES Webboard
        After having started this book, I became engaged in a dialog at the
GES Webboard* in which some participants were, yet again, questioning the
necessity of believing in unconditional security. This discussion has surfaced
a number of times over the many years of my participation in that board. The
position adopted in this book (in harmony with GES) is that a rudimentary
acceptance of unconditional security is necessary for faith to find saving
sufficiency.
        A counter article (written by a popular, dissenting securitist who
denies that unconditional security is a necessary component of saving faith)
was posted as a focal point of contention. Part of that article (and the ensuing
interaction on the GES Webboard) intersects the present discussion in that
those denying the necessity of security point out that many passages make
eternal life (and salvation for that matter) conditioned on performance.
Because many outside the securitist camp are confused by the fact that
eternal life is both a gift and a reward and since Scripture does not always
clearly differentiate between the quantitative and qualitative aspects of
eternal life, dissenting securitists make a counterclaim to the thesis made
herein. These securitists contend that unconditional security should not be
regarded as a subjective necessity for saving faith because of the lack of
biblical clarity. Thus, they do not believe that belief in OSAS (once saved
always saved) is a necessary component of saving faith because of what they
perceive to be a lack of biblical perspicuity regarding unconditional security.
        The chart in Illustration 26. Into Life (Eternal) classifies those
passages most closely associated syntactically with Jesus’ promise of eternal
life as a reward (which is its form in the parable of the sheep and the goats).

*
    GES = Grace Evangelical Society.
Page 90                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
Since I had just finished that chart before engaging in the latest online
discussion about the subjective necessity of security and since that
discussion intersects this chart, applying these observations to that debate—
at least at a rudimentary level—is prudent.
        I already had addressed the topic regarding the necessity of believing
in unconditional security years before in my book The Outer Darkness.
Since that book has not yet been released and is too arduous for some
teenagers, responding to this issue herein is well advised (since I am writing
this book in part for my teenage sons). In fact, the very week in which I
finally returned to doing the indexing for this appendix, I received a flier
from GES dealing with this very subject. The continuing debate surrounding
this very important subject requires an ongoing defense. I side with GES on
this subjective necessity of security.

Bible Study
       In the interim between initially writing this appendix and coming
back to insert the indexing fields, I attended a monthly Bible study in
another city where a fellow attendee (whom I pseudonymously will refer to
as a good Joe or simply Joe for short) asserted that he knew he was saved
because at one point in his life he believed in Jesus and his life radically
changed as a result—a typical proveitist claim.
       Months earlier I had been invited to teach a lesson on the parable of
the sheep and goats at that same Bible study. I pointed out the error of
proveitism during that previous session. The regular teacher for this monthly
meeting and I are in agreement. Therefore, on this subsequent occasion,
when Joe shared his conversion experience, the teacher (and others) pointed
out potential problems with Joe basing his assurance on his performance.
The teacher asked for my input.
       My question to Joe was simple: “Do you believe that you can believe
in Jesus for a ham sandwich and go to heaven as a result.” His response was,
“I do not understand the question.” So I followed up with this question: “Do
you believe that you can believe in Jesus for a hotdog and go to heaven as a
result?” Again, his response was, “I do not understand the question.” Being
as persistent in my line of inquiry as Joe was in trying to evade it, I followed
up with yet a third question: “Do you believe that you can believe in Jesus
for a hamburger and go to heaven as a result?” His final reply yet again was,
“I do not understand the question.”
       Basically, Joe was refusing to answer my questions since doing so
would have led to an exposure of the fallacy of his position. A similar thing
happened to Jesus. Legalists came to Jesus and asked Him a question that
was intended to put Him on the spot. He responded by saying that He would
answer their question if they answered His question. In order to avoid
answering Jesus’ question, they pled ignorance by simply saying, “We do
not know” (Mt 21:27). Times have not changed very much. The problem
Appendix 1. Broken Allegiances                                          Page 91

was not that the question was difficult to answer but that the proper answer
would have exposed the fallacy of the opposing position. So the legalistic
opposition refused to answer.
       In exasperation Joe responded to my questions by asking if I were
trying to question his salvation. I did not answer his question because he
would not answer mine. I did not see any point in pursuing a dialogue with
anyone refusing to deal honestly with the issues.
       Joe thought that he had provided infallible proof that he was saved.
Instead, in my opinion, he had supplied considerable proof that he had never
been saved. One does not get regeneration by trusting in Christ for moral
transformation. Moral transformation is a poor substitute for regeneration.
Sadly, those trusting in Christ for moral transformation as their means of
reaching heaven are on a highway to hell.

Jeremy’s Question
        During the same week that I read the counter article posed at the GES
Webboard, I received a call from my oldest son, Jeremy, who had just come
from a Bible study that taught that your fruit proves whether or not you are
saved. He, in turn, had been reading through my book Breaking the Rocking
Horse and was thankful for my refutation therein of the fruit-proves-root
position. Although he realized that those advocating the fruit-proves-root
theory were in error, he did not grasp the full seriousness of their error. So I
have added this appendix for his sake and for the sake of those like him who
believe in unconditional security yet think that belief in such security is
optional.
        I mentioned to Jeremy that many in his Bible study probably are lost
because, logically, whether or not they admit it, they are conditioning their
salvation from hell on their performance. Understandably, he was reluctant
to think so, wanting to assume the best about his friends in his Bible study.
As commendable as it may be (from one point of view) to think the best of
our friends who oppose unconditional security, from another point of view it
is very dangerous. Indeed, holding on too tightly to the hope that such
friends are saved may deaden one to the reality of the danger. If, as I believe,
belief in unconditional security really is a necessary part of saving faith, then
many of our friends are in grave danger. Hope for the best but prepare for
the worst is the best strategy. Yes, we hope that our friends who believe in
conditional security are, somehow, saved. Yet concern dictates that we, as
unconditional securitists, warn them as to their probable fate.

Pascal's Wager
      Pascal’s wager, as applied to this discussion, may be presented in this
manner: “If you believe in OSAS, but NOSAS is true, then you have little (if
anything) to lose by seeking to earn rewards in heaven. If you disbelieve in
Page 92                                        Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
OSAS, but OSAS is true, then you stand to lose everything by seeking to
earn the reward of heaven.”*
                  Illustration 44. Wager of NOSAS versus OSAS

                                      True                       False
                                                                      †
NOSAS                                  ☺
OSAS                                   ☺                           ☺
Securitists have little to nothing to lose by believing in OSAS, even if
securitists are wrong. From the typical NOSAS perspective, the most that a
godly proponent of OSAS stands to lose is the loss of a few rewards, which
in the opinion of NOSAS does not really matter anyway.20 Relative to
heaven versus hell, the faithful securitist has everything to win and nothing
to lose.
       Alternatively, from an OSAS perspective, faithful conditionalists
potentially have everything to lose if they believe in NOSAS and prove to be
wrong. From an OSAS perspective, a godly advocate of NOSAS stands to
lose everything if NOSAS is false. If faith in OSAS is necessary for
salvation from hell, as some in the OSAS camp (such as GES and I) believe,
then many godly advocates of NOSAS are going to hell.
       Basically, a godly believer has everything to gain and nothing to lose
by believing in OSAS. Granted, this wager does not make OSAS right. And
for the sake of rewards, securitists will do everything they can to make sure
they are right in affirming OSAS. But it might help ease the minds of some
securitists to know that those in the OSAS camp will come out winners
regardless; whereas those in the NOSAS are taking an awful risk. To express
this point concretely:

    •   If conditional security is right, then my sons and I are saved. We not
        only believe in Jesus but have fruit demonstrating that to be the case.
    •   If unconditional security is right (and if faith in unconditional security
        is necessary), then although my sons and I are saved, many of our
        professing Christian friends are lost. They believe in Jesus but have a
        theology that demonstrates that they are trusting additionally in their

Probability of Security
        A division exists in the securitist ranks as to whether elementary faith
in some form of unconditional security is a necessary component of saving
faith. For my part, I believe that at the moment of regenerating faith a person
must believe in his or her own unconditional security in order to experience
regeneration.* One cannot believe in Christ for conditional security and get
unconditional security instead.
        What about my fellow securitists who affirm unconditional security
yet think that believing in unconditional security is optional? They are not
playing Russian roulette with their own salvation (in terms of heaven versus
hell), but I would wager, in Pascal’s sense of the word, that they are taking
soteric risks with the salvation of others.
                Illustration 45. Wager of OSAS-U versus OSAS-N

       If those securitists who regard belief in OSAS as unnecessary are

abbreviations, if OSAS-U securitists are correct, then OSAS-N securitists

are doing nothing to jeopardize the salvation of those who believe in
