       2
         The rudimentary aspect of the proveitist model may be summarized
as: A ⇒ B ⇒ C. If (A) you are a sheep, then (B) you will produce wool.
Consequently, if (B) you will produce wool, then (C) you will be saved ( =
go to heaven). Contrastively, the securest model uses an ampersand instead
and reaches a different conclusion: A & B ⇒ C. If (A) you are a sheep and
(B) if you produce wool, then (C) you will be saved (= be rewarded in
heaven).
       The secondary aspect of the proveitist model is the bidirectional
direction of its arrows: A ⇔ B ⇔ C. The securitist model is, by default,
mono-directional: A & B ⇒ C. However, if A & B parenthetically are
grouped together so as to express one term, then the securest model could be
expressed by a singular bidirectional arrow: (A & B) ⇔ C. Regardless, the
type of salvation envisioned by the proveitist model would be soteric but for
the securitist model mistholic.
       3
          As argued in Mere Christianity and Moral Christianity, the import
of Mt 7:19 would be: If you are a bad tree, then you will produce bad fruit,
and if you produce bad fruit, then you will go to hell. This warning certainly
seems to be a simple chain of causation: A ⇒ B ⇒ C.

                    (A) Bad tree ⇒ (B) Bad fruit ⇒ (C) Hell

       Bad fruit is the immediate cause for being thrown into hell, and being
a bad tree is the ultimate cause for being thrown into hell. Both jointly
constitute the reasons a person is sent to hell (A & B ⇒ C). Those securitists
who believe that the sole reason a person is sent to hell is because an
individual lacks the gift of eternal life might seek to counter by appealing to
my smoking gun analogy herein (p. 29) in conjunction with my argument
therein (i.e., in Mere Christianity and Moral Christianity) that the bad fruit
in that context merely refers to non-saving faith and thereby contend that
(A) unbelievers are sent to hell because of (B) unbelief, not because of
performance, so that sins are not a basis for damnation.
       On the other hand, as pointed out in my discussion of that context
within Mere Christianity and Moral Christianity, the lost are not thrown into
hell for simply being sinners but also for sinning. They are condemned to
Page 122                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
hell, at least in part, because of their moral performance. The judicial nature
of this evidence is confirmed by Jesus when He responds to the defendant:
“Depart from Me, you who practice lawlessness” (Mt 7:23).
        As for my discussion herein, an asymmetrical relationship is being
posed for salvation versus damnation. Separation (and thus salvation from
hell) is based solely upon regeneration or the lack thereof. Damnation,
however, takes both indisputable evidence (i.e., the lack of one’s name in the
Book of Life) and circumstantial evidence (i.e., the Books of Works) into
consideration. An asymmetrical relation thus is being suggested herein
between separation and damnation. Given that I criticize the asymmetrical
proposal by Calvinists who favor infralapsarianism, I briefly will explain
why the asymmetrical relation between salvation and damnation being
advocated here is not susceptible to the same criticism.
        In contradistinction to the illogical asymmetrical supposition of
infralapsarianism, the asymmetrical juxtaposition being posed herein
between damnation and salvation is consistent logically because if one’s
name is in the Book of Life, then the negative circumstantial evidence found
in the Books of Works will be seen in a different light than if one’s name is
not in the Book of Life. Without that indisputable evidence of regeneration,
negative circumstantial evidence becomes part of the soteriological case
against the defendant. In sharp contrast, one who possesses eternal life does
not come into soteriological judgment (Jn 5:24); hence circumstantial
evidence cannot function in the soteric capacity when the defendant has
eternal life. A believer’s works can only be judged misthologically at an
eschatological judgment, not soteriologically.
        Contrastively, in infralapsarianism reprobation really is just as
unconditional as election in terms of the logical composition of its
constituent parts. The alleged asymmetrical relationship between
infralapsarian reprobation and election actually is symmetrical because the
asymmetrical relationships within them are logically congruent. As
explained theologically in The Outer Darkness, the heart of
infralapsarianism may be summarized as:

•   Election = unconditional regeneration + conditional justification.
•   Reprobation = unconditional preterition + conditional condemnation.

Correspondingly, because infralapsarian election is unconditional,
infralapsarian reprobation must be unconditional also. Rather than provide a
theological rationale in the present book, perhaps a mathematical illustration
will suffice.
Endnotes                                                            Page 123

                       Illustration 65. ∆ABC ≅ ∆DEF




      In mathematics, if two sides and the included angle of one triangle are
congruent to two sides and the included angle of a second triangle, then the
two triangles are congruent. This side-angle-side relation is abbreviated as
SAS. Thus, triangle ABC (∆ABC) is congruent to (≅) triangle DEF (∆DEF).
This allows one to deduce that the unknown corresponding sides (BC and
EF) also are congruent. In other words, SAS is known because

                               S: AB ≅ DE
                               A: ∠A ≅ ∠D
                               S: AC ≅ DF

       Accordingly, ∆ABC ≅ ∆DEF is the proper mathematical deduction.
Consequently, BC ≅ EF is a valid inference. One need not be in the dark
about the relationship between BC and EF if one knows math. Likewise,
tracing out the logical relations between corresponding components in an
infralapsarian ordo salutis leads to an inescapable conclusion regarding the
relation between infralapsarian election and reprobation.
                   Illustration 66. Election ≅ Reprobation




       Contrary to the infralapsarian contention counter wise, infralapsarian
reprobation must be unconditional because it is congruent to infralapsarian
election. Since this election is unconditional, its corresponding reprobation
must be unconditional also. Thus, infralapsarian election is symmetrical, not
asymmetrical, to reprobation. Infralapsarianism logically leads to the
conclusion that God unconditionally chose to save some and damn others.
For logical and theological reasons, I reject infralapsarianism.
       For that matter, I also reject the proposed infralapsarian asymmetrical
relationship between regeneration and justification. I believe both are
conditional. Nevertheless, as a logician looking only at the above
summations, I would not object to the possibility of an asymmetrical
Page 124                                   Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
relationship between regeneration and justification if it could be shown to be
biblical. However, since I provide counter evidence in The Outer Darkness
to such a proposal, I need not do so here. Rather, I am only demonstrating
that an asymmetrical relation is possible if logically and biblically
defensible. For this reason, contrary to some of my FG counterparts, I
perceive an asymmetrical relation between salvation and damnation. Yet it is
not the asymmetrical relation proposed by Calvinists.
        When salvation is considered in its fullest scope, it encompasses
consideration of one’s works because full salvation includes the
misthological range of salvation. Likewise, when damnation is considered in
its fullest scope, it must encompass consideration of one’s works because the
works of the lost will determine their degree of suffering in hell. From this
perspective, salvation and damnation might be regarded as symmetrical in
that both are conditioned on regeneration and performance—when salvation
and damnation are discussed in their fullest scope.
                 Illustration 67. Damnation Versus Salvation




       Conversely, when salvation and damnation are stripped down to
answering the most basic question—whether one goes to heaven or hell—an
asymmetrical relation is perceivable. Works cannot save us, but they can
damn us. Works can play a role, albeit a secondary role, in condemning a
person to hell. Degeneration plus works result in damnation. Works do not
factor into consideration for soteric salvation, however. This basic
dimension of salvation is conditioned solely on regeneration without any
reference to one’s works. The soteric relationship between damnation and
salvation is thus asymmetrical. In the above geometrical illustration, this
asymmetrical relation is pictured by a three-sided triangle in antithetical
juxtaposition with a two-sided digon. For those not familiar with digons, let
it be noted that the juxtaposition is even sharper than what is being
represented by these three-sided and two-sided figures on this two-
dimensional page.
Endnotes                                                               Page 125

                 Illustration 68. Digon is Three Dimensional




       In actuality, a regular digon is a two-sided figure that only truly exists
without degeneration in the three-dimensional space of spherical geometry.
(A digon degenerates in two-dimensional space.) Thus, the two-sided digon
on the left must be understood in the three-dimensional context on the right.
In sharp contrast, a triangle is a three-sided figure that only exists in two-
dimensional space. To even put a triangle and digon in the same space, as I
did in the initial two-dimensional digon illustration, is a misrepresentation of
the three-dimensional digon.
                         Illustration 69. Orange Digon
Page 126                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
        One might picture a digon as the two edges of a wedge on the surface
of a solid ball. To use a rough analogy, think of it as the two perimeter sides
on an orange after you have cut out a wedge. The wedge border is the digon.
Indeed, one could use this for a children’s object lesson. Draw a digon on
the orange skin (i.e., two lines in the shape of a wedge). Label the outside of
one line as eternal life. Label the outside of the other line as salvation. Then
cut out the wedge. Your labels still will be intact on the spherical edges of
the remaining orange skin. Explain that salvation from hell has nothing to do
with works. Their works are nowhere to be found on the orange skin.
        Then have them draw a triangle on a piece of paper and label one side
as death, the other as works, and the other as hell. Explain that if a person
dies in a state of spiritual death, then that person’s works will condemn that
individual to hell. Then emphasize the obvious: Works have nothing to do
with going to heaven; they are not part of the orange digon. The only thing
that determines whether we will go to heaven is whether we have eternal
life. The only people for whom works have a role to play in regards to
determining whether they go to hell are those who live in a two-dimensional
world lacking eternal life.
        In contrast, those who are saved have something that the lost do not
have—eternal life. This extra dimension raises those who are saved to a
higher dimension. In this higher sphere of existence, salvation is connected
singularly with having eternal life just as the labels of salvation and eternal
life are connected by the singular three-dimensional orange peel.
      4
        Anyone who receives the mark of the beast will be tormented in hell
forever (Rev 14:11). Thus, receiving the mark of the beast will prove that
one is not a believer. Proveitists might seek to counter the approach taken
herein by arguing that if taking the mark proves that one is not a believer,
then, according to the methodology adopted within this book, not taking the
mark is necessary to reach heaven. That is, can proveitists not turn my
argument against itself?
      I maintain that (A) by making perseverance a necessary proof that one
is a believer, (B) proveitists have made perseverance a condition to reach
heaven. Can proveitists counter that (A) by my making not taking the mark a
necessary proof that one is a believer, (B) I have made not taking the mark
a condition to reach heaven? Am I not advocating conditional security—a
security conditioned on not taking the mark of the beast? Not at all.
      As explained more fully in The Outer Darkness, I believe that God will
not allow a believer to take the mark of the beast. If a believer subcomes in
intent to taking the mark of the beast, God will chastise that believer with
premature death before the believer has an opportunity to actually take the
mark (also see GNTC, 1302). To be sure, God will judge that believer, even
posthumously, for the intentions of the believer’s heart (Heb 4:12).
Nevertheless, the intent to take the mark of the beast does not result in the
believer being sent to hell.
Endnotes                                                                Page 127

      Some securitists, regrettably, have taken a view of God’s chastisement
that logically leads to conditional security. According to such securitists,
God disciplines believers in order to keep them saved soteriologically. Such
an approach quickly leads to conditional security because a proveitist can
merely assert that God chastises believers who would succumb to apostasy
with premature death before they commit apostasy in order to keep them
saved. In this way, preservation is made contingent on perseverance. When
the logical implications of this theology are traced out, not committing
apostasy is necessary to remain saved. For this and other reasons, more
thoughtful securitists acknowledge that believers can commit apostasy and
remain saved.
      By extrapolation, I contend that believers could take the mark of the
beast and remain saved—if God were so inclined to allow them to take the
mark of the beast. From prophecy I logically deduce that God is not so
inclined, however. Yet from soteriology I logically deduce that if God were
so inclined, then believers could take the mark of the beast and remain
saved. God has not conditioned their security on their not taking the
mark of the beast.
      In stark contrast, proveitists believe that God has conditioned the
believer’s security on the believer not committing apostasy. For this reason
proveitists insist upon the impossibility of apostasy. Whereas proveitists
believe that it is necessary for believers not to commit apostasy in order to
reach heaven, I, in counterpoint, do not believe that it is necessary for
believers not to take the mark of the beast in order to reach heaven. Whereas
it might be asserted that I believe that not taking the mark of the beast is a
necessary proof prophetically, proveitists believe that perseverance is a
necessary proof soteriologically. The potential proveitistic objection is not
comparing apples with apples.
      In reality, I do not equate infallibility with necessity, only with
certainty. This necessary proof, as a proveitist may wish to call it, is better
called infallible proof. It is not a soteric necessity. If a more precise label is
deemed desirable, as to what kind of certainty is involved in not taking the
mark of the beast, I would call it unconditional certainty. God will use
irresistible force, namely premature death, to prevent believers from taking
the mark of the beast, not because He has to do so in order to keep them
saved but because He is free to limit His toleration of just how far He will let
backsliders slide. In the OT, God has a reputation of dealing severely with
severe backsliders. There is no reason to think that He is going to be very
tolerant of believers who backslide in the tribulation when He is pouring out
His wrath upon earth dwellers.
      5
         Man was born to rule. Christians are born again with a view to
rulership as well. Man does not forfeit his birth, and Christians do not forfeit
their new birth. Nevertheless, the intended misthological result of one’s birth
may be forfeited.
Page 128                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
     6
          To speak theologically, although their last state is not worse
positionally or ontologically than it was before, it is worse experientially.
      7
         The converse argument cannot be substantiated that because Jesus
commends the sheep for helping these brothers of Mine rather than these
brothers of yours, the sheep are not believers. Although some interpreters
argue that the sheep are nonbelievers (or implicit rather than explicit
believers), this argument will not hold up. See my book Sheep and Goats:
Four Misthological Interpretations. The sheep are Gentile believers who
help Jesus’ Jewish evangelists. The sheep are distinguished from the
brethren ethnically, not soteriologically.
      8
        Even a lost pharisaical scribe could correctly affirm: “You shall love
the Lord your God with all your heart, and with all your soul, and with all
your strength, and with all your mind; and your neighbor as yourself”
(Lk 10:27). The unsaved Pharisees of Jesus’ day asserted their sacrificial
love for God just as the unregenerate Pharisees of our day avow their
passionate love for Jesus. Unfortunately, for the Pharisees of both the past
and the present, a self-perceived love for God (no matter how strong the
impression or how vigorously expressed in performance) does not constitute
saving faith. While Pharisees are banking on their love for God as qualifying
them for heaven, God is asking them, “Do you trust Me to give you eternal
life as a free gift that is not conditioned on your love for Me?” As always,
their legalistic response is, “No.” They have the audacity to think that their
love qualifies them for entrance into heaven, and, in the process, they try to
justify themselves by their loving performance (Lk 10:29). They miss out
justification by faith because they are trying to be justified by loving
performance. Sadly, legalists read the parable of the good Samaritan and
think that it means that they can be saved from hell by being good Joes.
They think that it proves that one can be justified by legalistic love when the
parable was given in a context refuting legalistic love as a possibility for
self-justification.
      9
         It will not do for the proveitist to insist that we are not in Kansas
anymore when we reach the parable of the sheep and the goats. One cannot
take a flight of fantasy and forget the real world—a world in which some
unbelievers live better lives than some believers—and pretend that the way
one lives determines whether one is a genuine believer. Proveitists are not
free to forget the theological terrain and escape to a make-believe world
where they cry, Off with his head! every time someone fails to live up to
proveitistic expectations.
      10
           Of course, Satan and his followers can perform great signs
(Mt 24:24) and false signs (2Thess 2:9). Conversely, the evidence that Judas
and these followers of Jesus in Mt 7:22 performed genuine miracles is so
compelling that even some well-known proveitists acknowledge this
probability. At least some of these unbelievers cast out demonic beings and
performed authentic miracles by the power of God.
Endnotes                                                             Page 129

      This is not to say that every time a demon appears to be cast out that it
actually is cast out, much less that it is cast out by the power of God (cf.
2Cor 11:14). Some proveitist commentators have done a splendid job of
describing the logical possibilities surrounding lost people casting out
demons in Mt 7:22. What these commentators have failed to deduce is that
this means that fruit does not prove root. Wool does not prove regeneration.
Proveitism consequently proves false.
      11
         Explicitly, Paul chastises the Corinthian believers by saying you
defraud your brethren (1Cor 6:8-9). These sheep defraud rather than help
their brethren. If not helping others is a sign of dead faith (Jam 2:17)—a
faith that has no life, a faith that registers zero on the helping-fellow-
believer’s scale—then the actions by the Corinthian believers is a negative
faith by way of comparison.
                       Illustration 70. Faith Spectrum




The notion that dead faith (that produces no benefits in terms of helping
fellow believers) proves that the person with such dead faith is a nonbeliever
is nonsensical. If hurtful faith (which is worse than non-helpful faith) does
not prove that the Corinthian defrauders are not brothers, then non-helpful
faith cannot possibly prove that non-helpful believers are not brothers.
      12
         God can and does graciously obligate Himself to reward us based on
our performance. To be sure, Paul asks in Romans, “Who hath first given
[prodidomi] to him that it might be paid back to him again?” (11:35) It is
impossible for us to obligate God by our initiative contribution, but this does
not mean it is impossible to obligate God by our derivative consecration.
Those translations that leave out the word first lead the reader astray. The
preposition before (pro-) suggests that prodidomi should not be translated
simply as give (didomi).
      The NIV translates the verse badly and makes what should be a
rhetorical question a ridiculous question: “Who has ever given to God, that
God should repay him?” The answer is, “Lots of people,” when the verse is
mistranslated in this manner. For one thing, the Lord’s giving servants are
repaid by the Lord:

      But when you give a reception, invite the poor, the crippled, the
      lame, the blind, and you will be blessed, since they do not have
      the means to repay you; for you will be repaid at the
      resurrection of the righteous. (Lk 14:13-14)
Page 130                                         Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep


Giving to others can be considered as giving to the Lord (Mt 25:40).
Therefore, it is possible to give to God with the result that He will repay you.
Thematically, other passages lead to the same conclusion (e.g., Mal 3:10;
Mt 6:3-4; Lk 6:38).
     13
        The contextual and conceptual parallels between Mt 25:46 and
Jn 12:25 are complementary.
                Illustration 71. Parallels for “Into Eternal life”

In the Johannine context, service to Jesus results in the future acquisition of
eternal life in terms of being honored and praised by God. In the Matthean

terms of being given the kingdom as an inheritance. God will reward faithful
believers with eternal life as both a subjective and objective reward.
      14
         Jesus promises, “He who overcomes shall thus be clothed in white
garments; and I will not erase his name from the book of life” (Rev 3:5). The
promise that the names of overcomers will not be erased has been twisted by
many into a warning that the names of non-overcomers will be erased. Even
some securitists accommodate this mistaken perspective by posing that the
Book of Life in this verse is different from the Book of Life described
elsewhere in Revelation, specifically in Rev 20:12-15. Such securitists might
regard the Book of Life in Rev 3:5 to be what is described herein as the
Book for Life. In such an arrangement, such securitists suggest that names
can be removed from the Book for Life, signifying that one can lose their
rewards.
      Conceptually, such a proposal is certainly attractive. Indeed, Rev 3:11
affirms that unrealized rewards (i.e., potential crowns) may be lost. Rewards
that already belong potentially to believers can be lost before the final
realization of such rewards at the bema. Certainly, one could describe this
Endnotes                                                            Page 131

loss of unrealized rewards as having one’s name erased from the Book for
Life. Truly, latent rewards can be lost.
      Exegetically, however, arguments for permanence in the Book of Life
(derived from the fact that those names are written from the foundation of
the world) would seem to apply equally well for permanence in the Book for
Life because of the seemingly corresponding equivalency: Their inheritance
is prepared from the foundation of the world. Notwithstanding this surface
similarity, however, this objection is not insurmountable in that the
preparation could be contingent and cooperative.
      The same is not true of the Book of Life. Unlike the preparation
pertaining to the Book for Life, which potentially could be generic, the
written individualization of the names in the Book of Life is explicitly
individualistic. Furthermore, the Book of Life is a record that is made and
maintained independent of the believer’s works. Most emphatically, the
Book of Life is not dependent on the Books of Works. Contrastively, the
Book for Life is definitely dependent upon the Books of Works for its
entries.
      The emphatic contrastive conjunctive in Rev 3:5 would be rendered
well as: “He who overcomes will thus be clothed in white garments, and
[kai] I will not erase his name from the Book of Life; indeed [kai], I will
confess his name before My Father and before His angels (Rev 3:5; TM). In
terms of the present discussion, the promise in this verse is as if Jesus had
said, “Instead of erasing His name from the Book of Life, I will write His
name in the Book for Life.” This figure of speech is known in Greek as a
litotes. The point of the litotes is not that Jesus might erase the name from
the Book of Life. Rather, it is that Jesus will do the opposite of erasing the
name from the Book of Life. The opposite would be to record it in the Book
for Life.
      15
          The Colossian believers had a misthological hope reserved
(apokeimai) in heaven (Col 1:5). Paul also referred to the crown of
righteousness which is reserved (apokeimai) for faithful believers (2Tim
4:8). Nevertheless, the realization of the reservation is contingent upon
successful completion of the competition. The hope of such potential crowns
can be forfeited (Rev 3:11). Similarly, all the Colossian believers were
qualified (hikanoo) by their Father for the inheritance (Col 1:12). Yet they
may become disqualified (katabrabeuo) for the prize/reward by their
performance (Col 2:18), just as Paul, who was qualified presently, could
become disqualified (katabrabeuo) subsequently for the prize/reward
(1Cor 9:27) if he did not successfully complete the race. The realization of
this inheritance for which they already were potentially qualified would have
to be earned as a reward (Col 3:24). A successful misthological presentation
was conditioned on their steadfast continuation in the faith (Col 1:22-23).
Paul labored strenuously for this type of presentation on behalf of others
(Col 1:28-29). Entrance into the kingdom, nonetheless, as well as
Page 132                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
redemption already had been realized (Col 1:13-14; 2:13-14). Therefore,
these twin soteriological benefits unconditionally were assured. In summary,
by paying their nonrefundable entrance fee and providing them the
opportunity, strength, and necessary training, their Father had qualified them
to run the race and win it if they would avail themselves of His recourses.
They would not have to pay the fee if they lost the race. But neither would
they gain the reward if they lost the race. The race and the prize might be
lost; they themselves could not be.
      16
         As explained in more detail in The Outer Darkness, dwelling in the
reward city is a reward. This city will be the capital city, not the only city.
Those believers cast into the outer darkness will dwell outside the city.
Although this city will have no need of the sun or experience night, the rest
of the planet will. Thus, those believers cast into the outer darkness during
the parabolic night will live on the part of the planet that still experiences
nighttime darkness. The parabolic descriptions of this darkness surely
encompass more than nighttime darkness, but they need not entail less.
              Illustration 72. Darkness Outside the Reward City
Endnotes                                                             Page 133
     17
        See The Outer Darkness for a potential distinction between dwelling
places (monai; pl. of mone) and prepared place (topos). In that proposal, the
singular mone is equated with the singular topos. For sake of conciseness,
this technical distinction is not being implemented herein. Rather, the
dwelling places are the prepared places. The many mansions (plural) refer to
arrangements being made for the city citizens collectively; the singular
refers to the preparation being made for each of them individually. In this
imagery, the reward city is composed of many mansions: one for each
occupant.
       18
          On the other hand, believers’ works conceivably could be recorded
as those works are committed, and the believers’ enrollment in the Book for
Life might be entered in the future as a result of how they do at judgment.
Even if this is the case, rewarded sheep will find their place prepared for
them from the foundation of the world in that God foreknew and
foreordained their positions of reward based on their foreseen coming to
faith and foreseen persevering in faith. Although their works supply the
building material for Jesus’ ongoing work in preparing their mansions, the
allotments for those mansions already have been predetermined by the
Father in accordance with His plans and foreknowledge.

         Christian profession plus good performance is not equatable with

     20
        Some extremists among the forfeitist ranks believe that securitists are
lost because (from this narrow forfeitistic perspective) proponents of
unconditional security are teaching a false gospel that results in their being
soteriologically accursed (cp. Gal 1:8-9). Indeed, some of these ultraistic
conditionalists believe that more moderate conditionalists fall into the same
soteriological condemnation because these moderate conditionalists do not
make salvation as conditional as the ultraistic securitists. Using the same
logic, some ultraistic proveitists apparently would deduce that if you teach
that believing in Christ is a free-will response, then you necessarily are lost
because faith (from this narrow proveitistic perspective) that comes about
from free will is not saving faith. Describing NOSAS in terms of either
extreme would be unfair to the majority within NOSAS who take a more
moderate position. Most conditional securitists are more charitable than their
extremist counterparts and would not limit entrance into heaven to those
conditionalists who hold to their particular brand of conditionalism.
Therefore, most conditionalists would acknowledge that godly securitists are
saved.
     Though written from the opposing perspective of unconditional
security, the present book likewise prefers (out of theological conviction) to
be charitable to those in the securitist camp who do not believe that
unconditional security is necessary subjectively. In other words, I do not
Page 134                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
believe that those holding to OSAS-U are lost even though I advocate
OSAS-N. Yet, as one who holds to the conviction that OSAS-N is true, I
cannot be so charitable so as to conclude that those who reject OSAS are
embracing a saving form of the gospel. One cannot be saved by faith in
one’s works, and the rejection of OSAS necessarily leads to faith in one’s
performance as a false hope of reaching heaven. In summary, from the
moderate perspective, the conclusion reached herein is true: Those who
embrace OSAS and live godly lives have little (if anything to lose) if they
are wrong; whereas, those who embrace NOSAS have everything to lose if
they are wrong. As a mathematician, simply looking at the OSAS-NOSAS
debate purely from the perspective of Pascal’s wager, I merely point out that
NOSAS is far more dangerous than OSAS.
      As for forfeitistic extremists who would contend otherwise, they are
refuted by both securitists and by their own fellow forfeitists. As for the
extremist appeal to Gal 1:8-9, anyone reading the book of Galatians should
be able to discern for themselves that the Galatian curse being invoked by
Paul in the context of that epistle is being called forth upon those who
pervert the gospel by making soteriological security conditioned on
performance. Therefore, forfeitistic extremists who try to use this passage to
invoke a soteric curse upon those who do not condition soteriological
security on performance show themselves to be the ones for whom the curse
contextually is intended. Contextually, that particular curse is intended to be
applied to performatists, not to securitists.
         Godly advocates of OSAS will enter the kingdom by faith and inherit
the kingdom because of their godly behavior. Godly advocates of NOSAS
will not enter the kingdom because their accursed faith is in their godly
performance. For those godly advocates of NOSAS who at one time held to
OSAS, they will enter the kingdom but not inherit it because they have
fallen from grace into preaching an accursed gospel. In summary, if OSAS is
true, then OSAS is a win-win proposition, and NOSAS is a lose-lose
proposition. Conversely, if NOSAS is true, then OSAS is still a win-win
proposition.
      21
         As if these dual errors were not enough, our pastor compounded the
irrationality of his Easter sermon by pointing out that those who crucified
Jesus were the religious people who thought they were better than everyone
else because they loved and served God. Basically, those religionists thought
they were going to heaven because they were better than everyone else. Our
pastor inadvertently was inviting the congregation to share in the religious
mentality of those who crucified Jesus by sharing in an attitude of moral
superiority that (according to his sermon) would entitle them to heaven. He
was inviting the congregation to consider themselves better than those so-
called Christians (speaking from his perspective) who think that they can get
into heaven just by believing in Jesus for eternal life without dedicating their
lives to Him in loving service. Logically, salvation (according to the logical
Endnotes                                                              Page 135

extension of his sermon) is conditioned on being better than those people
who merely believe in Jesus for fire insurance. By logical extension (if not
by verbal admission), he considers himself morally superior to those people
who merely believe in Jesus for eternal life without dedicating themselves to
the Lord’s service.
      Like the Pharisees of old, such pastors and congregations consider
themselves better than others and think that they will enter heaven as a
result. By our pastor’s own admission, the religious people who professed to
love and serve God were the ones who crucified Jesus. Because of their love
and service for God, they were blinded to their need of Christ. Therefore, it
should be concluded that Christian religionists likewise reject the salvation
by faith alone provided by Jesus, opting instead for a salvation from
damnation that takes their love and service into account. They believe that
their morally superior loving service must count for something in regard to
entering heaven.
               Illustration 73. The Addition of Loving Devotion




      To be sure, our pastor tries to soften his legalism as much as possible to
make it more palatable. A few months later, during August, when preaching
in Nehemiah about the importance of making promises, he sought to relate
this text to what it means to become a Christian. Once again, he expressed
his disbelief that those who simply believe in Jesus in order to escape the
fires of hell are saved. In his opinion, more than faith was required. Loving
devotion to Jesus not only was desired; it was necessary. He believed that
loving, devoted followers of Christ would be saved from hell; mere believers
in Christ would go to hell.
      To his credit, within this sermon our pastor stressed that he was not
anywhere near perfect (so Christians do not get into heaven because of their
moral perfection). This emphasis is reminiscent of the theme that Christians
are not perfect, just forgiven. At the same time, his offsetting stress on being
lovingly devoted to Christ as a necessity to reach heaven necessarily limited
Page 136                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
entrance into heaven to those who (like himself) were better than others
because of their loving devotion to Christ.
      While painting himself as needing forgiveness just like everyone else,
he logically (yet probably unwittingly) simultaneously painted himself as
being morally better than those who merely believe in Jesus. He criticized
those who merely believe in Jesus just so that they can be forgiven and go to
heaven. Unashamedly, he was looking down his long, soteriological nose at
those who merely believe in Christ for eternal life. He dismissed the
possibility of their salvation being genuine. What chance did they have of
reaching heaven compared to someone like him? Very little, it would seem,
at least in his self-assessment. Logically, he was not claiming to be perfect,
just better than them. He was self-assured that he would go to heaven but
skeptical that many of those whom he had baptized would make it to heaven
because of their lack of devotion.
                            Illustration 74. FAICA




      Ironically, a few weeks later, when our church was going to have a
baptismal service, our pastor stressed that faith alone in Christ alone
(FAICA) suffices to qualify one for baptism. Indeed, this emphasis is
biblical (Acts 8:36-37). Yet I could not help but think about his remarks a
few weeks previously when he discounted the salvation of those who merely
believe in Christ for eternal life. Unthinkingly, it would seem, he allowed
that FAICA was sufficient for baptism but denied that it was sufficient to
reach heaven. One should not be surprised when he states that he does not
believe that many of those whom he baptized are going to heaven when he
makes two different qualifications. His qualification for baptism is simple
faith alone in Christ for eternal life. His qualification for heaven is faith plus
being better than those who merely believe in Christ alone for eternal life.
Only religious bigots make it to this pastor’s heaven.
Endnotes                                                                Page 137

      Religious bigotry manifests itself in an unjustified I-am-better-than-you
attitude. Non-Christians charge Christians with having an I-am-holier-than-
thou attitude. This frequent criticism by non-Christians of many professing
Christians is not entirely without justification. In proveitistic Christianity, as
manifested in these sermons by our proveitist pastor, the proveitistic attitude
of moral superiority comes across as an I-am-better-than-you disposition.
The pastor encourages his congregation to share his I-am-better-than-you
perspective so that they may perceive themselves as being saved because
they are better than those who merely believe in Christ for eternal life.
Proveitists think that eternal life is to be had (as a gift!) only by those who
(like themselves) manifest a morally superior affection for Christ.
      Certainly, our pastor and our congregation would deny that they are
religious bigots. I suspect that the Pharisees of Jesus’ day did not consider
themselves hypocrites or whitewashed tombs either—though Jesus accused
them of being both. Jesus saw through their self-deception and penetrated
through to the logical implications of their theology. Likewise, this charge of
Christian bigotry is deduced from the logical implications of the theology of
our pastor and his fellow preachers in our congregation, not from their self-
assessment. Granted, I am thankful for the love displayed by our pastor and
congregation for God. Unfortunately, I have strong reason to believe that
they are trusting in what they perceive to be their morally superior love for
God as a means of reaching heaven, which means, in all probability, that
they are on a path that leads to hell.
      As for me and my family, I want us to love God, not so that we can be
morally superior to others and thus merit heaven, but so that we can hear His
words, “Well done,” when we reach heaven. Loving God is a meritorious
response that has the potential to invoke His praise and reward. God rewards
those who love Him—as long as they are not trying to insult Him by
thinking that their love for Him is a contributing factor to their salvation
from hell. The only thing that can merit heaven is the love and blood of
Christ. Our loving devotion is too defective to be mingled with His loving
blood as a means of reaching heaven. We cannot taint His pure atoning
blood with our blood, sweat, or tears.
      Does this mean that loving devotion counts for nothing? Of course not.
Will those Christians who love God be considered better Christians than
those Christians who do not love God? Of course they will. That is how it
should be. Christians who love Jesus are better Christians than those who do
not. Furthermore, God will reward those Christians who love Him in ways
that He will not reward those Christians who do not love Him. Being better
than someone else in terms of loving service is a matter of merit and reward.
      Regrettably, following through on the logical implications of such an
assessment leads to the conclusion that our pastor and congregation believe
that they merit heaven because of their moral superiority. After all, a loving
person is, no doubt, morally superior to a non-loving person.
Page 138                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
      Even in this case though, humility triumphs over religiosity. I will not
claim that my family and I love Jesus, in terms of either affection or
devotion, more than our pastor or other members in our congregation. I am
not in a contest with them to see if I can reach heaven by loving Jesus more
than they do. Praise God for the common grace that He bestows that even
enables lost religionists (who think that they are better than everyone else) to
love Him and experience fellowship with Him. I will concede that they
experience affection and devotion for God and intimacy with God. For all I
can tell, experientially and externally (as opposed to logically and
theologically), their experience with God is indiscernible from what my
family and I experience.
      On the other hand, I would remind Christian religionists that the love of
God is that we keep His commandments, which means first and foremost
that we believe in the name of His Son Jesus Christ for eternal life. Those
who believe in their love as a requisite for the gift of eternal life are going to
have an impossible time convincing God that they believe in His Son alone
for this gift. They certainly have failed to convince me.
      In his sermon on the importance of making promises, our pastor used
Neh 9:38 to liken becoming a Christian to promising to be a loving, devoted
wife to Christ—a marriage covenant. A thinking congregant might want to
ask a number of questions. What happens to the believer who makes this
promise but fails to follow through? What happens to those believers who
start off with loving excitement but then fall into an adulterous love affair
with the world? Do they become enemies of Christ so that they are no longer
brides of Christ (Jam 4:4)? If a morally, virtuous, loving commitment is
required to become a Christian, is it not also required to stay a Christian? Do
those believers who lose their first love for Christ, lose their salvation
(Rev 2:4)? If the gift of eternal life is a wedding band, does Christ take it
back if His wife is unfaithful?
      Many such questions are left unanswered by the proveitist simpleton.
Rather than speculate on how our pastor might answer such questions, it
suffices to point out that in misconstruing the gift of eternal life as a
wedding band, proveitists are denying that it is a free gift. Wedding bands
are not given freely. What man buys a bag full of expensive wedding bands
and passes them out to every woman on the street? Wedding bands are given
to women who promise lifelong devotion. They are not free gifts, so why use
them to picture the free gift of eternal life?
      In his follow-up sermon on this passage the following Sunday, our
pastor again reiterated his interpretation of Neh 9:38 by asserting that
becoming a Christian is signing up to keep God’s commandments. Whoa! I
thought that one became a Christian by believing in Christ for eternal life.
Our pastor thinks otherwise.
      One might be tempted to give him the benefit of the doubt in that
becoming a Christian might be taken by some to mean, for instance,
Endnotes                                                               Page 139

becoming a full-fledged disciple as opposed to fledgling disciple as opposed
to unfledged disciple. For example, I use these terms in this manner:

     1. Unfledged disciples = unsaved disciples.
     2. Fledgling disciples = newborn and/or immature, saved disciples.
     3. Full-fledged disciples = fully committed, mature, saved disciples.

      If our pastor meant that one becomes a Christian (i.e., full-fledged
believer) by signing up, so to speak, for the rigors of full-hearted
commitment to God-enabled performance, then I might demur slightly
semantically, but I would concur theologically. As to semantics, I would
prefer to say that even fledgling believers are Christians since they are
saved. Or as expressed in Breaking the Rocking Horse, the fact that all full-
fledged disciples are Christians does not prove that all Christians are full-
fledged disciples. A distinction might be suggested between becoming a
Christian and being one.
      This potential difference in wording aside, however, our pastor
removed all doubt (and therefore all room for the benefit of the doubt) when
he immediately clarified his position within this follow-up sermon by
stipulating that one cannot come to Christ for salvation from hell if one still
intends to live like hell. By making such a statement, our pastor basically
was saying that salvation from hell is conditioned on the way a person lives:
A person cannot live like hell and expect salvation from hell.
      In such a context, our pastor thereby was illustrating the fact that he
believes that becoming a Christian is equivalent with becoming saved
(which is the normal and proper sense in which this expression should be
understood). The problem, then, is that he has made becoming saved from
hell conditioned on the way a person lives. His perspective reduces to
salvation by works.
      Basically, our pastor was appealing to the common mentality that finds
it appalling to suggest that one can live like the devil and still go to heaven.
Because it is commonly recognized that the devil is not going to heaven,
presenting the performance perspective in this manner does not raise the red
flag of salvation by works in the minds of most congregants. They do not
realize that they are being suckered by the devil into thinking that getting to
heaven is a matter of living better than others since the devil sugarcoats it as
being better than him. When this poisonous line of thinking is traced out,
however, the works-righteousness premise becomes apparent.
      Within this hellish perversion of the gospel of supposedly free grace,
not only is it necessary to live better than those who live like the devil (or
who live like hell) in order to be saved from hell, it is also necessary that one
live better than the lost. Which lost? The bad lost? How about the good lost?
Suppose the requirement is only that one live better than the bad lost. How
much better than the bad lost must one live?
Page 140                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
       Aside from such problems of soteriological relativism, this perspective
still reduces to the religious bigotry in which the legalist thinks: “I am going
to heaven because I live better than ___________.” Filling in the blank with
the devil seems like a safe bet—until one thinks through the logical
implications of such a risky proposition because (among other reasons) our
pastor was not limiting his performance perspective to living better than just
the devil. He was using those who live like hell as his point of reference. So
in this case, the blank would be filled in like this by the unsuspecting
congregant: “I am going to heaven because I live better than those who live
like hell.” This reduces to a I-am-going-to-heaven-because-I-live-better-
than-them attitude. The them frequently will vary from one legalistic
preacher or sermon to the next. Nevertheless, the common denominator is
holier-than-thou, performance-based superiority.
       Before the proveitist is through, the proveitistic requirement that one
live better than those who live like hell will become a requirement to live
better than those going to hell. After all, for proveitism to be true, the saved
must live better than the lost. For according to proveitists, the way one lives
proves whether one is lost. In proveitism, the saved may not be perfect, but
the saved are better than the lost. Before all is said and done, getting into
proveitist heaven is conditioned on living better than those who are not
going to heaven.
       This pastor encourages his congregation to live better than those who
think that they can get into heaven simply by believing in Jesus for eternal
life. Simply believing in Jesus for eternal life is not good enough according
to his sermons. A promise like Jn 3:16 is not good enough. An offsetting
promise like Neh 9:38 also must be made by those hoping to get to heaven.
A person must promise to live better than those who think that they can get
to heaven simply by believing the Lord’s promise. Faith is not enough. The
Lord’s promise is not enough. Your promise to do better and thus to be
better also is necessary. Your promise to outperform those who merely
believe the Lord becomes the proveitistic necessity.
       Contrary to my pastor, I do not believe that my going to heaven is
conditioned on my being better than them—regardless of how the them is
defined. I do not believe I have to live better than murderers, drunkards,
homosexuals, or even the devil in order to go to heaven. My going to heaven
has been secured unconditionally by the blood of Christ—independent of my
performance. Does this mean that I am going to live like the devil or start
killing people or start getting drunk or start engaging in homosexual
behavior? Of course not!
       The very week I am writing this note (and in which my pastor preached
this follow-up sermon), a friend of mine at work was expressing again his
shock that I have never taken a drink of beer or even tasted an alcoholic
beverage. I was raised a Baptist teetotaler and have no desire to turn my
Endnotes                                                               Page 141

back on that heritage. Nevertheless, I am not about to conclude that I am
going to heaven because I am a teetotaler.
      Sure, I believe that I will be rewarded in heaven for living in a manner
consistent with my particular Christian convictions on this matter. However,
my going to heaven is not a reward based on the way I live. By making
getting to heaven contingent on the way a person lives (and thus on being
better than the next person or some other person), our pastor, contrastingly,
has made heaven a reward based on the way the seemingly morally superior
person lives.
      Beware of those who make getting into heaven a footrace you win by
living better than the devil. The devil does not mind losing such a footrace if
that is what it takes to gain your soul. In fact, the devil is quite delighted to
lose that race in order to gain your soul, by suckering you into his counterfeit
gospel. Through his false prophets, he preaches and chides: “Come on, you
can beat me! Live better than the devil so that you can go to heaven! Surely
you are better than me!” Too many fools have taken the devil’s bet (and thus
bait) only to find out when they die that getting to heaven is not based on
living better than the devil or those who live like him.
      I was born in Georgia, so I will express my vantage point by modifying
Charlie Daniels’ Devil Went Down to Georgia as a starting point.

The devil went down to Georgia, he was looking for a soul to steal.
He was wanting to blind; he didn’t mind: he was willing to make a deal.
When he came across a young preacher, preaching hard against the devil’s sin,
the devil sat down on a pew up front and said, “Boy let me tell you what:
You might think you’re hot, but you are not. Give the devil his due.
I’ll bet my bow against your soul that I’ve got people better than you.”
The young boy said, “I’m a preacher of God, and you can’t win.
I’ll take your bet, and you’re going to regret, because I am better than them.”

With a wicked grin, the devil opened his show,
opening his case with the best of his best,
moralists and philanthropists wouldn’t you know.
The young preacher cringed; his mind began to spin.
How could he escape the jam he had put himself in?
The devil grinned, knowing he could lure him back in,
closing his case with drunkards and murderers on death row.

The young preacher laughed and started to crow:
“If that is all you’ve got, you’re going to lose.
I’m going so steal this show.
I’ve never been drunk, never even taken a drink.
I’ve never been on death row.
I’ve had my faults, I’ll admit.
But I ‘ve turned from my sin. I’m holy within.
Page 142                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
I’m not perfect, but I’m forgiven, so I’m going to heaven.
I’m covered; I’m washed; I’m whiter than snow.
Praise God, I’m better than the best you’ve got!

The preacher let out a shout, wiping the sweat from his brow.
He, the preacher, had won the show.
The devil he had beaten, or so the preacher thought,
as the devil put away his bow and bowed his head .
Little did the preacher know, hidden beneath the devil’s bow
was a sinister twist upon the devil’s brow,
a masking of the devil’s true intent.
The preacher had bought the devil’s show.
The devil had stolen the preacher’s soul.

The devil’s smirk and snicker were hidden from the preacher.
Not until later did the preacher realize that he was a sucker.
The devil intended to lose the wager,
if that is what it took to gain this preacher.

The devil had lost the bet or so the preacher thought,
until at last the preacher stood before the Lord.
In gambling with performance, the devil was not beaten.
His intent was merely hidden. The preacher he had kept from heaven.
The preacher had made a mistake when he made that bet.
He had taken the bait, hook, line, and sinker when he made that wager.

The person the preacher had to be better than
was not the devil’s pathetic comedians,
making fun of goody-two-shoed Christians.
The preacher had to be as good as the Lord,
Too late did the preacher realize that he was uncovered,
because he thought he had to be better than others,
rather than simply trust in the Lord.

Proveitistic preachers need to repent
of trying to be better than others
 as their hope of standing faultless before the Lord.
You can’t put on a show to show that you are better than others,
as your hope of entering the pearly gates of heaven.
To be whiter than snow, don’t put on a show.
You need the blood of the Lamb. It is all you need to show.

     The young preacher in the above illustration could not wholly lean on
Jesus’ name because he was trusting in his performance as a moral necessity
to get to heaven. For him, to lean on Jesus’ name meant to stand on his own
performance and to lean on Jesus for additional support. Instead, those
Endnotes                                                            Page 143

singing My Hope Is Built On Nothing Less should stand on Jesus’ blood and
righteousness alone as their solid rock of unconditional security and lean on
the promise of this irrevocable security as they face daily problems. But as
it is, performatists are not covered by the blood of the Lamb because that
blood does not cover those trying to get to heaven by outperforming the
devil (or his minions). They think they are standing on Christ when, in fact,
they are only leaning on Jesus for additional support.
                     Illustration 75. Better Than Others




      As a postscript to this endnote, I must interject that a few weeks later
(after this book already had been submitted for the first proofreading), on
sports recognition Sunday at our church, in which our church honors athletes
from the local high school, to his credit our pastor explicitly denied that
being a Christian is a matter of being better than everyone else. However,
even in this very sermon he made becoming a Christian a matter of giving
your life (and thus your performance) to Christ. Consequently, he
conditioned being a Christian on having Christ change your behavior—for
the better of course. Getting to heaven was made contingent on living better.
Better than whom? Better than yourself without Christ at the very least. By
logical extension, moreover, as is proven by his other sermons, he also
conditions reaching heaven on being better than those without Christ. Seeing
that even our own pastor is uncomfortable with the logical conclusions
necessitated by his own sermons, one should feel uneasy about adopting his
performatistic perspective.
      22
         One OSAS-U advocate appealed to Gal 4:27 as indicating that more
people will be in heaven than in hell. A possible OSAS-N rejoinder would
be to say that there will be more people who take the narrow gate than Jews
who do not. In other words, believers will outnumber unbelieving Jews.
      Other texts that might be used to support the OSAS-U contention that
salvation will be fairly universal among professing believers include the
following:
Page 144                                   Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
    •   Whoever (pas ho) denies the Son does not have the Father; the one
        (ho) who confesses the Son has the Father also (1Jn 2:23).
    •   Every (pan ho) spirit that confesses that Jesus Christ has come in
        the flesh is from God (1Jn 4:2).
    •   Whoever (hos) confesses that Jesus is the Son of God, God abides in
        him, and he in God (1Jn 4:15).

      Is John really affirming that anyone who at any time for any reason
confesses Jesus as God’s come-in-the-flesh Son proves himself or herself to
be a genuine believer? Such an interpretation is hard to accept since, among
other things, John also addresses the equally valid possibility of a person,
even a believer, denying Jesus (1Jn 2:23; 4:3). OSAS-U advocates
acknowledge, and rightly so, that genuine believers can, on occasion, deny
Christ. Making confession or denial of Christ absolute criteria by which to
determine who is a genuine believer is fundamentally unsound. Profession of
faith or confession of Christ does not prove regeneration. The narrow gate is
not easily opened to such a broad confession.
      Genuine believers can, at times, deny Christ. Pseudo believers, on the
other hand, can, in an orthodox manner, confess Christ. On such matters,
proveitists, forfeitists, and OSAS-N securitists are agreed. If OSAS-U
securitists wish to contend otherwise, they may find themselves playing a
solo act. Two possible rejoinders to a potential solo appeal by OSAS-U to
such passages may be made in passing.
      First, OSAS-N could contend that John is discussing confession that
presumes a salvifically narrow understanding of what it means for Jesus to
be the Christ. For example, Jesus, on occasion, presupposes that the person
making such a confession is a believer: “Everyone (pas) therefore who shall
confess Me before men, I will also confess him before My Father who is in
heaven” (Mt 10:32). From the nature of the affirmation, everyone is
understood to mean every believer. Like Jesus, John is not contemplating a
confession made in any context but a confession made in a context such as
what he/He has provided—a context in which sincerity and soteric accuracy
are a given.
      For example, John affirms: “We know that we abide in Him and He in
us, because He has given us of His Spirit” (1Jn 4:13). The us who are given
of His Spirit are those who have been given His Spirit (1Jn 3:24). By the
Spirit (given to us), we may know that we abide in Christ as we
experientially share in God’s spirit of love and in a confession made possible
to us by the Spirit who indwells us. To be given the Spirit is not sufficient
grounds for concluding that one abides in Christ in the manner envisioned in
1Jn 4:13. Those who have been given the Spirit must participate in the spirit
of the Spirit regarding love and confession. The potential abiders are
believers. John might well be understood, in this context at least, as saying,
“Whoever of us confesses that Jesus is the Son of God, God abides in him,
Endnotes                                                               Page 145

and he in God: (1Jn 4:15). Only those who have the Spirit can have the
Spirit’s spirit in this sense. Additionally, one might cite Paul’s statement in
Rom 10:9-16 as a conceptual parallel. Whoever confesses will be saved. But
in that Pauline context, the clarification is supplied that this whoever does
not refer to unbelievers, only to believers.
      Likewise, in an epistle where it is said that everyone who loves has
been born of God (1Jn 4:7), it must be admitted that everyone who loves his
brother in Christ is a genuine believer and that only genuine believers can
love their Christian brethren. In like manner, everyone who makes the type
of confession that John envisions conceivably might be understood as
having been born of God (and possibly also as loving God). John is not
affirming an abiding fellowship with God for all forms of loving and
confessing, only for those expressions that reflect the Spirit’s spirit by those
who have the anointing of the Spirit (1Jn 2:27) abiding in them ontologically
(1Jn 3:9). If so, experiential abiding is only entertained in the cases of those
who have ontological abiding. In other words, only those who have a Father-
child relationship can have this Father-child fellowship. Accordingly, if John
has narrowed the whoever in such passages to those who genuinely believe,
as opposed to the masses who merely profess to believe, then OSAS-U
cannot presume that an affirmation of OSAS is not part of the criteria which
narrows the validity of such a confession. This OSAS-N interpretation
counters an OSAS-U attempt to take whoever in a broad sense. These texts
do not prove that anyone who makes any confession of Christ has entered
the narrow gate required by Christ.
      Alternatively, some OSAS-N advocates might be so bold as to suggest
that John’s statements about whoever could be taken absolutely: Whoever
(whether believer or unbeliever) confesses Jesus as the come-in-the-flesh
Son of God is acting in harmony with God’s Spirit in making such a
confession and might be said to have (fellowship with) the Father (in terms
of that profession). If this is the case, unbelievers who confess faith in Christ
would be demonstrating a spirit that is from God (as the ultimate source of
such a profession) and mutually abiding in God (to the degree that their lips
and lives reflect that confession). Through God’s enabling grace, even the
lost can make a profession of Christ and live lives that, to a considerable
degree, are in harmony with God’s will. If this understanding of John’s
statements is entertained, Paul’s statement in 1Cor 12:3 might be considered
a conceptual parallel: “Therefore I make known to you, that no one speaking
by the Spirit of God says, ‘Jesus is accursed’; and no one can say, ‘Jesus is
Lord,’ except by the Holy Spirit.” Just as the profession that Jesus is Lord
shows the influence of the Spirit, so the confession of Jesus as Christ shows
a harmony with the Spirit—even if the person making such a confession is
lost. Not everyone who makes such a profession of Jesus as Lord is saved or
has entered the narrow gate (Mt 7:13-23). Rather, as every lie can be said to
find its source in the devil (1Jn 3:8), so every confession of Jesus as Christ
Page 146                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
finds its source in the Spirit and finds itself in harmony, to some degree,
with God’s affirmation. Could it be that just as the synoptic gospels entertain
the possibility of unbelievers finding harmony with God via repentance as a
potential precursor to saving faith that this Johannine epistle on fellowship
allows the possibility of unbelievers experiencing some degree of harmony
with God via confession of Christ?
      John affirms that it is possible to have (echo) the Father and the Son,
the Son of God, and God (1Jn 2:23; 5:12; 2Jn 1:9). To have someone can
mean to have a relationship and/or fellowship with that person, to have a
union and/or communion with that person. In 1Jn 2:23 and 2Jn 1:9,
fellowship (i.e., communion) is being affirmed or denied; whereas, in
1Jn 5:12 relationship (i.e., union) is being affirmed or denied. BDAG rightly
lists 1Jn 2:23 and 2Jn 1:9 as denoting a relationship of communion in its
definition of have (echo) and omits a reference to 1Jn 5:12 in this list. The
relationship described in1Jn 5:12 would be one of union.
      A popular distinction between relationship and fellowship is useful to
delineate these two aspects. For the broadening of the soteric gate to all
professors by OSAS-U to be meaningful, (1) confession would have to
prove fellowship, and (2) fellowship would have to prove relationship.
      The first proposition is open to question in that a person could confess
and yet not love. What would this prove about the person’s profession?

    •   The one who loves his brother abides in the light and there is no
        cause for stumbling in him. But the one who hates his brother is in
        the darkness and walks in the darkness, and does not know where he
        is going because the darkness has blinded his eyes. (1Jn 2:10-11).
    •   We know that we have passed out of death into life, because we
        love the brethren. He who does not love abides in death. Everyone
        who hates his brother is a murderer; and you know that no murderer
        has eternal life abiding in him. We know love by this, that He laid
        down His life for us; and we ought to lay down our lives for the
        brethren. But whoever has the world's goods, and beholds his
        brother in need and closes his heart against him, how does the love
        of God abide in him? (1Jn 3:14-17)
    •    No one has beheld God at any time; if we love one another, God
        abides in us, and His love is perfected in us. (1Jn 4:12)

     Loving one’s brother in Christ is certainly a necessary condition for
abiding in Christ. If these verses are read in isolation, loving one’s Christian
brethren also appears to be a sufficient condition for abiding in Christ. Of
course, this presupposes that one is a believer. After all, John is talking
about loving one’s believing brethren. Granted, John also may be limiting
his discussion of confession (much as he does love) to those who are
regenerate. Just as he would not mean that everyone on the planet who loves
Endnotes                                                             Page 147

(in the broad sense of the word) is born of God (1Jn 4:7), so he should not be
understood as thinking that everyone who confesses Christ (in any sense of
the word) is born of God. If this line of reasoning is followed, then it stands
to reason that John is limiting the confession he has in mind to that made by
believers who are loving.
      The second proposition is open to objection in that John might be open
to the suggestion that even the lost can experience some degree of abiding in
fellowship with God. One well-known Free Grace commentator who limits
the scope of confession made in 1Jn 4:15 to Christians who are regenerate
and loving nevertheless acknowledges that being of God in 1Jn 5:19 can
apply in various degrees to regenerate believers. If being of God can occur in
various degrees, then would it not stand to reason that having fellowship
with God could vary in accordance with how well one’s confession matches
one’s performance? And if this is the case, could it be said that even
unbelievers who demonstrate a degree of love and confession have some
degree of fellowship with God? Indeed, the Free Grace commentator to
whom I alluded is well known to have advanced the thought that unbelievers
can have some form of fellowship/harmony with God via repentance;
although, in a subsequent publication he said that this is not true fellowship
(compared with the personal harmony with God experienced by the saved).
      The proposal being entertained herein is that both perspectives are
accurate. The potential for true fellowship is limited to true believers.
Estranged fellowship is open to non-believers. Jesus was a friend of sinners
(Mt 9:10-13; 11:19; Mk 2:15-17; Lk 5:29-32; 7:34; 15:1ff). Some of these
sinners were simply table diners with Jesus. Others may have been
unfledged disciples. In any event, Jesus practiced friendship evangelism,
extending His friendship to those who needed to make an evangelistic
response but who had not yet done so. In truth, He was a friend of sinners,
even unsaved sinners, yet the friendship He experienced with them was
strained in that their sins still separated them from Him at the soteric core.
      The most notorious case in point would be that of Judas. As John
relates, the OT text fulfilled by Judas in his betrayal of Jesus was Ps 41:9:
“Even my close friend, in whom I trusted, who ate my bread, has lifted up
his heel against me.” Jesus and Judas were friends. Jesus trusted in Judas,
and Judas trusted in Jesus. Have you ever had to befriend someone whom
you suspected was stabbing you in the back? Jesus was in a similar situation.
He knew that Judas would betray Him (Jn 6:64,71), yet Jesus befriended
Judas. This fellowship was sweet to Judas but bitter-sweet to Jesus. Before it
was over, it became bitter-sweet to Judas also. As even some well-known
proveitists have acknowledged, in all probability Judas initially thought
himself to be a believer in Jesus.
      Judas believed in Jesus in terms of discipleship. Judas had given up
everything to follow Jesus. As a result, Judas was performing miracles by
the power of Jesus’ name and was experiencing fellowship with Jesus. Judas
Page 148                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
had the proof that proveitists demand as proof that one is a genuine believer.
But he did not genuinely believe in Christ for eternal life. The supposition
taken herein is that Judas did not believe in Christ alone for eternal life as a
gift; otherwise, he would have had eternal life. Consequently, Judas proves
that OSAS-U is false. Not all faith in Christ is saving faith.
      The most reasonable hypothesis is that, like the proveitists of today,
Judas based his assurance of salvation on his fellowship with Jesus—a
fellowship that Jesus had taught was conditioned on performance. By basing
his salvation from hell on his fellowship and his fellowship on his
performance, Judas was basing his salvation from hell, in part, on his
performance. Thus, like today’s proveitists, Judas lacked eternal life as a gift
because he had never received it as a gift. The life (i.e., fellowship with
Jesus and knowing Jesus) that Judas was experiencing was not grounded in
his possession of the gift of eternal life.
      Fellowship should be based on relationship, not relationship on
fellowship. Even unbelievers can experience a level of fellowship with Jesus
that is indistinguishable, by human perception, from the fellowship
experienced by genuine believers. Estranged fellowship may not necessarily
feel strange to both parties—the Lord may be the only one feeling the
incompatibility of the partnership. However, when the lost stand at last
before the Lord, they will find that the fellowship they experienced with
Him was never based on a saving relationship with Him (Mt 7:23).
      Light cannot have true fellowship with darkness. Even if the darkness
imagines itself in true fellowship with the light and even if the light tries to
embrace the darkness, they still are “unequally yoked” (2Cor 6:14; ESV).
Satan has blinded the eyes of many unbelievers into thinking that they are
believers because they are experiencing estranged fellowship with the Lord
and mistaking it for genuine fellowship. They are clueless since they have
based their relationship with Christ on their fellowship with Christ, and thus
on their performance. Just as a Christian befriending a lost person does not
make that lost person a Christian, so Christ befriending a legalist does not
make that legalist a believer. In the end, just as Judas betrayed Christ with a
kiss of death, so proveitism betrays Christianity with a kiss of legalism.
      In the first OSAS-N interpretation proposed above, the well-known
truth is affirmed: Relationship does not guarantee fellowship. For example,
just because two people are married does not mean that they have
fellowship. The alternative OSAS-N proposal inversely suggests that, to a
degree at least, a person might have fellowship without having a
relationship. For example, just because two people have fellowship, this
does not mean that they have a marital relationship.
      John records an occasion in which Jesus encountered a woman at a well
who had marital relations with five husbands and was having sexual
relations with a sixth man (Jn 4:17-18). She knew the difference between
having a relationship and having fellowship with a person. So did John. In
Endnotes                                                               Page 149

the three texts under discussion (1Jn 2:23; 4:2; 4:15), and in this text as well,
the Greek word John uses for have is echo, and what is had is a
Person\person. The same word regarding having relations (echo) might be
used to encompass both possibilities—relationship and fellowship—without
implying that one assures the other. Just as having relations with a person
does not necessarily mean having a relationship with that person, so having
a person does not necessarily mean to have a relationship with that person.
Fellowship might be meant instead. The woman at the well was having
sexual relations/fellowship with her live-in boyfriend devoid of a marital
relationship.
      Communion/fellowship is experienced in degrees. The degree and
consistency to which a person confesses Christ will determine, to a
considerable degree, the fellowship that person has with Christ. To some
extent, the one does not guarantee the other. Relationship does not
necessarily imply fellowship, and fellowship may not necessarily prove
relationship.
      If this alternative OSAS-N interpretation of these three Johannine texts
is accepted, then it is exceedingly precarious for goats to presume that their
fellowship with God proves that they have a relationship with God. The lost
can do things that are in harmony with God’s will and, to some extent,
experience harmony with God as a result.
      Regardless, the entire proveitist approach to 1John suffers a serious
setback in that even proveitists do not believe that everyone who confesses
Christ necessarily is born again. So much for using these confessional texts
to determine if someone is born again! Even more telling, John exhorts these
forgiven little children to abide in the Lord so that they may not be
shamefully put away from Him. See 1Jn 2:12,28 (TM). Therefore,
experiential abiding cannot be the criteria by which to determine who is a
genuine believer when those who know that they are genuine believers are
exhorted to abide—much less when the possibility of failure is entertained
and the consequences are meted out at the bema after the rapture
(presupposing a dispensational understanding of the latter point). Unfaithful
believers will be put away shamefully in the outer darkness (on the outskirts
of the kingdom).
      As an aside, John seems to pose multiple ways of abiding experientially
in God—or at least of knowing that one is abiding. Loving one’s brother in
Christ is a necessary and sufficient condition for abiding in Christ (1Jn 2:10-
11; 3:14-17; 4:12). Confessing Jesus as the Christ also seems to be a
necessary and sufficient condition for abiding. But how can multiple
necessary and sufficient conditions exist for the same result? Different
stratum or spheres of abiding would seem to be in view.
      For instance, what are we to make of those cases in which someone
confesses that Jesus is the Christ yet does not love his brother? According to
one set of texts, it might be argued that such a person abides in Christ
Page 150                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
because he confesses Christ. On the other hand, judging from another set of
texts, this unloving believer does not abide in Christ because he does not
love his brother in Christ. What about someone who has the Spirit but who,
at times, denies Christ or sometimes does not love his brother? Such a
person might be regarded as abiding by some texts but not abiding by other
texts. How is it possible to abide yet not abide in the same Person at the
same time if not in different degrees or stratum?
      The most straightforward deduction is that such a person would both
abide in Christ yet not abide in Christ. Logically, this is impossible if abide
always is taken as a degreeless monolithic absolute in which one either
abides in Christ (absolutely) or one does not abide in Christ (at all).
However, such an absolute conclusion is unreasonable. Biblical evidence
