and everyday experience concur that some people simultaneously (at least
partially) meet one necessary and sufficient condition for abiding but fail
(partially or completely) to meet another equally valid necessary and
sufficient condition for abiding. Induction leads to the conclusion that one
can abide in Christ in one degree, stratum, or subsphere yet not abide in
Christ in relation to another degree, stratum, or subsphere.
      Exegetically, the stratum approach is suggested by Peter’s description
of developing Christian character in terms of addition:

       Now for this very reason also, do your very best to
       • add to your faith, moral virtue
       • and to your moral virtue, knowledge;
       • and to your knowledge, self-control,
       • and to your self-control, perseverance,
       • and to your perseverance, godliness;
       • and to your godliness, brotherly kindness,
       • and to your brotherly kindness, love (2Pet 1:5-7; TM).

A progression is indicated. Love is the goal (the end product) of this Petrine
list. Consequently, in comparing the Pauline list in Gal 5:22-23, and noting
that it begins with love, one might suspect that he has listed love first as
being most important. Yet one might conclude, in deference to Peter, that
love is the most difficult to perfect. Consequently, one might not stray too
far to suggest that some believers may do better in some of these qualities
than in others. Like a child who might come home with a report card
showing an A in English, a B in math, and a C in science, some Christians
might rate an A in knowledge, a B in godliness, and only a C in love. They
are abiding in Christ at different levels and at different stratum.
      Correspondingly, confession results in harmony with God regarding
confession. It does not result in harmony with God regarding loving the
brethren. One action does not substitute for the other. One cannot make up
for not loving the brethren by confessing Christ more.
Endnotes                                                               Page 151

      To claim that all believers are walking equally in harmony with God in
all areas of their lives is devoid of realism. To contend that all unbelievers
are walking in equal disharmony with God in all areas of their lives is
unrealistic as well. One could be (more) in harmony with God in one area
but (more) out of harmony with God in another area.
      Beware of confusing harmony with progeny! Just because a wolf in
sheep’s clothing may be very much in harmony with God in one area of his
or her life (such as living a morally decent life), this does not mean that he or
she is in harmony with God in another area—such as believing in Christ
alone for eternal life.
      As an additional aside, in relation to the Matthean text regarding
whoever confesses cited above, note that Jesus proceeds with a warning that
is applicable to believers in terms of rewards pertaining to whoever denies:
“But whoever shall deny Me before men, I will also deny him before My
Father who is in heaven” (Mt 10:33). Likewise, the negative aspect of John’s
statement in 1Jn 2:23a (regarding the denial of Christ by whoever) has
application to believers. A relative whoever would limit the application to
believers. An absolute whoever broadens it to unbelievers. In either case,
believers are included in the application and are the focal point of the
warning a few verses later in 1Jn 2:28.
      The stated eschatological purpose in the Johannine epistles for abiding
is so that believers may not be put away shamefully from the Lord when He
returns. Specifically, they are exhorted to abide and thereby develop a
mature love in order that (hina), they may have confidence rather than
experience shame on the day of judgment (1Jn 2:28; 4:17). This
eschatological use of hina is resumed in 2Jn 1:8: “Watch yourselves, in
order that [hina] you might not lose what we have accomplished, but in
order that [elliptical hina] you may receive a full reward” (TM). Although
hina is used to express temporal purposes in the Johannine epistles, the
eschatological purpose conveyed by hina in these epistles is exclusively
misthological.
      Whatever the scope of the much debated temporal use of hina in
1Jn 5:13, concerning one’s present assurance of eternal life, the
eschatological scope of hina is clearly misthological rather than
soteriological. The Johannine telescope is focused on rewards. Those who
insist that the focus of the epistle is on who will make it to heaven have
pointed their telescope in the wrong direction or, at least, have misaligned
their lenses. The eschatological lenses within this telescope are focused, by
John, on a misthological horizon.
      23
         The majority of Free Grace soteriologists tends to agree with the
majority view held throughout the history of Christendom by regarding the
entrance in Mt 7:21 as soteric. Some very astute Free Grace misthologists,
on the other hand, believe that the entrance in Mt 7:21 is mistholic. As a
Free Grace misthologist, I certainly am sympathetic to the mistholic
Page 152                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
interpretation adopted by some of my peers, although not to all permutations
of it. A basic misthological formulation could offer this simple translation:
“Not everyone who says to Me, ‘Lord, Lord,’ will enter the kingship
[basileia] of heaven; but he who does the will of My Father who is in
heaven” (Mt 7:21). Ethical righteousness would be necessary for entrance
into the kingship. Those believers who fall short of that ethical righteousness
would be expelled into the outer darkness (Mt 7:23).
      Certainly, I have no objection to such an interpretation conceptually or
linguistically. Indeed, some passages where this mistholic translation would
be preferable have been indicated in the body of this present book. See
kingship in the index. Also, in The Outer Darkness I provide passages where
this interpretation verifiably is necessary. At the same time, for reasons
advanced therein and elsewhere, I do not find this interpretation to be the
most likely one in Mt 7:21-23. If the leading Free Grace soteriologists who
hold OSAS-U are left with advocating a minority view regarding the nature
of this entrance so as to consider it mistholic, their doing so is
counterintuitive to their appealing to the majority view as a basis for finding
OSAS unnecessary.
      Additionally, they still would have to explain the presence of birds,
tares, and leaven in the kingdom. If these are mistholic personages, how will
conservative misthologists explain the casting of the tares into the
eschatological fire (Mt 13:40). Oppositely, if these parabolic figures pertain
to soteric themes, as seems to be the case, then how does OSAS-U explain
the unregenerate status of such people after OSAS-U has opened up
regeneration to practically everyone within professing Christendom? For
that matter, it would seem that OSAS-U opens up the doors of heaven to the
orthodox and heterodox alike.
      24
         This statement about Christ’s present intercession is made more so in
way of response to forfeitists; nevertheless, it also has applicability to other
forms of legalism. Some forfeitists would argue that if Christ’s present
intercessory ministry is necessary to secure our eternal redemption, then
what He did for us on the cross is not enough to secure it. Logically, the
point is valid—if His present ministry actually is necessary soteriologically.
However, this forfeitist argument overlooks two things. First, the difference
between necessary and sufficient causation versus simple sufficient
causation should not be missed. Jesus’ death on the cross was a necessary
and sufficient cause for our eternal redemption. In terms of having done
everything that was objectively necessary to procure our redemption,
everything necessary for this finished security was completed on the cross.
Even if one accepts the perspective that the Lord’s present intercessory
ministry helps to keep us saved, it is not because of some deficiency in what
already has been accomplished on the cross. Rather, His ongoing ministry
could be viewed as a sufficient, rather than necessary, condition for eternal
redemption.
Endnotes                                                              Page 153

      By way of rough analogy, if you thermo-seal a letter in plastic and then
seal this plastic-coated letter in a waterproof vault, then thermo-sealing it
was unnecessary to keep it dry. Nonetheless, thermo-sealing it is a sufficient
condition to keep it dry. A logician would criticize this analogy because the
vault would not be necessary. However, if the person doing the thermo-
sealing will not do so without sealing the letter in the vault, then sealing the
letter in the vault is necessary and sufficient while the thermo-seal is merely
sufficient. Notwithstanding the possible adequacy of this modified analogy, I
only provide this analogy for the sake of argument. The statement in
Heb 7:25 might be understood better misthologically rather than
soteriologically: “He is able to [misthologically] save forever those who
draw near to God through Him, since He always lives to make intercession
for them.” To be sure, Heb 6:18 provides a reference to double security. Yet
it too might be taken better misthologically: “In order that by two
unchangeable things, in which it is impossible for God to lie, we may have
strong encouragement, we who have fled for refuge in laying hold of the
[misthological] hope set before us.” Nevertheless, if double security can be
made misthologically, then no logical reason exits to suppose that it cannot
be made soteriologically.
      Second, the whole premise that Jesus’ present ministry on our behalf
includes some activity for the purpose of soteric security is subject to
strenuous objection. GNTC clearly (and I think rightly) rejects such a notion
(pp. 1060, 1197). Not only is the soteric foundation finished, Jesus is seated.
In contrast to some securitists, I am inclined to think that Jesus’ prayer that
the Father would keep us (Jn 17:11,15) has applicability to our unconditional
security. Nevertheless, even if this prayer is part of His provision of
unconditional security to the NT believer, this prayer was made before His
death. This prayer is part of the life that He lived for us in order to save us
from hell, and this prayer was made effective through His death. In
theological terms, we are saved from hell not only by the passive obedience
of His death but also by the active obedience of the life He lived while on
earth. This prayer is part of that active obedience—an obedience which
already has been completed soteriologically. Therefore, I am inclined to
agree with those securitists who say that Jesus’ prayer keeps us saved.
However, I am also willing to concede that the dimension of His prayer life
that keeps us saved from hell is that dimension of His prayer life that He
prayed for us before His death. He no longer has any need to offer such a
prayer today. At most, if any such prayer still occurs today, it is not out of
necessity and can only supply additional security.
      At least two primary texts might be marshaled against the perspective
that everything that was objectively necessary for our justification was
completed with Christ’s death. Both verses seem to suggest that Christ’s
death was not enough to accomplish our justification. His resurrection was
also soteriologically necessary: “If Christ has not been raised, your faith is
Page 154                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
worthless; you are still in your sins” (1Cor 15:17). Christ was “raised for our
justification” (Rom 4:25; ESV). In some sense, Christ’s resurrection was
necessary for our justification.
      The interpretation of this second passage is a center of considerable
controversy. Commentators generally opt for a variation of one of three
interpretations that revolve around how to translate the parallel usages of dia
in the first and second halves of the verse:

    (1) Retrospective dia that looks back in both halves:
        • He who was delivered up because of our transgressions,
                 and was raised up because of our justification.
    (2) Prospective dia that looks forward in both halves:
        • He who was delivered up for our transgressions,
                 and was raised up for our justification.
    (3) Retrospective dia followed by prospective dia:
        • He who was delivered up because of our transgressions,
                 and was raised up for our justification.

Dia normally is used retrospectively (eis would have been the more natural
candidate had Paul wished to speak prospectively). Therefore, I am quite
content with interpretation number one:

                         Because we sinned, Jesus died.
                       Because we are justified, Jesus arose.

The sequence is: justification → resurrection. Yet more than mere sequence
is implied. Indeed, some commentators adopting this interpretation would
say that the resurrection was a necessary effect or at least an inevitable
consequence: justification ⇒ resurrection.
        Other commentators, who prefer one of the latter two other options,
find it difficult to understand why the resurrection would be necessary or
inevitable if justification already was accomplished objectively before the
resurrection took place. In other words, since everything objectively
necessary for our justification was completed by Jesus’ crucifixion, why
would the resurrection still be objectively necessary? Did Jesus mean, “It is
finished…except I have to rise from the dead”? No. Nine versus later, Paul
will confirm that believers objectively have been “justified by His blood”
(Rom 5:9). Naturally, when Jesus said, “It is finished” (Jn 19:30), He was
speaking only of everything being finished objectively. He had done His part
in finishing the basis for our justification. The subjective response of saving
faith was still necessary on our part.
        This observation is not lost on commentators since they point out that
all earlier references to justification in the epistle before Rom 4:25 refer to
justification by faith (i.e., subjective justification). Consequently, some
Endnotes                                                                Page 155

commentators are inclined to adopt either the second or third interpretation
for this verse as well: Jesus was raised for our subjective justification. In
other words, His resurrection is the proof that is normally necessary to cause
us to believe in the objective justification that He accomplished for us. This
objective resurrection is signified by the reference to “by His blood” (to use
Paul’s words) and by the statement “it is finished” (to use Jesus’ words).
Jesus’ resurrection is causal, not becausal. The Lord’s resurrection is not
necessary for our objective justification but for our subjective justification
(so EBC). Jesus’ resurrection is a practical necessity for us to believe in
what He accomplished for us on the cross. The basis for our subjective
justification has been laid by His resurrection; the objective basis for our
justification has been completed by the crucifixion.
        Accordingly, the prospective view of dia in either of these latter two
interpretations might be considered viable—if it were not for 1Cor 15:17.
However, I fail to see how these latter explanations would do justice to this
cross reference. If the resurrection is only pragmatically necessary for the
subjective response of faith, why is it that a subjective response of faith
would be nullified by the lack of an objective physical resurrection?
Apparently, an objective physical resurrection is somehow necessary for an
objective justification.
        As a result, I will maintain my preference for interpretation number
one (and its retrospective view of dia) since this interpretation seems to
accord better with 1Cor 15:17. Accordingly, Rom 4:25b could be
represented symbolically as: objective justification ⇒ resurrection. The
contrapositive would be: ; resurrection ⇒ ; objective justification. If
there is no physical resurrection, then there is no objective justification.
After all, that appears to be what Paul is saying: “If Christ has not been
raised, your faith is worthless; you are still in your sins” (1Cor 15:17; cp.
1Cor 15:14). Thus, I would regard 1Cor 15:17 as the logical contrapositive
of Rom 4:25b. Consequently, Rom 4:25 could be regarded as a hinge verse
between subjective and objective justification. Although all previous
references in the epistle are to subjective justification, objective justification
follows a few verses after this transitional verse.
        The dilemma that remains to be explained is how the Lord’s active
and passive obedience that culminated in the crucifixion could be the sole
condition for our objective justification and the resurrection a necessary
condition of our objective justification. Actually, this assessment contains
the solution. The resurrection is not a condition for objective justification;
rather, the resurrection is only a necessary condition of objective
justification. God does not need this vindication of course. His knowledge of
our justification is not dependent upon the resurrection. Nevertheless, He
needed to give the resurrection (in order to accomplish other objectives).
        When swiping my debit card to purchase an item at the grocery store,
the printout of the receipt is an inevitable consequence of the transaction
Page 156                                     Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
going through. I may not take the receipt, but it is printed out just the same.
The clerk has some compulsion to offer me the receipt whether or not I take
it. For reasons perhaps not altogether apparent to us (or for reasons that
perhaps can be gleaned from the contexts of Paul’s statements as to why
Christ’s resurrection is important for postconversional purposes), God,
likewise, is under compulsion (of His own making for other objectives that
He wishes to accomplish pursuant to our justification) to offer us the receipt
of the resurrection. He would not accept the payment without offering the
receipt (since this receipt serves a further purpose). If there is no receipt, the
payment did not go through because God would not accept the payment
without giving a public physical vindication. Justification is not an end to
itself. It serves much greater purposes: glorification and co-glorification in
resurrected bodies. These greater purposes require giving of the receipt.
        The resurrection is an objective receipt that proves that the objective
debt has been paid. The resurrection is not part of the payment.
Nevertheless, the resurrection is indispensible proof of the payment. Just as
heat is a necessary effect of fire, the resurrection is a necessary consequence
of justification.

     •     Successful purchase ⇒ printed receipt.
     •     Objective justification ⇒ physical resurrection.

        Of course, Jesus’ sacrifice is also the basis for our sanctification as
well. His work as High Priest still continues, but it is based upon and made
possible because “it is finished.” Hence, Jesus is not working still to
validate, prove, or complete His work on the cross relative to our
justification unto the gift dimension of eternal life. Rather, because His
work at the cross “is finished,” His is presently working out the power of His
resurrected life in our lives so that we may qualify for co-rulership with Him
in heaven. Because work # 1 (the cross) is finished and work #2 (His work
as High Priest—which requires our work/cooperation with Him) is still
occurring, work # 3b (co-glorification) is made possible.
        Work #1 necessarily results in work #3a (glorification) because work
#2 is not necessary for glorification. The question will be posed at this
juncture: “Did Jesus have to be raised from the dead in order for us to have
glorified bodies and thus for work #1 to have its fully intended effect?”
Certainly from texts such as 1Cor 15:44,53, one would answer affirmatively.
For the stinger of death to be removed, the resurrection of our physical
bodies is necessary (1Cor 15:56). But why would God not just allow death
to have our bodies and be satisfied with saving our spirits? Because that
would have deprived us of our power for living the power of Christ’s
resurrected life in our physical bodies and of the ultimate reason for living
for Christ in these physical bodies.
Endnotes                                                               Page 157

        Christ’s resurrected life provides the power for our sanctification
(Rom 6:4-11) and ultimately for misthological life (Rom 6:22). His death is
our death (Gal 2:20; Col 3:3). His life is our life (Rom 6:6; 2Cor 13:4;
2Tim 2:11). His resurrection is our resurrection (Eph 2:6; Col 2:13; 3:1). His
ascension is our ascension (Eph 2:6). His rulership is our rulership
(Rom 8:17b; 2Tim 2:12).
        Justification is just the seed. Co-glorification is the mature fruit. God
planted the seed with the intent of reaping mature fruit (Mk 4:26-29). A
farmer does not plant a seed for the purpose merely of having it germinate
underground. Rather, the ultimate purpose is so that the seed may bear
mature fruit. Although the ideal may not be realized in all the seed that is
planted, this does not prevent the farmer from planting the seed or detract
from his purpose in planting the seed.
        God’s original purpose for humanity was that humanity might rule
this physical universe in physical bodies. To accomplish that objective (and
thus prevent His original purpose from being thwarted), He had to redeem
our physical bodies. As this could not be done independently of Christ, the
resurrection of Christ was necessary. See must (dei) in Lk 9:22; 24:7, also in
Lk 24:46 (MT). The resurrection of Christ was not necessary for our
objective justification (thus not necessary for the germination: the
impartation of regeneration and the salvation of our metaphysical spirit);
however, the resurrection was absolutely indispensible for our co-
glorification with Christ in glorified physical bodies. God would not provide
a plan of salvation that allowed our bodies to remain unredeemed. Hence,
the resurrection of Christ is a necessary part of God’s redemptive plan. No
resurrection, then no redemption.
        The mature fruit is not necessary for the germination of the seed that
the farmer planted (and likewise the resurrection is not necessary for
justification)—except for the fact that the farmer never would have planted
the seed were it not for the expectation of mature fruit (and God never would
have allowed Jesus to die physically for us were it not for the expectation of
our ruling physically with Jesus). From the perspective of the exception, the
resurrection was a necessity.
        When Jesus cried, “It is finished!” the seed was planted. Everything
necessary for germination had been completed. The grain of wheat had
fallen into the earth (Jn 12:24). Nevertheless, for it to grow up and produce
mature fruit, that germination below the ground would have to sprout above
the ground. While the sprouting above the ground admittedly does prove that
a sprouting has occurred below the ground, the sprouting above the ground
does so much more. The ultimate purpose of the sprouting above the ground
is not to prove that a sprouting has occurred below the ground but to provide
the means of achieving the ultimate objective: mature fruit. The primary
purpose of the resurrection is not to provide vindication (although it does do
that beyond question, Rom 1:4); rather, the primary objective of the
Page 158                                    Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
resurrection is to provide everything necessary (including vindication) that is
required for our maturation. Ultimately, Jesus was raised bodily from the
dead so that we might co-rule with Him in glorified bodies.
                 Illustration 76. Justification  Resurrection




          To summarize the above presentation in terms of the illustration,
because we are justified objectively Jesus rose physically (justification ⇒
resurrection). This summation addresses the logical sequence expressed in
Rom 4:24. Just as the grain of wheat died before it could bear fruit, so Jesus
died before He could provide life and produce fruit (Jn 12:24). He had to
accomplish our objective justification before He could provide us spiritual
life (a logical ordo salutis). The crucifixion had to take place before there
could be a resurrection (a natural ordo salutis). Germination must occur
before there can be maturation. On the other hand, if there was no
resurrection, then there never would have been a crucifixion. In terms of
purpose, God never would have sent His Son to die, if He had no intention
of raising Him from the dead. If there was no resurrection, then there never
would have been any justification (; resurrection ⇒ ; justification). This
summation addresses the contraposition of 1Cor 15:17 where the apodosis
(i.e., the end result, the purpose) is placed first. The ultimate purpose of the
crucifixion was not our justification but our full (and thus bodily)
redemption and maturation (pleres, Mk 4:28).
       25
          The basic premise of a theology of inconsistency is accurate: One can
believe savingly in the gospel without being logically consistent in all
aspects of one’s theology. A person may believe in Jesus as Christ without
fully understanding everything involved. For example, believing in Jesus for
eternal life and yet not believing in His deity is logically inconsistent. When
the logical implications of the various pieces of information are sorted out, it
becomes apparent (to orthodox theologians at least) that Jesus had to be God
in the flesh in order to make a legitimate offer of eternal life. Yet many of
Endnotes                                                               Page 159

His early followers savingly believed in Jesus for eternal life while failing to
grasp the full implications of His divinity.
     Jesus is Lord. A person does not have to understand the full
implications of His Lordship in order to believe in Jesus for eternal life.
Unmediated logical consistency would lead to the conclusion that believing
in Jesus for the gift of eternal life makes no sense if one does not also
believe in Jesus for the reward of eternal life. He promised eternal life in
both capacities. It would make no sense to believe in His offer of the gift of
eternal life and disbelieve His offer regarding the reward of eternal life.
Logical consistency dictates both propositions stand or fall together for those
who have heard both propositions. The only reasonable (i.e., logical) thing
for believers to do is to submit their lives to the Lordship of Christ:

      I urge you therefore, brethren, by the mercies of God, to present
      your bodies a living and holy sacrifice, acceptable to God,
      which is your logical service of worship. And do not be
      conformed to this world, but be transformed by the renewing of
      your mind, that you may prove what the will of God is, that
      which is good and acceptable and perfect (Rom 12:1-2; TM).

      Based on the information Paul has just provided these believers, he
urges them to submit their performance to the Lordship of Christ because it
is the logical thing to do. Hence, if one is to be logically consistent with the
full biblical message, one must submit to the Lordship of Christ. Logical
consistency demands it. The Lord Jesus, however, does not limit His
promise of the gift of eternal life to those who are logically consistent to that
degree of consistency. Otherwise, He would have to make receiving the gift
contingent on earning the reward! Logically, salvation by grace apart form
works would be impossible.
      Similarly, it logically was inconsistent for Jesus’ early followers to
believe in Him for eternal life but not believe what He told them about the
necessity of His dying on the cross. Logically, He could not offer them
eternal life without dying on the cross. The Bible demonstrates the logical
consistency of both propositions, while at the same time showing that
believing all the logical implications is not soteriologically necessary. His
early followers believed savingly in Him for eternal life while disbelieving
that He would die on the cross. Thankfully, Jesus does not demand that we
get all our theological ducks in a row in order to be saved. None of us are
good enough logically so as to qualify soteriologically if that were the case.
      To illustrate but one small facet of the complexity, (1) some within the
Free Grace camp believe that in the logical order (ordo salutis) of events
that take place at the moment of saving faith, regeneration proceeds
justification; (2) some believe that justification proceeds regeneration;
(3) some believe there is no logical order. I prefer the second approach.
Page 160                                       Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep
Even supposing that I am correct in deducing that justification proceeds
regeneration (in terms of logical as opposed to temporal sequence), does one
have to understand that Jesus proleptically justified those who believed in
Him for eternal life so that they were justified before they were
regenerated—even before He died on the cross? Of course not. Logical
consistency cannot be pressed to the point of making the gate so narrow that
no one can enter it.
      Nevertheless, a theology of inconsistency cannot be expanded infinitely
so as to pose that a biblical affirmation of saving faith embraces those who
believe that eternal life is a free gift that cost them anything (much less
everything). The Bible is clear that believing in Christ savingly must be done
exclusively without any belief in one’s activity as a soteriological necessity.
In other words, believing savingly in Christ for the gift of eternal life
necessarily rules out trusting simultaneously in one’s works for this gift. One
cannot be saved by believing in Jesus and in one’s works for the free gift of
eternal life. A theology of inconsistency must be counterbalanced with a
theology of exclusivity.
      26
         Surely, the Galatians who were charged with having fallen from
grace still believed that faith was necessary. They just did not believe that
faith was enough (Gal 5:4). Certainly, the Jews who failed to gain
righteousness because they sought it by works (Rom 9:30-33) thought that
faith was necessary as well to please God. Had they not read the OT
(Gen 15:6; Num 14:11; 20:12; Ps 78:22, 32; 106:12,24; Is 7:9; 28:16; Jonah
3:5; Hab 2:4)? Yet they failed to gain righteousness because they sought it
by works. Paul did not need to specify that they were seeking it by faith plus
works because (by adding works to faith) they had made faith a work. To
believe in a salvation conditioned on faith plus works is to disbelieve a
salvation that is freely granted apart from works. Believing works to be
necessary is to disbelieve that faith is sufficient. To believe in faith plus
works for soteric entrance is to disbelieve the gospel (Gal 1:8-9).
      27
         Faith does not have to be 100% of all that it could be in every aspect
in order to be saving. However, what faith that exists must be 100% free of
trust in one’s performance as a soteriological necessity in order for faith to
result in salvation from hell. Saving faith must be purely in Christ (rather
than partially in one’s performance) for the gift of eternal life.
      28
         The very day in which I included the rough draft for this second
appendix, our pastor made believing in Jesus for the gift of eternal life
equivalent to serving Jesus with one’s life. (This was prior to the Easter
service in which he did so again.) This book does not address a hypothetical
vexation but a tragic distortion that reoccurs with alarming frequency within
the pulpits across our land. People are equating the condition for eternal life
as a reward with the condition for eternal life as a gift. They mistakenly
think that they will receive the gift of eternal life as a result of their service. I
fear, and have good reason to believe, that in the majority of our
Endnotes                                                           Page 161

congregations, the goats far outnumber the sheep. Unlike some proveitists,
who would express similar concerns about the majority of professing
Christians being lost, I am not basing my assessment on the amount of wool
produced by the congregants but on the sounds of their bleating, the sermons
upon which they are feeding, and the books they are reading.
